<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T4 findings for Eschatological salvation received by faith -->

**T4 — Relational Interfaces**

- *T4.1.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating from God toward the human person — and if so, how?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence overwhelmingly shows eschatological salvation operating primarily as a divine initiative directed toward the human person. God is the consistent grammatical and theological agent: in Joh 3:17 "God sent his Son not to condemn but to save the world"; in Eph 2:8 salvation by grace through faith is "God's gift"; in Tit 3:5 "God saved us not by our righteous deeds but by his mercy"; in 2Ti 1:9 "God saved believers and called them to holiness by grace purposed before time." The Spirit is given as dōrea (Act 2:38, Act 10:45, Act 11:17). Righteousness is bestowed as gift (Rom 5:15-17, M38-A-VCG-03). Christ is the Savior sent by divine appointment (Act 13:23, 1Jo 4:14, Act 5:31). The divine-to-human vector is structurally foundational — salvation does not originate in the human person and moves toward them from outside, grounded in divine purpose established before time.
- *T4.1.2* — _What does the evidence reveal about the basis on which God extends this characteristic — is it conditional, unconditional, covenantal, or re…_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence reveals a complex basis combining unconditional divine initiative with covenantal grounding and responsive appropriation. The initiative is unconditional in origin: Eph 2:8 states salvation is "God's gift, not of works"; 2Ti 1:9 places it in grace "purposed before time"; Tit 3:5 grounds it in "mercy" not human righteousness. The covenantal dimension is explicit: Act 13:23 roots the Savior in David's covenant line; Rom 11:26-27 grounds all Israel's future rescue in prophetic covenant promise. The responsive dimension is present but secondary: salvation is appropriated by those who call (Act 2:21, Rom 10:13), believe (Eph 2:8, Act 16:31), confess (Rom 10:9), and hear (Eph 1:13). The faith-response is itself not an originating condition but a reception point — Eph 2:8 explicitly denies that even faith is self-generated. The overall structure is: unconditional divine initiative, covenantally grounded, received through responsive faith.
- *T4.1.3* — _What does God's extension of this characteristic reveal about his disposition toward the human person?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence reveals a divine disposition of unreserved, initiative-taking saving intent toward the human person. Joh 3:17 states the entire purpose of the Son's sending is to save, not condemn, revealing saving as God's primary disposition rather than a reluctant concession. 1Ti 2:4 shows God desiring "all people" to be saved — the disposition is universal in scope. Tit 2:11 presents grace "appearing" to bring salvation to all, revealing a disposition of generous self-disclosure. Eph 2:5 shows God acting on an inner state of spiritual death — the person contributes nothing, God acts wholly from his own merciful disposition. Rom 5:15-17 (M38-A-VCG-03) shows the gift abounding "far beyond" what the trespass produced, revealing an overflowing rather than minimal generosity. Tit 3:5 grounds salvation in "mercy," not human worth. The aggregate picture is a God whose disposition toward the human person is actively, extravagantly, and unconditionally saving — prior to and without regard to human merit.
- *T4.1.4* — _If the evidence is silent on God-to-human operation, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence is not silent on God-to-human operation; it is the dominant vector of this entire characteristic. See T4.1.1 and T4.1.2 for detailed findings. God's initiative in sending the Son (Joh 3:17), giving grace as gift (Eph 2:8), giving the Spirit (Act 10:45, Act 11:17), and working salvation through mercy (Tit 3:5) constitutes the structural foundation of M38-A across all ten VCGs.
- *T4.2.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating in the human person's movement toward God — in seeking, supplication, worship, or…_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence shows multiple forms of human movement toward God in connection with eschatological salvation. Calling on the name of the Lord (Act 2:21, Rom 10:13) is an act of invocation directed toward God. Heart-belief and mouth-confession (Rom 10:9-10) are inner and outer acts of orientation toward God and Christ. The jailer's urgent question "what must I do to be saved?" (Act 16:30) represents conscience-awakened seeking directed toward the divine. Simeon's reception of salvation (Luk 2:30) and his readiness to depart in peace represent fulfilled hope resting in God. Doxological response — the heavenly multitude ascribing salvation to God and the Lamb (Rev 7:10, Rev 19:1) — constitutes worship as the human-to-God culmination of salvation. The apostle's intercessory prayer for others' salvation (Rom 10:1) represents the saved person's movement toward God on behalf of others.
- *T4.2.2* — _What does the evidence reveal about the inner posture required for this movement?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence identifies multiple inner postures required for the human-toward-God movement in receiving salvation. Heart-belief (Rom 10:9-10) names an inner faculty of trust directed toward the risen Christ. Calling on the Lord's name (Act 2:21, Rom 10:13) requires the will to invoke, an active turning of the person toward God. Repentance (Act 2:38, Act 2:40) involves a decisive inner turning away from sin and moral separation. Godly sorrow (2Cor 7:10) identifies a specific grief-quality that produces genuine repentance rather than worldly despair. Meekness/humility of will (Jam 1:21) enables the soul to receive the implanted word. The self-renunciation texts (Mat 16:25, Mar 8:35, Luk 9:24) reveal that surrender of self-protective instincts is the posture through which the true self is eschatologically saved. An awakened conscience seeking rescue (Act 16:30) and a will striving to enter the narrow gate (Luk 13:23) also appear. The common inner posture is receptive, non-self-sufficient, will-turning orientation.
- *T4.2.3* — _What does the human-to-God direction of this characteristic reveal about the person's relationship with God?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The human-toward-God movement in eschatological salvation reveals a relationship of complete dependence, recognized need, and responsive trust. The disciples' astonished question "who then can be saved?" (Mat 19:25, Mar 10:26, Luk 18:26) reveals a relationship in which the human person recognizes their own complete inability — no self-sufficiency before God is possible. Calling on the name (Act 2:21, Rom 10:13) reveals a relationship of invocation — the person as one who calls, God as the one who saves. Confession of Christ as Lord (Rom 10:9) reveals the person's relational alignment under divine authority. The doxological response (Rev 7:10, Rev 19:1) reveals that at completion the relationship is one of joyful ascription — the person giving all credit for rescue to God and the Lamb, holding nothing for themselves. Self-renunciation texts (Mat 16:25 etc.) reveal that the relationship to God requires release of autonomous self-possession. The relationship is one of acknowledged need, receptive dependence, and grateful worship.
- *T4.2.4* — _If the evidence is silent on human-to-God operation, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence is not silent on human-to-God operation. See T4.2.1 and T4.2.2 for detailed findings. Calling, belief, confession, repentance, intercessory prayer, and doxological worship all appear as human-toward-God movements in the evidence across M38-A-VCG-02, VCG-09, and VCG-10.
- *T4.3.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic being extended by one person toward another — and if so, how does it operate in that giving…_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence shows eschatological salvation being extended person-to-person in several modes. Peter exhorts the crowd to be saved from a corrupt generation (Act 2:40), functioning as a herald extending the opportunity of rescue. Paul adapts himself to all people in order to save some (1Cor 9:22) and denies himself to seek others' rescue (1Cor 10:33). Paul endures suffering so the elect may obtain salvation (2Ti 2:10). The apostle aims to provoke jealousy in his kinspeople to save some (Rom 11:14). A teacher who watches his doctrine and life will save his hearers (1Ti 4:16). Jam 5:20 names rescuing a wandering sinner as saving a soul from death. Jude 23 urges snatching others from fire. The human giving of this characteristic is consistently mediated through proclamation, witness, relational persistence, and costly self-sacrifice — the person does not originate salvation but acts as its conduit.
- *T4.3.2* — _What inner conditions or orientations in the giver enable genuine extension of this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence reveals specific inner conditions required in those who mediate salvation to others. Missional love is foundational: Paul's self-adaptation (1Cor 9:22) and self-denial (1Cor 10:33) are driven by love for others' rescue, with the will wholly oriented toward the eschatological good of others rather than personal advantage. Intercessory desire and prayer (Rom 10:1) name the heart's sustained longing as an enabling condition. Willingness to suffer for others' rescue (2Ti 2:10) identifies self-sacrifice as an enabling posture. Faithful teaching with a watchful conscience (1Ti 4:16) names doctrinal and moral integrity as enabling conditions for saving one's hearers. The rescue of wandering sinners (Jam 5:20) and snatching from fire (Jude 23) require courageous, fear-engaged moral seriousness. The common thread is: the giver must themselves be rooted in received grace (not self-originating rescue) and must be moved by other-directed will rather than self-interest.
- *T4.3.3* — _What does the evidence reveal about what the person must have received or become before they can genuinely give this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence implies that genuine mediation of salvation to others requires prior reception of salvation oneself, though this is stated indirectly. Paul's self-adaptation (1Cor 9:22) and self-denial (1Cor 10:33) are intelligible only as responses to grace already received — the motivation is "that many may be saved" from a position of already being saved. 2Ti 2:10 shows Paul enduring suffering for the elect's salvation as one who has himself received it. The teacher who saves his hearers (1Ti 4:16) must watch his own life and doctrine — implying a prior integrity of received truth. Jude 23 urges rescue of others "with fear, hating even the garment stained by the flesh" — requiring a conscience already engaged by what sin means. Rom 10:1 reveals intercessory desire as a movement of one who has already received what they desire for others. The rescue of wandering sinners (Jam 5:20) presupposes the rescuer is not themselves wandering. The evidence consistently implies: to mediate salvation one must first have received the gift rather than originated it.
- *T4.3.4* — _If the evidence is silent on the giving direction, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence is not silent on the giving direction. See T4.3.1 through T4.3.3. Human-to-human mediation of salvation appears in M38-A-VCG-05 (Paul's missionary self-sacrifice), VCG-08 (apostolic witness and intercession), VCG-10 (Jam 5:20, Jude 23), and VCG-04 (Act 2:40). The giving direction is a sustained pattern in the evidence.
- *T4.4.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic being received by a person from another — and if so, how does it operate in that reception?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence frequently presents salvation as received through a human intermediary bearing a proclaimed message. Act 11:14 links household salvation to a message proclaimed by Cornelius's messenger. Act 13:26 presents the word of salvation as dispatched to receptive hearers. Eph 1:13 structures reception as: "hearing the gospel of salvation → believing → being sealed with the Spirit." Rom 1:16 identifies the gospel as God's power unto salvation received through faith in the message. 1Cor 1:21 states God chose the "foolishness of preaching to save those who believe," making the proclaimed word the human channel. Jam 1:21 presents the "implanted word" as the medium through which salvation is received. The inner act required for reception is faith — hearing, trusting, and accepting the message rather than generating rescue from within.
- *T4.4.2* — _What inner conditions enable or block reception of this characteristic from another person?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence identifies both enabling and blocking conditions for reception from a human intermediary. Enabling conditions include: a receptive, fear-reverent hearing stance (Act 13:26); an awakened conscience seeking rescue (Act 16:30); meekness of will before the implanted word (Jam 1:21); and belief in the message received (Eph 1:13, 1Cor 1:21). The will's openness to hearing is essential — Act 28:28 contrasts Gentiles "who will listen" with those who rejected the message. Blocking conditions include: love of truth refused (2Th 2:10, "perishing refused love of truth"), understanding closed to the deliverance offered (Act 7:25), and the devil removing the word before it can take root (Luk 8:12). Act 8:20 reveals a heart oriented toward purchasing grace as a commodity — a mercenary inner orientation that fundamentally misunderstands what is being offered. The primary blocker is the will's closing against truth; the primary enabler is the will's openness and receptive faith.
- *T4.4.3* — _What is the inner-being state of the person who encounters this characteristic from another but does not receive it?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence describes the non-receiving person in terms of several inner states. Refusal of truth's love (2Th 2:10) characterizes the perishing — the will is closed against the very content that would bring rescue. Act 7:25 describes understanding that was closed to the deliverance offered — the people "did not understand" that God was working salvation, a failure of perception combined with unreceptive inner state. Luk 8:12 shows the word removed before faith can form — the inner state remains unchanged, without the transformation faith would bring. Those who reject the message in Act 28:28 are contrasted with Gentiles who will listen. The state of the non-receiver in Rev 12:10 is one of perpetual accusation without vindication. 2Pe 2:20 describes those who once knew the Savior but turned back — characterized as worse off than their original ignorance, with defilement returning. The non-receiver exists in a state of perishing (1Cor 1:18), wrath-facing (1Th 5:9), condemnation (Mar 16:16), and ongoing spiritual danger — the structural opposite of the rescued state.
- *T4.4.4* — _If the evidence is silent on the receiving direction, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence is not silent on the receiving direction. See T4.4.1 through T4.4.3. Reception of salvation through a human-borne proclaimed word is a core mechanism in M38-A-VCG-02 (Eph 1:13, 1Cor 1:21, Rom 1:16) and M38-A-VCG-04 (Act 2:47, Jam 1:21). The receiving direction is extensively documented.
- *T4.5.1* — _Does the evidence indicate whether this characteristic operates differently within existing relational bonds versus across relational distan…_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence shows salvation operating across relational distance as a characteristic feature. Joh 4:42 identifies Jesus as "Savior of the world," confirmed by Samaritans — crossing ethnic and religious boundaries. Act 13:47 mandates salvation to the ends of the earth. Rom 11:11 and 11:26 show salvation moving from Israel's stumbling to Gentile inclusion and back. Act 10:45 and Act 11:17 present the Spirit-gift given equally to Gentiles as astonishing boundary-crossing. 1Ti 2:4 states God desires "all people" to be saved. Within existing bonds, 1Cor 7:16 shows a believing spouse holding uncertain hope for an unbelieving partner, and 1Ti 2:15 links salvation to faithful domestic conduct. The evidence suggests no operation restricted to pre-existing relational bonds — salvation characteristically crosses ethnic, social, and religious distance — but relational proximity also functions as a channel (household salvation, Act 11:14, Act 16:31).
- *T4.5.2* — _Does this characteristic operate within covenantal contexts only, or does it cross covenantal boundaries?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence consistently shows eschatological salvation crossing covenantal boundaries rather than remaining confined to them. The Samaritans confess Jesus as "Savior of the world" (Joh 4:42); salvation is declared available to "all who call" without ethnic restriction (Act 2:21, Rom 10:13); Act 13:47 mandates carrying salvation to "the ends of the earth"; Act 10:45 and 11:17 record the Spirit-gift crossing to Gentiles; Rom 11:11-14 describes salvation reaching Gentiles through Israel's stumbling; 1Ti 2:4 states God desires "all people" to be saved. Joh 4:22 preserves the covenantal origin ("salvation is from the Jews") while Act 13:23 roots Jesus in Davidic covenant. The pattern is: covenantal origin that generates universal scope — salvation originates within covenant history but is not bounded by covenantal membership. The ethnic and merit-based restrictions are explicitly refused (Act 15:11, Act 15:1 contrast).
- *T4.5.3* — _What does the evidence reveal about the relational scope of this characteristic — who is included and who is not?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence presents eschatological salvation with an explicitly universal intended scope bounded only by receptive response. Included: all who call (Rom 10:13, "everyone who calls"), all who believe (Act 16:31, "you and your household"), Samaritans (Joh 4:42), Gentiles to the ends of the earth (Act 13:47), all Israel eschatologically (Rom 11:26), all people as God's desire (1Ti 2:4), a multitude from every nation (Rev 7:10). The gift is characterized as given equally — not differentiated by ethnicity, gender, or social standing. Excluded by their own response: those who refuse truth (2Th 2:10), those who do not call or believe (Mar 16:16), those who neglect salvation (Heb 2:3), those who do not hold fast (1Cor 15:2). The remnant-election texts (Rom 9:27) preserve divine sovereign selectivity within this universal scope. The structure is: universal divine intent meeting particular response, with no human category intrinsically excluded from the offer.
- *T4.5.4* — _If the evidence is silent on relational boundaries, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence is not silent on relational boundaries. See T4.5.1 through T4.5.3. The evidence is notably explicit about boundary-crossing (Gentile inclusion, universal scope) and boundary-origin (covenantal rootedness in Jewish-Davidic history), making relational scope one of the most extensively discussed dimensions of this characteristic.
- *T4.6.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating in relation to other spiritual beings — angelic or adversarial — and if so, how?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence shows both angelic and adversarial engagement with eschatological salvation. On the angelic side: Luk 2:11 presents an angel announcing the Savior's birth; Rev 7:10 and Rev 12:10 depict heavenly beings witnessing and proclaiming salvation's completion. Rev 12:10 specifically names the accuser's defeat as the moment salvation is declared — the heavenly accuser is cast down, conscience of the accused is vindicated. On the adversarial side: Luk 8:12 shows the devil actively removing the word from hearts to prevent belief and thereby block salvation — faith is the battleground. Rev 12:10 identifies Satan as the one who "accuses them day and night," framing eschatological salvation as the resolution of adversarial accusation. The adversary works against the reception of salvation; angelic beings witness and declare it.
- *T4.6.2* — _Is this characteristic a site of adversarial activity — something that can be attacked, distorted, or weaponised by adversarial spiritual po…_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence explicitly identifies eschatological salvation as a site of adversarial attack and distortion. Luk 8:12 names the devil as the agent who "removes the word from hearts to prevent belief and salvation" — the inner act of faith is the battleground and the devil targets it directly. Rev 12:10 presents Satan as the perpetual accuser whose activity works against the inner-being assurance that salvation secures — his defeat is the occasion for salvation's declaration. Act 8:20 shows the gift of God being targeted for commodification — a distortion of salvation's grace-character into a purchasable commodity, which Peter identifies as a spiritually dangerous inner orientation. False teaching that adds conditions to salvation (Act 15:1, Act 15:11) also functions as a distorting force — placing works-requirements where grace-reception belongs. The adversary's primary strategy appears to be: remove or corrupt the word before faith can be exercised, or distort salvation's character from gift to transaction.
- *T4.6.3* — _Is this characteristic communicated, strengthened, or mediated through angelic ministry in the evidence?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence shows angelic involvement at two points, though not as primary mediators of salvation itself. Luk 2:11 records an angel announcing the Savior's birth — angelic ministry at the point of disclosure. Rev 7:10 and Rev 19:1 depict heavenly beings (angelic and redeemed multitudes) participating in the doxological proclamation of salvation's completion — their role is witness and worship rather than mediation. Rev 12:10 presents the heavenly voice declaring salvation at the moment the accuser is cast down — an angelic or heavenly figure announcing salvation's vindication. The birth announcement (Luk 2:11) is the clearest case of angelic communication of salvation-news. However, the primary mediatorial role is assigned to Christ (Heb 7:25, his ongoing intercession), to the proclaimed word (1Cor 1:21), and to the Spirit (Eph 1:13, Tit 3:5). Angelic ministry in the evidence is more declaratory and doxological than structurally constitutive of how salvation reaches the human person.
- *T4.6.4* — _If the evidence is silent on the spiritual beings interface, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence is not silent on the spiritual beings interface. See T4.6.1 through T4.6.3. Adversarial activity against salvation-reception (Luk 8:12, Rev 12:10), angelic declaration of salvation (Luk 2:11, Rev 7:10), and the accuser's defeat as the occasion of salvation's announcement (Rev 12:10) all appear in the evidence.
<!-- /EVIDENCE -->

<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T5 findings for Eschatological salvation received by faith -->

**T5 — Formative and Developmental Dimension**

- *T5.1.1* — _Does this characteristic produce transformation in the person — and if so, does it change the person's condition, the person's orientation t…_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence shows transformation operating at the level of condition, orientation, and both simultaneously. Condition is changed: in Eph 2:5 the person moves from spiritual death to being "made alive with Christ"; in Rom 5:15-17 the inner state moves from condemnation to justification; in Tit 3:5 the Spirit's regenerating work changes the person's inner constitution; in Act 2:47 persons are added to the community of the saved from a prior state of being outside it. Orientation is also changed: Rom 5:9-10 shows the person's orientation shifting from enemy-of-God to reconciled-through-Christ; the self-renunciation texts (Mat 16:25, Mar 8:35) show the will reoriented from self-preservation to Christ-following; 2Cor 7:10 shows godly grief reorienting the person away from sin toward life. The doxological completion texts (Rev 7:10, Rev 19:1) show both a condition of being fully delivered and an orientation of praise. Both condition and orientation are transformed, with condition-change typically preceding and enabling orientation-change in the evidence's sequence.
- *T5.1.2* — _Is the transformation produced by this characteristic reversible or irreversible in the verse evidence?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence presents a genuinely mixed picture, with both irreversibility and reversibility indicated in different texts, creating partial disjointedness. Irreversibility is implied by: Heb 7:25 ("saves completely," with unbroken intercession), Eph 2:5 (made alive, a past completed state), 2Ti 1:9 (grace "purposed before time"), 1Th 5:9 (appointed for salvation, not wrath), and the assurance language of Rom 5:9-10. Reversibility or serious risk is indicated by: 2Pe 2:20 (those who escaped defilement through knowing the Savior but fall back are "worse off"), 1Cor 15:2 (believing "in vain" is a genuine risk if the gospel is not held fast), Heb 2:3 (neglecting salvation risks inescapable judgment), Heb 5:9 (salvation conditioned on obedience), and Mat 10:22 (salvation tied to enduring to the end). The evidence does not resolve this tension — it holds both irreversible-divine-purpose and genuine-human-risk as live data points. The transformation's permanence appears to depend on which axis of the evidence is foregrounded: the divine-gift axis (irreversible) or the human-receptivity axis (conditionally maintained).
- *T5.1.3* — _If the evidence is silent on transformation, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence is not silent on transformation. T5.1.1 and T5.1.2 document extensive evidence of both condition-change and orientation-change across M38-A-VCG-01 through VCG-10. This prompt is answered by the presence of transformation evidence; no silence to record.
- *T5.2.1* — _Does the verse evidence describe a sequence of inner states through which this characteristic moves the person — a before, during, and after…_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence describes sequences explicitly in several texts. Eph 1:13 gives a clear sequence: hearing the gospel → faith exercised → sealed with the Spirit — three distinguishable inner states. 2Cor 7:10 sequences: encounter with godly grief → repentance → salvation without regret, contrasting with worldly grief → death. Rom 5:10 moves: enemy status → reconciliation through Christ's death → present and future salvation through risen life — three stages. The jailer sequence (Act 16:30-31): conscience-awakened danger → urgent seeking → faith directed at Christ → promised rescue. Eph 2:5 implies: spiritual death → divine action on that dead state → made alive with Christ. The self-renunciation texts (Mat 16:25) structure: self-preservation will → surrender → eschatological finding/keeping. The before-state is consistently: sin-laden, condemned, dead, lost, or enemy; the during-state involves the will turning, faith exercised, or word received; the after-state is saved, justified, reconciled, sealed, and secured.
- *T5.2.2* — _What are those states, and what does the sequence reveal about how this characteristic works?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — Drawing on the sequences identified in T5.2.1, the recurring inner states across the evidence are: (1) **Before**: spiritual death (Eph 2:5), enemy-status (Rom 5:10), lost/perishing (Luk 19:10, 1Cor 1:18), burdened by sin (Luk 7:50, 1Ti 1:15), conscience awakened to danger (Act 16:30), or the will closed against truth (2Th 2:10). (2) **At reception**: faith exercised (Eph 2:8), will turning (Act 2:38, Rom 10:9), word received (Eph 1:13), calling invoked (Act 2:21, Rom 10:13), self surrendered (Mat 16:25). (3) **After reception**: justified (Rom 5:16), reconciled (Rom 5:10), sealed with Spirit (Eph 1:13), peace received (Luk 7:50), made alive (Eph 2:5), incorporated into community (Act 2:47). (4) **Ongoing**: being saved (1Cor 1:18), working out salvation (Phili 2:12), enduring to the end (Mat 10:22), growing in grace (2Pe 3:18). (5) **Completion**: rescued fully into heavenly kingdom (2Ti 4:18), reigning in life (Rom 5:17), doxological worship (Rev 7:10, Rev 19:1). The sequence reveals that salvation is structured as movement from death/lostness through a pivot of receptive faith into present-and-progressing rescue with eschatological completion.
- *T5.2.3* — _If the evidence is silent on sequence, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence is not silent on sequence. T5.2.1 and T5.2.2 identify five distinguishable stages (before, reception, after-reception, ongoing, completion) drawn from multiple VCGs. No silence to record.
- *T5.3.1* — _What mechanism does this characteristic use to produce change in the person — discipline, encounter, gradual formation, sudden transformatio…_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence reveals multiple mechanisms. The primary mechanism is encounter with the proclaimed word meeting the act of faith: 1Cor 1:21 (God's "foolishness of preaching" saves those who believe), Eph 1:13 (gospel heard and believed), Jam 1:21 (implanted word saves the soul). This encounter-mechanism can be sudden (Act 16:30-31, the jailer's immediate crisis and response) or presupposed as a completed event from which ongoing transformation flows. A second mechanism is divine regenerative action: Tit 3:5 names the Spirit's "regenerating and renewing work" as the mechanism, acting on the inner person directly rather than through cognitive encounter alone. A third mechanism is the sustained will under pressure: M38-A-VCG-05 presents endurance as the mechanism through which salvation is reached (Mat 10:22, Mat 24:13, Heb 5:9). A fourth is self-renunciation: the will releasing self-interest (Mat 16:25 register) as a mechanism of eschatological finding. The mechanisms differ by context — crisis produces sudden encounter, formation produces gradual endurance, Spirit-action produces regeneration — but all converge on the same transformation.
- *T5.3.2* — _Does the evidence distinguish between mechanisms in different contexts?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence does distinguish mechanisms by context, though not always explicitly. In crisis contexts: Act 16:30-31 shows sudden encounter, awakened conscience, immediate faith-response, and rapid rescue — a crisis-encounter mechanism. In preaching contexts: 1Cor 1:21 and Eph 1:13 show the proclaimed word as the mechanism, requiring hearing, then believing, then sealing — a sequential word-faith-Spirit mechanism. In perseverance contexts: Mat 10:22, Mat 24:13, and Heb 5:9 show sustained endurance of the will as the mechanism — a gradual, sustained-will mechanism operating over extended time under pressure. In inner-moral contexts: 2Cor 7:10 shows godly sorrow producing repentance as the mechanism — an affective-moral movement. In divine-regenerative contexts: Tit 3:5 shows the Spirit acting directly on the person through regeneration and renewal — a divine-transformation mechanism not mediated through human crisis or preaching. In self-renunciation contexts: Mat 16:25 register shows the will's release of self-interest as the mechanism. The mechanisms are context-differentiated, not uniform, suggesting that salvation's transformation operates through varied divine and human channels suited to the person's specific condition.
- *T5.3.3* — _If the evidence is silent on mechanism, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence is not silent on mechanism. T5.3.1 and T5.3.2 identify at least five distinct mechanisms (crisis-encounter, word-faith-Spirit sequence, sustained endurance, affective-moral repentance, and divine regeneration) differentiated by context. No silence to record.
- *T5.4.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating in relation to suffering or affliction — as a response to it, a product of it, or…_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence shows suffering functioning in multiple relations to eschatological salvation. Suffering as a context requiring endurance: Mat 10:22, Mat 24:13, and Mar 13:13 present persecution and hatred as the context within which the persevering will holds until salvation is reached. Suffering as the path of the mediator: 2Ti 2:10 shows Paul accepting suffering as the context in which the elect obtain salvation; Heb 2:10 describes Christ as "made perfect through suffering" as the founder of salvation — suffering is structurally part of the path to eschatological completion. Self-renunciation as voluntary suffering: Mat 16:25 register frames willingness to lose life as the condition of eschatological finding — suffering-of-self-surrender as the mechanism. Salvation as deliverance from affliction: Heb 2:3, Heb 5:9, and Rev 12:10 position salvation as the resolution of danger, affliction, and accusation. The evidence treats suffering not as incidental but as structurally present — both in the mediator's path (Christ and apostles) and in the believer's perseverance context.
- *T5.4.2* — _Does suffering deepen, test, reveal, or produce this characteristic in the person?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence shows suffering functioning primarily as a testing and deepening context rather than as the generative cause of salvation. Suffering tests perseverance: Mat 10:22, Mat 24:13, and Mar 13:13 present external hatred and persecution as the pressure under which the will's genuine commitment to salvation is tested — those who endure show their salvation is real; those who defect show it was not. Suffering reveals salvific priority: 1Cor 5:5 (delivering the man to Satan for fleshly destruction so his spirit may be saved) reveals that suffering of the body can be the occasion through which eschatological rescue of the spirit is distinguished from temporal comfort. Paul's suffering in 2Ti 2:10 deepens his identification with others' salvation — the apostle's affliction becomes the medium through which others receive the eschatological gift. Heb 2:10 shows Christ's suffering as the constitutive path through which he became the "founder of salvation" — suffering not incidental but perfecting. The evidence is partially disjointed here: suffering is more consistently a testing/deepening context than a producing cause, with the exception of Christ's suffering which is structurally generative.
- *T5.4.3* — _If the evidence is silent on the relationship to suffering, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence is not silent on the relationship to suffering. T5.4.1 and T5.4.2 document suffering as context for endurance (M38-A-VCG-05), as path of mediators (2Ti 2:10, Heb 2:10), as constitutive of Christ's founding of salvation, and as the occasion for testing whether perseverance is genuine. No silence to record.
- *T5.5.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic participating in the longer arc of character formation and sanctification — shaping the per…_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence shows partial but genuine engagement with ongoing formation. 1Cor 1:18 and 1Cor 15:2 use present-tense sōzō ("being saved"), indicating salvation as an ongoing state rather than solely a past event. Phili 2:12 urges "working out your own salvation with reverent seriousness," indicating that salvation engages the will, conscience, and sustained moral effort over time. 1Ti 4:16 links watching one's life and doctrine to saving both self and hearers — an ongoing formational discipline. 1Ti 2:15 conditions ongoing salvation on persisting in faith, love, holiness, and self-control — naming virtues that must be sustained over time. 2Ti 1:9 grounds the calling in a "holy calling" — linking salvation's reception to ongoing sanctified identity. 2Pe 3:18 calls those who know the Savior to grow in grace and knowledge — salvation-identity as a starting point for ongoing formation. The evidence does not present sanctification as the mechanism of salvation but does show salvation generating an ongoing formational trajectory in the person.
- *T5.5.2* — _What does the evidence reveal about the role of this characteristic in that longer arc?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence reveals that eschatological salvation functions as both the origin-event and the ongoing context of sanctification, though the relationship is more implicit than systematically stated. Salvation received generates a calling to holiness: 2Ti 1:9 states God "saved us and called us to a holy calling" — salvation and sanctification are joined in the same divine act, with sanctification as the trajectory opened by rescue. 2Th 2:13 describes salvation received through "the Spirit's sanctifying work and personal belief in truth" — the Spirit's sanctifying activity is the inner medium through which salvation is appropriated, making sanctification constitutive of the reception. 1Ti 2:15 conditions ongoing salvation on persisting in holiness as a virtue-state. 2Pe 3:18 shows the saved person called to grow in grace and knowledge. The evidence positions salvation not as sanctification's endpoint but as its precondition and initiating frame — rescue from condemnation opens the space within which ongoing formation proceeds. The characteristic does not replace sanctification but generates the saved-and-calling context within which character formation becomes possible and mandatory.
- *T5.5.3* — _If the evidence is silent on formation and sanctification, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence is not silent on formation and sanctification, though the engagement is partial rather than systematic. T5.5.1 and T5.5.2 document relevant evidence in Phili 2:12, 1Ti 4:16, 2Ti 1:9, 2Th 2:13, 2Pe 3:18, and 1Ti 2:15. The evidence shows salvation as the precondition and initiating frame of ongoing formation, though sanctification is not the primary focus of this characteristic's vocabulary.
- *T5.6.1* — _Does the verse evidence point this characteristic toward an eschatological fullness — a future state toward which its present operation is o…_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence extensively points present salvation toward eschatological fullness. Rom 5:9 grounds present justification in future assurance of being "saved from wrath." Rom 13:11 names salvation as "nearer than when we first believed," framing it as an approaching horizon. Mat 10:22 and Mat 24:13 tie final salvation explicitly to "the end" — a future telos reached through present endurance. Heb 7:25 describes Christ saving "completely" those who come to him through unbroken intercession — a salvation that extends into the future without limit. Rev 7:10 and Rev 19:1 present the doxological endpoint of the eschatological arrival. Eph 6:17 describes the "helmet of salvation" worn in present spiritual warfare oriented toward final protection. 2Ti 4:18 anticipates rescue into "the heavenly kingdom." The present experience of salvation is consistently oriented toward a future fullness: complete rescue from wrath, entry into the kingdom, and the reigning in life (Rom 5:17) — with present salvation as the real but partial first installment.
- *T5.6.2* — _What does the present experience of this characteristic anticipate about its future fullness?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence specifies what the present experience of salvation anticipates. Present justification anticipates future deliverance from wrath: Rom 5:9 explicitly draws this inference — "having been justified, we shall be saved from wrath." Present sealing with the Spirit (Eph 1:13) anticipates the fullness of Spirit-possession as eschatological guarantee. Present reigning through received grace and righteousness (Rom 5:17) anticipates the full exercise of that reign in eschatological life. The "helmet of salvation" (Eph 6:17) as present armor anticipates final protection. Present being-saved (1Cor 1:18) anticipates full deliverance. The approaching character of salvation (Rom 13:11) anticipates a state where temporal proximity becomes arrival. Rev 7:10 and Rev 19:1 show the completion state as corporate, joyful, doxological, and unbounded — what present salvation's individual, partial assurance anticipates. 2Ti 4:18 names specific content: "the heavenly kingdom." The present experience is thus a real but incomplete anticipation — it possesses the thing (justification, reconciliation, sealing) while awaiting its fullness (complete rescue from wrath, entry into glory, and eternal reigning).
- *T5.6.3* — _If the evidence is silent on eschatological trajectory, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence is not silent on eschatological trajectory; it is among the most extensively documented dimensions of this characteristic. T5.6.1 and T5.6.2 draw on texts across M38-A-VCG-05, VCG-08, VCG-10, and VCG-04. No silence to record.
- *T5.7.2* — _Does the evidence indicate generational consequence — a deposit carried forward beyond the individual?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence shows limited but present generational extension. Household salvation (Act 11:14, Act 16:31) indicates that an individual's reception of salvation creates conditions for others in the same relational unit to receive rescue — the salvific reality reaches beyond the individual to those connected by household bond. Paul's endurance in suffering produces salvation for the elect (2Ti 2:10) — one person's sustained inner posture creates eschatological consequence for others not yet saved. The apostle's missionary self-adaptation (1Cor 9:22) and self-denial (1Cor 10:33) generate salvation in others across time as a consequence of one person's inner orientation. However, the evidence does not describe a clearly biological or generational deposit — the extension is relational and missional rather than genealogical. The evidence for strictly generational consequence (parent to child, covenant line to descendant) is limited to Act 2:39 ("the promise is to you and your children") which is not in the evidence block, making this finding partial and noting its boundaries honestly.
<!-- /EVIDENCE -->

<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T1 findings for Eschatological salvation received by faith -->

**T1 — Definition**

- *T1.1.1* — _What is this characteristic called in the programme — and what does the name itself signal about its essential nature?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The characteristic is named "Eschatological salvation received by faith." Each element of the name carries definitional weight. "Eschatological" signals that the salvation in view is not merely temporal, physical, or therapeutic but oriented toward ultimate destiny — rescue from final judgment and condemnation, participation in the coming kingdom. "Salvation" (from the Greek sōzō/sōtēr/sōtēria register) signals rescue, deliverance, and preservation from a real and structural danger — the name implies the existence of a threat from which rescue is needed. "Received" is the critical inner-being marker: salvation is not achieved, generated, or earned by the human person but accepted as a gift — the inner posture is receptive, not productive. "By faith" names the specific inner faculty through which reception occurs: trust directed toward Christ as the ground and source of rescue. Together the name signals that this characteristic is constitutionally relational (it exists between the divine giver and the human receiver), that it is directional (from God toward the person, not self-generated), and that its inner-being locus is the will and trust-faculty of the person as they turn toward and receive what God offers.
- *T1.1.2* — _What do the primary Hebrew and Greek terms reveal at the definitional level — before deeper lexical analysis begins?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The characteristic is anchored in four primary Greek terms, each revealing a distinct definitional facet. (1) sōzō (G4982): the verbal form — "to save, rescue, deliver, preserve" — signals an active divine intervention removing the person from a state of danger or destruction. Its verbal character emphasizes event and process: salvation is something being done to and for the person, not a static possession. (2) sōtēr (G4990): the noun naming the agent — "Savior" — definitionally concentrates all salvific power and identity in a single person (Christ), making salvation permanently personal and relational rather than abstract. (3) sōtēria (G4991): the abstract noun — "salvation, rescue, deliverance" — names the state or outcome achieved, suggesting that salvation is a knowable condition that can be possessed, proclaimed, received, and praised. (4) sōtērion (G4992): the adjectival/neuter noun — "saving, that which saves" — appears in contexts of public proclamation (Luk 2:30, Act 28:28, Eph 6:17) and suggests salvation as a visible, communicable reality available for encounter. Together these terms reveal that salvation is: definitionally active (sōzō), agentively personal (sōtēr), outcome-real (sōtēria), and publicly available (sōtērion). The dōrea/dōrēma register (G1431/G1434) adds the dimension of unearned gift, definitionally excluding all self-origination.
- *T1.1.3* — _Does the name carry directional, relational, or constitutional implications that orient the enquiry from the outset?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — Yes on all three counts. (1) Directional: the name "received by faith" encodes a direction — salvation moves from God toward the human person, not from the human person toward God or outward from the self. The enquiry is oriented toward a downward-and-inward movement: divine grace descending, human faith receiving. This prevents any misreading of the characteristic as human achievement or self-construction. (2) Relational: "salvation received by faith" implies a relationship with a Savior — there is no impersonal mechanism here. The sōtēr-naming (M38-A-VCG-01) makes the relational structure explicit: Christ is Savior, the person is the one being saved. The name therefore orients the enquiry toward a two-party relation at its center. (3) Constitutional: "eschatological" carries constitutional implications about the depth-level of the characteristic — it is not surface behavior or social adjustment but the inner being's ultimate destiny and orientation. The name signals that what is at stake is the whole person's final condition: life or death, reigning or perishing, justification or condemnation. The enquiry is thus oriented from the outset toward the deepest constitutional layer of the person — not a behavioral trait but an inner-being state of ultimate significance.
- *T1.2.1* — _What kind of inner-being phenomenon does this characteristic appear to be — an act, a disposition, a condition, a quality, or something else…_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — At first encounter the corpus suggests this characteristic is composite: it appears simultaneously as an act, a condition, and a disposition, with different verse-groups emphasizing different aspects. The faith-reception verses (M38-A-VCG-02) present it as an inner act: heart-believing (Rom 10:9), calling on the name (Act 2:21, Rom 10:13), confessing with the mouth (Rom 10:10) — these are punctiliar or decisive acts of the will turning toward Christ. The present-being-saved verses (M38-A-VCG-04) present it as an ongoing condition: believers "are being saved" (1Cor 1:18, 2Cor 2:15), the Lord "adds the saved daily" (Act 2:47) — a state being progressively inhabited. The endurance verses (M38-A-VCG-05) present it as a disposition of sustained will: "enduring to the end" (Mat 10:22, Mat 24:13) — not a single act but a persevering orientation. The gift-language verses (M38-A-VCG-03) present the saved state as a condition received and worn — righteousness bestowed, grace abounding, the inner being made alive. The most accurate preliminary classification is: this characteristic is a received condition, entered through an act of faith and sustained by a disposition of persevering trust, producing a progressive transformation of the inner being toward eschatological fullness.
- *T1.2.2* — _Is this characteristic simple in structure or does it appear to have constituent elements at first encounter?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — At first encounter the characteristic appears structurally complex, comprising multiple constituent elements that operate in sequence and in tension. The constituent elements visible at the surface level are: (a) the divine initiative — God's gift of grace and saving purpose (Eph 2:8, 2Ti 1:9, Tit 3:5), which is logically and causally prior; (b) the human receptive act — faith, calling, confessing, repenting (M38-A-VCG-02, M38-A-VCG-09), which is the experiential entry point; (c) the resulting inner state — justification, peace, alive from death, conscience cleansed (Rom 5:9-10, Eph 2:5, Tit 3:5), which is the immediate condition following reception; (d) the ongoing process — being saved (1Cor 1:18, 1Cor 15:2, Act 2:47), persevering, working out salvation (Phili 2:12), which is the sustained temporal form; (e) the eschatological telos — full salvation from wrath, heavenly doxology, the soul's final rescue (Rom 5:9, Rev 7:10, 1Pe 1:9), which is the future completion. These elements are not independent but nested: divine initiative enables faith-reception, which produces inner transformation, which sustains perseverance, which reaches eschatological completion. The characteristic is therefore not simple but structurally layered, with each layer presupposing the one beneath it.
- *T1.2.3* — _What is the current best working description of this characteristic — encapsulating its constitutional location, the faculties it primarily …_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — Based on the corpus evidence at T0-T1 level, the working description is: Eschatological salvation received by faith is a received inner-being condition, grounded in divine grace and initiated through the will's trust-directed turning toward Christ, through which the human person is transferred from the state of condemnation, death, and perishing into the state of justification, life, and eschatological security. Constitutionally it is located in the will/volitional faculty as its primary locus, engaging also the conscience (which is awakened to danger and then cleansed), the mind (which receives truth and is made wise for salvation), and the affections (which respond in peace, gratitude, and praise). Its impact on the person is comprehensive and progressive: immediate impact includes faith-reception, peace, and conscience-cleansing; sustained impact includes persevering will, growing assurance, moral formation, and missional orientation toward others; ultimate impact is the soul's telos — full eschatological possession of the rescued self in doxological relation with God. The characteristic is not self-generated but wholly received, not once-only but progressively sustained, not individually isolated but communally realized, and not fully present but oriented toward a future fullness that constitutes its completion.
- *T1.3.1* — _What is the structural opposite of this characteristic — the inner-being reality that stands against or excludes it?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The corpus identifies multiple structural opposites, all clustering around the same inner-being pole. The most explicit and recurrent is "perishing" (apollumi/apōleia): 1Cor 1:18 sets "those being saved" directly against "those perishing"; 2Cor 2:15 deploys the same contrast; 2Th 2:10 names perishing as the outcome of refusing truth; Luk 8:12 shows the devil removing the word precisely to prevent belief and avoid salvation. The second structural opposite is "condemnation" (katakrima): Joh 3:17 names it as the displaced alternative to salvation; Rom 5:16 sets the gift of justification against condemnation following trespass. The third is "wrath" (orgē): Rom 5:9 contrasts present justification with future salvation from wrath; 1Th 5:9 sets salvation against wrath as the two possible divine appointments. The fourth is "death" (thanatos): Rom 5:15-17 sets grace and life against the death that spread through Adam; Jam 5:20 sets soul-rescue against death. The fifth is "spiritual blindness/closed will": Luk 8:12 (heart where word cannot take root), 2Th 2:10 (refusing love of truth), Act 16:30 inverting to the positive (conscience awakened to danger). The primary structural opposite is therefore the state of perishing — an inner-being condition characterized by closed will, refused truth, condemnation, and movement toward death and final wrath rather than rescue and life.
- *T1.3.2* — _What does this characteristic explicitly exclude or resist?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The corpus is explicit on multiple exclusions. (1) Self-generated works as ground of salvation: Eph 2:8 states faith and salvation are "entirely God's gift, not human achievement"; Tit 3:5 excludes "righteous deeds"; 2Ti 1:9 excludes "works"; Act 15:11 refuses "law-observance" as the condition; Act 15:1 identifies the false circumcision-requirement as the contested opposite. The characteristic resists any works-based or merit-based self-construction. (2) Self-preservation as the fundamental life-orientation: the self-surrender VCG (M38-A-VCG-06) makes explicit that the instinct to "save one's life" by clinging to it is structurally excluded — it produces loss rather than salvation. (3) Purchasing or commodifying the gift: Act 8:20 explicitly excludes the attempt to buy the gift of God — salvation resists mercenary or transactional inner posture. (4) Passive indifference: Heb 2:3 names neglect of salvation as itself a fatal orientation — the characteristic resists the inner passivity that assumes rescue without receptive engagement. (5) Apostasy and abandonment: 2Pe 2:20 shows that returning to defilement after knowing the Savior is explicitly dangerous — the characteristic resists interior defection. (6) Human judging that usurps divine authority: Jam 4:12 identifies only God as Savior and Destroyer, excluding human pretension to that role.
- *T1.3.3* — _What is this characteristic not — where does it end and something else begin?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The corpus allows several boundary-defining observations. (1) This characteristic is not physical healing or temporal deliverance per se: while sōzō occasionally carries physical resonances (Mat 27:42 — "he saved others," Joh 10:9 — "find pasture"), the characteristic as defined is specifically eschatological and spiritual in scope — rescue from sin, condemnation, and final judgment. Physical preservation (Mat 24:22, Mar 13:20) appears in the corpus but is distinguished from the primary eschatological meaning by context. (2) This characteristic is not sanctification or moral formation as an independent category: Tit 2:11-12 and 2Th 2:13 link salvation to sanctification, but sanctification is a consequence and accompanying process of salvation, not identical with it. (3) This characteristic is not justification as a separate legal act, though justification is included within it: Rom 5:9 distinguishes present justification from future salvation-from-wrath, showing justification as a component of the larger salvation-complex, not equivalent to it. (4) This characteristic ends where self-generation begins: wherever the inner being treats rescue as self-produced, merited, or purchasable (Act 8:20, Act 15:1, Eph 2:8), the characteristic is excluded. (5) This characteristic ends where defection replaces perseverance: 2Pe 2:20 and Heb 6:4 show that abandonment of known salvation constitutes a transition out of the characteristic's territory.
- *T1.4.1* — _In what distinct ways does this characteristic operate within the inner person?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The corpus evidences at least five distinct modes of operation within the inner person. (1) Reception-mode: faith as the inner act of receiving what God gives — heart-believing, calling, confessing (Rom 10:9-10, Eph 2:8, Act 16:31, M38-A-VCG-02). This is the decisive punctiliar moment of orientation toward Christ. (2) Awakening-mode: the conscience and will are aroused to their own danger and need, producing urgent seeking of rescue — the jailer's question in Act 16:30, the disciples' astonishment in Mat 19:25, the crowd's cry in Luk 18:26 (M38-A-VCG-07). (3) Transformation-mode: the inner being moves from death to life, from condemnation to justification, from guilt to righteousness — the Spirit renews, the conscience is cleansed, the self is remade (Tit 3:5, Rom 5:16-17, Eph 2:5, M38-A-VCG-03). (4) Perseverance-mode: the will sustains itself under pressure, holding fast to the gospel, enduring to the end — not a once-off act but a sustained inner orientation (Mat 10:22, Mat 24:13, 1Cor 15:2, M38-A-VCG-05). (5) Doxological-mode: the saved inner being overflows in praise, gratitude, and ascription of glory to God — 2Cor 9:15 (doxology over the gift), Rev 7:10, Rev 19:1 (heavenly ascription), Luk 2:30 (Simeon's peace-filled release, M38-A-VCG-10). These modes are not strictly sequential but may overlap or recur across the life of the saved person.
- *T1.4.2* — _Does this characteristic operate differently depending on context, direction, or constitutional level?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — Yes. Three contextual differentials are evident. (1) Direction — toward self versus toward others: in the self-directed mode the inner being receives salvation (M38-A-VCG-02), is transformed (M38-A-VCG-03), and perseveres (M38-A-VCG-05). In the other-directed mode the inner being is oriented toward others' rescue — apostolic self-adaptation (1Cor 9:22), self-denial for others (1Cor 10:33), intercessory prayer (Rom 10:1), person-to-person witness (Jam 5:20, Rom 11:14). The characteristic operates outwardly as a missional disposition when the self is already a recipient. (2) Temporal context — initial versus sustained: in the initial moment faith-reception is the primary operation (calling on the name, heart-believing, confessing). In sustained time the persevering will becomes dominant — holding fast (1Cor 15:2), enduring to the end (Mat 10:22), working out salvation (Phili 2:12). (3) Constitutional level — individual versus communal: at the individual level salvation is personally appropriated through faith (Act 16:31, Luk 7:50). At the communal level the Lord "adds to the community those being saved" (Act 2:47), household salvation is a unit (Act 11:14, Act 16:31), and "all Israel" is a collective eschatological object (Rom 11:26). The characteristic thus scales from individual inner act to communal and national scope without losing its inner-being character.
- *T1.4.3* — _Does this characteristic have a communicative or speech-based mode of operation — and if so, how does it function?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — Yes. The corpus evidences a speech-based mode of operation at several points. (1) Confession (homologeō): Rom 10:9-10 (M38-A-VCG-02) is the primary text — "confessing with the mouth that Jesus is Lord" is paired with heart-belief as the dual inner-outer mechanism of salvation. The spoken confession is not merely a social performance but an inner-being act that completes and expresses the volitional turning of faith. Salvation is thus partly constituted by its verbal declaration. (2) Calling on the name: Act 2:21 and Rom 10:13 name "calling on the Lord's name" as the decisive speech-act through which salvation is received — the invocation is both a speech-act and a volitional act, inseparable from faith. (3) Proclamation received: Act 11:14, Act 13:26, and Eph 1:13 (M38-A-VCG-02) all identify hearing the proclaimed word as the channel through which salvation enters the inner being — the characteristic depends on a communicative event (proclamation-hearing) as its ordinary delivery mechanism. (4) Testimony and witness: Joh 4:42 shows salvation received through direct hearing of testimony; 1Jo 4:14 grounds assurance in apostolic testimony. (5) Doxological speech as culmination: Rev 7:10, Rev 12:10, Rev 19:1 show that salvation's completion is itself voiced — the speech-act of praise is the characteristic's doxological mode at full expression.
- *T1.5.1* — _What is the first or most immediate inner-being response to receiving or encountering this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The evidence across VCGs points to several immediate responses, the most consistent of which is faith exercised as an inner turning of will toward Christ. Act 16:30-31 shows the immediate response with narrative clarity: conscience is awakened to danger, the will urgently seeks rescue, and the answer is "believe in the Lord Jesus." Luk 7:50 captures the immediate experiential outcome: Jesus tells the woman "your faith has saved you — go in peace" — the immediate inner-being response is peace (eirēnē) following the faith-act. The Samaritan confession in Joh 4:42 shows that direct encounter produces immediate recognition and trust: "we know this is truly the Savior of the world." Act 2:21 and Rom 10:13 identify "calling on the name" as the immediate act. Luk 19:9 shows immediate household-encompassing rescue arriving with the word. In M38-A-VCG-07, the most immediate response to encountering the impossibility of salvation by human effort is astonished dependence — "who then can be saved?" — which redirects the inner being entirely onto God. The immediate responses thus cluster into: (a) faith exercised as the turning of will toward Christ, (b) peace received as the immediate experiential fruit of that turning, and (c) astonishment that drives the inner being off self-reliance and onto God.
- *T1.5.2* — _Is the immediate response consistent across the verse evidence, or does it vary?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The immediate response varies across the corpus, though several consistent patterns appear. The most consistent immediate response is faith exercised as a turning of the will — this appears across VCG-02, VCG-06, VCG-07, and VCG-09 as the decisive inner act. However, the affective texture of that turning varies significantly. In Luk 7:50 the immediate response is peace. In Act 16:30 it is urgent, alarmed seeking. In Joh 4:42 it is joyful recognition ("we know this is truly the Savior"). In Mat 19:25 and Luk 18:26 the immediate response to encountering salvation's human impossibility is astonishment. In Luk 2:30 (Simeon) the immediate response is quiet fulfillment — "now I can depart in peace." In 2Cor 7:10 the immediate response to godly grief is productive sorrow leading to repentance. In 2Cor 9:15 the immediate response to the gift is doxological overflow: "thanks be to God for his inexpressible gift." The variation is significant: the corpus does not model a uniform emotional or volitional experience of salvation-reception. What is consistent is the volitional orientation (turning toward Christ, trusting, calling) and the resolution of inner tension (from danger/guilt/death toward safety/peace/life). The affective form of the response varies by person, circumstance, and the specific aspect of salvation being encountered.
- *T1.5.3* — _Where the verse evidence is silent on immediate response, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — Several VCGs are relatively silent on immediate response, focusing instead on the condition or condition-outcome without specifying the first experiential movement. M38-A-VCG-03 (gift-language verses) largely describes the gift and its ground rather than the inner-being response to receiving it — with the notable exception of 2Cor 9:15 (doxological overflow) and Act 10:45 (astonishment at Spirit's outpouring). The gift-reception verses of Rom 5:15-17 describe the inner-being state produced (righteousness, life, reigning) but do not narrate the immediate experiential response to receiving the gift. M38-A-VCG-08 (salvation's scope — remnant, Israel, Gentiles) is largely silent on immediate response; these verses address the scope and direction of salvation, not the experiential moment of reception. M38-A-VCG-10 (doxological completion) describes the culminating state rather than the immediate response to initial reception — Rev 7:10, Rev 19:1, and Rev 12:10 show the completed doxological state, not the first movement of faith. The evidence for immediate response is concentrated in VCG-02 (faith-reception mechanisms) and VCG-07 (human impossibility and awakened conscience), with other VCGs providing richer evidence of sustained or completed states than of first responses.
- *T1.6.1* — _What does this characteristic produce in the inner being over time?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The corpus evidences multiple sustained effects developing within the inner being. (1) Growing assurance: the inner being moves from initial faith-reception toward settled confidence — Rom 5:9 shows future salvation from wrath secured by past justification; Heb 7:25 names Christ's perpetual intercession as the ground of unbroken rescue; 2Ti 4:18 depicts Paul's inner rest in "confident assurance of final eschatological preservation." (2) Persevering will: sustained possession of salvation shapes the will toward endurance — Mat 10:22, Mat 24:13, 1Cor 15:2, Heb 2:3 all show that the saved inner being is one that holds fast rather than drifts. (3) Moral formation: Tit 2:11 states that grace "appeared bringing salvation" and then teaches the person to live rightly; 2Th 2:13 joins salvation to sanctification through the Spirit's transforming work; 1Ti 4:16 connects a teacher's watchful conscience to the ongoing saving of himself and others. (4) Doxological orientation: over time the saved inner being is re-oriented toward praise and gratitude — the trajectory from Eph 2:8 (gift received) to Rev 7:10 (gift ascribed in praise) traces this arc. (5) Missional orientation: 1Cor 9:22, 1Cor 10:33, and Rom 11:14 show the apostle's sustained inner identity shaped entirely by the goal of others' rescue — salvation received produces a will directed outward toward others' salvation.
- *T1.6.2* — _What states, qualities, capacities, or orientations does sustained exposure to or possession of this characteristic establish?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — Sustained possession of this characteristic establishes the following states, qualities, capacities, and orientations in the inner being: (1) Settled assurance of eschatological security: Heb 7:25 grounds this in Christ's perpetual intercession; 2Ti 4:18 depicts it as "confident assurance of final eschatological preservation"; 1Th 5:9 names it as "election peace" replacing dread of condemnation. (2) Persevering will resistant to defection: Mat 10:22, 1Cor 15:2, Phili 2:12, and Heb 2:3 all presuppose that sustained salvation-possession requires and produces a will that holds rather than drifts. (3) Deepening knowledge of Christ: 2Pe 3:18 calls for growing in "grace and knowledge" of the Savior; 2Ti 3:15 shows Scripture making the mind "wise for salvation through faith." (4) Moral seriousness and holiness: Tit 2:11-12 shows salvation's grace teaching the person to live rightly; 2Ti 1:9 names the calling as one "to holiness"; 1Ti 2:15 ties sustained salvation to "faith, love, holiness, and self-control." (5) Missional orientation toward others: the apostle's sustained inner identity in 1Cor 9:22 and 1Cor 10:33 is entirely shaped by the goal of saving others — this is a capacity established over time, not merely an immediate response. (6) Doxological habituation: the arc toward Rev 7:10 and Rev 19:1 suggests that sustained salvation progressively re-orients the inner being toward praise as its natural mode.
- *T1.6.3* — _Does the sustained effect differ from the immediate response — and if so, how?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — Yes, the sustained effect differs meaningfully from the immediate response in kind, depth, and scope. The immediate response (T1.5.1) is characterized by the volitional act of faith-turning, accompanied by peace, urgency resolved, astonishment at divine sufficiency, or joyful recognition. These are punctiliar or near-punctiliar inner events. The sustained effect, by contrast, is characterized by: (1) Developed assurance replacing initial faith-exercise — whereas faith is the first movement, assurance (the inner confidence that rescue is secured and perpetual) is the sustained state. Heb 7:25 and 2Ti 4:18 show this as a deep, settled inner rest, not the initial urgency of calling on the name. (2) Persevering will replacing initial decision — Mat 10:22 and 1Cor 15:2 show that "holding fast" is the sustained form of what began as an act of believing; the same inner faculty is engaged but its sustained expression is endurance, not initial choice. (3) Moral formation replacing conscience-cleansing — the immediate effect on conscience is cleansing (Tit 3:5); the sustained effect is moral seriousness, holiness, and self-controlled living (Tit 2:11-12). (4) Missional orientation replacing self-focused reception — the immediate effect is reception of rescue for oneself; the sustained effect re-orients the inner being outward toward others' salvation (1Cor 9:22, Jam 5:20). The sustained effect is thus qualitatively richer, more integrated, and more other-oriented than the immediate response.
- *T1.7.1* — _What inner conditions or orientations enable genuine reception of this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The corpus identifies several enabling inner conditions. (1) Faith/trust directed toward Christ: this is the primary and irreducible condition — Eph 2:8, Mar 16:16, Act 16:31, 1Pe 1:9. Faith is not a work but the inner posture of receptive trust. (2) Awakened conscience: the jailer's question in Act 16:30 shows that genuine reception requires a conscience aware of danger — the inner being must recognize its need before it can receive rescue. (3) Meekness of will: Jam 1:21 identifies "meekness" as the inner orientation that allows the implanted word to save the soul — receptive humility enables the word's saving effect. (4) Repentance and inner turning: Act 2:38, Act 2:40, and 2Cor 7:10 (M38-A-VCG-09) show that genuine reception involves godly grief and will-turning away from sin — repentance is the inner movement that opens the will to receive salvation. (5) Hearing receptively: Joh 4:42 (the Samaritans' direct encounter), Act 11:14 (household saved through proclaimed message), and Act 13:26 (salvation-word sent to those who hear) all identify receptive hearing as an enabling condition. (6) Confession: Rom 10:9-10 includes mouth-confession alongside heart-belief as the paired inner-outer act that constitutes genuine reception.
- *T1.7.2* — _What inner conditions block, distort, or prevent reception?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The corpus identifies several blocking conditions with specificity. (1) Deliberate will-closure against truth: 2Th 2:10 names "refusing love of truth" as the inner condition that forfeits salvation; this is not ignorance but active rejection. (2) Heart preoccupied with wealth: Mar 10:26 (M38-A-VCG-07) identifies wealth as a barrier whose power generates the disciples' astonished question "who can be saved?" — riches orient the inner will toward self-sufficiency rather than receptive dependence. (3) Mercenary or transactional posture toward the gift: Act 8:20 shows Simon's heart as "mercenary" — attempting to purchase the Spirit's gift — which Peter identifies as a spiritually dangerous inner orientation. (4) Passive indifference or neglect: Heb 2:3 explicitly names neglect as a condition that blocks reception — the inner being that assumes rescue without engagement is in peril. (5) Seeking to preserve the self: Mat 16:25 and the VCG-06 parallels show that self-protective clinging — the instinct to save one's own life — is a structural blocker of eschatological salvation. (6) External word-theft before root: Luk 8:12 identifies the devil removing the word from the heart before it can produce belief — the condition blocked is faith, and the mechanism is the word failing to take root in the inner soil. (7) Apostasy after initial reception: 2Pe 2:20 and Heb 6:4 (M38-A-VCG-03) show that returning to defilement after tasting the gift places the inner being in greater peril than prior ignorance.
- *T1.7.3* — _What is the inner-being state of the person who encounters this characteristic but does not receive it?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The corpus characterizes the non-receiving inner being with notable specificity. (1) Perishing: 1Cor 1:18 and 2Cor 2:15 name the structural state as perishing — not a neutral undecided condition but an active movement toward destruction. The same word, the same moment, the same proclamation produces opposite inner outcomes. (2) Willful truth-refusal: 2Th 2:10 identifies those perishing as those who "refused love of truth" — the inner state is not ignorance but deliberate rejection, a will actively turned away. (3) Closed perception: Act 7:25 shows people unable to perceive the salvation being offered — "understanding was closed" — suggesting a non-receiving inner state characterized by a failure of receptive comprehension. (4) Self-protective clinging: Mat 16:25 and its parallels (M38-A-VCG-06) show the non-receiving inner state as one that seeks to preserve its own life, grasping at self-security rather than releasing to Christ. (5) Increasing peril after partial encounter: 2Pe 2:20 and Heb 6:4 describe a particularly acute non-receiving state — those who have tasted the gift and turned away are "worse off than before," in a condition of greater inner danger than pure ignorance. (6) Condemnation: Mar 16:16 names the non-receiving state directly — unbelief yields condemnation. The inner state of the non-receiving person is therefore not passive neutrality but an active condition of perishing, self-enclosure, and increasing exposure to judgment.
- *T1.8.1* — _What is the primary inner-being dimension of this characteristic from the programme's dimension vocabulary?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The primary inner-being dimension of this characteristic is the will/volitional dimension, with the faith-faculty as its specific operation. The decisive inner act throughout the corpus is the turning of the will toward Christ in trust: calling on his name (Act 2:21, Rom 10:13), believing in the heart (Rom 10:9-10), exercising faith (Eph 2:8), confessing with the mouth (Rom 10:10), working out salvation "with reverent seriousness" (Phili 2:12). The self-surrender passages (M38-A-VCG-06: Mat 16:25, Mar 8:35, Luk 9:24, Luk 17:33) make the volitional character most explicit: the decisive inner movement is the will releasing its self-protective claim. The endurance passages (M38-A-VCG-05) confirm that the sustained form of the characteristic is the persevering will that does not defect. The structural opposite (refusing love of truth — 2Th 2:10; will seeking self-preservation — Mat 16:25) is equally volitional. While faith engages the mind (knowledge of Christ, truth received) and produces affective states (peace, joy, praise), the primary constitutional locus is the will — the inner faculty of trust-directed choice and sustained orientation toward God as Savior.
- *T1.8.2* — _What evidence from the verse evidence supports this classification?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — The classification of the will/volitional dimension as primary is supported by the following specific evidence. First, the acts of reception are volitional: calling on the name (Act 2:21, Rom 10:13) is an act of the will directed toward God; confessing with the mouth alongside heart-believing (Rom 10:9-10) involves deliberate self-declaration; exercising faith (Eph 2:8) is a volitional orientation. Second, the self-surrender passages (M38-A-VCG-06: Mat 16:25, Mar 8:35, Luk 9:24, Luk 17:33) are the most explicitly volitional verses in the corpus — the inner act is the will releasing its self-protective grasp. Third, the endurance passages (M38-A-VCG-05) classify sustained salvation as a matter of will-persistence: "enduring to the end," "holding fast," "working out salvation with reverent seriousness" (Phili 2:12). Fourth, the blocking conditions (T1.7.2) are primarily volitional: refusing truth (2Th 2:10), neglecting salvation (Heb 2:3), seeking to save one's own life (Mat 16:25) — all are acts of misdirected will. Fifth, the awakened-conscience passages (M38-A-VCG-07) show the will's reorientation as the decisive inner event: Act 16:30 captures the moment of the will "urgently seeking rescue" as the pivot toward salvation. The mind and affections are engaged (knowledge of Christ, peace, praise) but always as expressions of and responses to the primary volitional turning.
- *T1.8.3* — _Does this characteristic carry secondary dimensions — and if so, what are they, and do they compete with the primary classification?_
  → **[CHAR-1]** E — Yes, the corpus evidences multiple secondary dimensions that enrich but do not displace the primary volitional classification. (1) Cognitive/noetic dimension: 2Ti 3:15 (scripture making one "wise for salvation through faith"), Act 13:26 (salvation-word sent to receptive hearers), Luk 1:77 (salvation-knowledge given through forgiveness), and Act 7:25 (understanding closed as a blocking condition) all show that the mind's reception of truth is a real component of the characteristic. The cognitive dimension is present but secondary — it is the will's trust-directed orientation that activates the mind's receptive knowledge, not vice versa. (2) Affective/experiential dimension: Peace (Luk 7:50), joy (Luk 19:9), astonishment (Mat 19:25), gratitude and praise (2Cor 9:15, Rev 7:10, Rev 19:1) are all affective responses arising from the characteristic. These are real inner-being states produced by salvation but they follow from the volitional reception rather than constituting its primary locus. (3) Conscience dimension: the awakened conscience seeking rescue (Act 16:30), the cleansed conscience after salvation (Tit 3:5), and the watchful conscience sustaining it (1Ti 4:16) constitute a conscience-dimension that is closely related to but distinguishable from pure volitional will. These secondary dimensions do not compete with the primary volitional classification; rather, they confirm it by showing the will's turning activating and integrating the other inner-being faculties around it.
