<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T4 findings for Physical rescue from mortal danger -->

**T4 — Relational Interfaces**

- *T4.1.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating from God toward the human person — and if so, how?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — Yes, the evidence shows God operating as the rescuing agent directed toward the endangered human person across multiple VCGs. In MB-VCG-01, Mat 8:25 presents the disciples crying 'Save us' to a divine presence who then acts; Mat 14:30 shows Peter's cry 'Lord, save me' met with immediate divine response — the rescue is from God toward the sinking human. Psa 69:18 (H6299 pa.dah) calls for God to 'draw near and ransom the soul,' framing the divine movement as proximity-then-rescue directed personally at the endangered self. In MB-VCG-03, Heb 5:7 records that Jesus 'prayed with anguished cries to be saved from death and was heard' — the Father's hearing and response is the divine-toward-human vector, even where the subject is the Son. In MB-VCG-04, Heb 11:7's Noah receives divinely-warned deliverance, and Luk 1:71's Benedictus announces God's act of rescue from enemies for a corporate people. The operative mechanism is consistently responsive: God moves toward the human in the moment of mortal threat, and the rescue is enacted by divine agency rather than human capacity.
- *T4.1.2* — _What does the evidence reveal about the basis on which God extends this characteristic — is it conditional, unconditional, covenantal, or re…_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The dominant basis revealed by the evidence is responsive: God extends physical rescue in direct response to the cry or petition of the endangered person. In Mat 14:30 Peter's cry is immediately answered; in Mat 8:25 the disciples' collective cry precedes the divine act of calming the storm. Psa 69:18 pleads for God to 'draw near' as though divine proximity must be invoked — rescue is responsive to supplication. MB-VCG-03 (Heb 5:7) specifies that Jesus was 'heard because of his reverence' — adding a qualitative condition (reverence, godly fear) to the responsiveness, suggesting the basis is not unconditional but is shaped by the posture of the petitioner. MB-VCG-04's Heb 11:7 introduces a covenantal dimension: Noah acts on divine warning within an existing covenant relationship, and the rescue is the covenantal consequence of that obedient faith. MB-VCG-05's Act 27:20 presents the inverse — where no rescue is expected and none is yet extended, hope is abandoned — but Paul's subsequent reassurance (not in the verse set) implies rescue ultimately does come. Across the evidence: the basis is primarily responsive-to-faith-cry, with covenantal dimensions where sustained obedience is in view, and with reverence as a qualifier in the Son's own petition.
- *T4.1.3* — _What does God's extension of this characteristic reveal about his disposition toward the human person?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence reveals God's disposition as one of proximate, responsive attentiveness to the endangered human. Psa 69:18 ('draw near to my soul, redeem it') implies God's willingness to close relational and spatial distance when the person is in peril — his disposition is toward nearness, not remoteness, in crisis. Mat 14:30's immediate response to Peter's single-word cry indicates a disposition of readiness: rescue does not require elaborate petition, only turning. Heb 5:7's statement that the Son was 'heard because of his reverence' discloses a disposition not of mechanical conditionality but of personal attentiveness to the quality of the petitioner's approach — God receives the anguished prayer and responds. MB-VCG-04's Heb 11:7 shows God as the one who warns (takes initiative to communicate danger) and thereby enables the faith-response that produces rescue — God's disposition includes prevenient communication. Across the evidence, the divine disposition is: responsive to the faith-cry, near in crisis, personally attentive to posture, and willing to warn as an act of protective provision.
- *T4.1.4* — _If the evidence is silent on God-to-human operation, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence is not silent on this direction; it is the primary axis of the characteristic. See findings T4.1.1, T4.1.2, and T4.1.3 above. God as rescuing agent directed toward the endangered human is attested in MB-VCG-01 (Mat 8:25, Mat 14:30, Psa 69:18), MB-VCG-03 (Heb 5:7), and MB-VCG-04 (Heb 11:7, Luk 1:71). No silence exists on this interface.
- *T4.2.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating in the human person's movement toward God — in seeking, supplication, worship, or…_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — Yes, the human-to-God movement is the defining interior phenomenon of this characteristic in its purest registers. MB-VCG-01 provides the clearest data: Mat 14:30 (Peter crying 'Lord, save me') and Mat 8:25 (disciples crying 'Save us, we perish') both present the imperilled human turning urgently toward God in the sole available act of the creaturely will — supplication under mortal duress. Psa 69:18 frames this movement as pleading for divine nearness: 'draw near to my soul, redeem it.' MB-VCG-03 deepens this register: Joh 12:27 shows Jesus himself contemplating the prayer 'Father, save me from this hour,' and Heb 5:7 records his 'anguished cries' offered 'with reverence' — the human-to-God movement here is not panic alone but anguished petition combined with deep reverence and volitional surrender. MB-VCG-04's Heb 11:7 presents Noah's sustained, obedient faith as the vehicle of the human-toward-God movement — not a single cry but embodied covenantal response over time.
- *T4.2.2* — _What does the evidence reveal about the inner posture required for this movement?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence reveals three distinct inner postures, each present in different registers of the sub-group. First, urgent, desperate faith-cry under terror: Mat 8:25 and Mat 14:30 (MB-VCG-01) present the posture as panic-driven but genuinely directed — the cry is to a specific rescuing presence, not mere screaming; faith is exercised even in its most elemental and terrified form. Second, reverent anguish with volitional surrender: Heb 5:7 (MB-VCG-03) specifies that Jesus was heard 'because of his reverence' (eulabeia) — the posture includes not only desperate petition but deep, reverential submission to the Father's will. Joh 12:27 shows the inner movement of contemplating the cry and then surrendering it: 'for this purpose I came to this hour.' Third, sustained, obedient faith acting over time: Heb 11:7 (MB-VCG-04) presents Noah's inner posture as 'reverent fear' that issues in embodied covenantal obedience — a prolonged rather than instantaneous posture. The contrast with Luk 23:39 (faith absent, demanding without reverence or repentance) implies that the absence of faith-posture is what renders the cry ineffective or unfruitful.
- *T4.2.3* — _What does the human-to-God direction of this characteristic reveal about the person's relationship with God?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The human-to-God movement in this characteristic reveals a relationship characterised by asymmetric dependence and practical recognition of divine sovereignty over life and death. In MB-VCG-01, the disciples' and Peter's cries reveal that in the moment of mortal threat, the default movement of the inner being is toward God rather than toward human resources — even imperfect, terror-driven faith recognises where rescue must come from. In MB-VCG-03, Joh 12:27 and Heb 5:7 reveal that even the Son's relationship with the Father includes the possibility of anguished petition for relief from suffering — the relationship is not one of stoic self-sufficiency but of genuine creaturely dependence within filial bond. Heb 5:7's 'reverence' (eulabeia) discloses a relationship in which the Father is approached not merely as resource but as one whose will is to be respected — the relationship contains both intimacy and holy awe. Psa 69:18's pleading for divine nearness reveals a relationship in which physical danger is experienced as relational distance to be closed.
- *T4.2.4* — _If the evidence is silent on human-to-God operation, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence is not silent; human-to-God movement in supplication and petition is the hallmark inner-being phenomenon of this sub-group. See T4.2.1 and T4.2.2 above. MB-VCG-01, MB-VCG-03, and MB-VCG-04 all contain human-to-God operation. No silence exists on this interface.
- *T4.3.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic being extended by one person toward another — and if so, how does it operate in that giving…_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence for human-to-human giving is present but indirect. MB-VCG-04 provides the clearest instance: Heb 11:7 shows Noah's faith-driven construction of the ark saving his household — the rescue is physically extended by one person (Noah) to those entrusted to his care (his family). The giving operates through sustained, obedient action motivated by reverent faith rather than by direct intervention in another's crisis. Luk 1:71's corporate rescue also implies that the acts of faithful figures (Zechariah prophesying, Zechariah's priestly lineage) carry deliverance toward a wider people. MB-VCG-02's crucifixion verses ironically testify to Jesus having 'saved others' (Mar 15:31, Luk 23:35) — his prior healing and rescue acts constituting a giving of physical deliverance from one person to others. Outside these instances, the characteristic primarily runs along the God-to-human axis; human-to-human physical rescue is peripheral in the verse set.
- *T4.3.2* — _What inner conditions or orientations in the giver enable genuine extension of this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence for human-to-human giving is limited (primarily MB-VCG-04), but it is sufficient to identify enabling inner conditions. In Heb 11:7, Noah's giving of rescue to his household is enabled by 'reverent faith' (eulabeia + pistis) that receives divine warning as credible and acts upon it over time. The inner condition is not courage per se but obedient reverence — a faith that takes the divine word seriously enough to enact it embodied, structurally, and persistently (building the ark). The mockers of MB-VCG-02 ironically testify that Jesus's prior giving of rescue to others ('he saved others') flowed from a posture that included willingness to expend himself for others while refusing self-preservation — 'self withheld' and 'will surrendered' as inner orientations that enabled the giving. Direct evidence for human-to-human giving conditions is thin; the corpus primarily depicts God as the giver, with Noah as the single extended human-giving case.
- *T4.3.3* — _What does the evidence reveal about what the person must have received or become before they can genuinely give this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence is limited but instructive. MB-VCG-04's Heb 11:7 implies that Noah could give rescue to his household because he had first received a divine warning ('warned by God about things not yet seen') and had become a man of 'reverent faith.' The giving capacity flows from prior reception of divine communication and a formed inner orientation of obedient reverence. MB-VCG-02's ironic testimony that Jesus 'saved others' (Mar 15:31, Luk 23:35) implies that the giving of rescue to others flows from having the power and will to rescue — which in Jesus's case is intrinsic rather than received. For human givers outside Jesus, the evidence (primarily Heb 11:7) suggests: prior divine communication must be received, and the inner being must be formed toward reverent, obedient faith before rescue can be extended to others. The evidence does not provide a generalised account of what human givers must have received; it offers only the Noah instance.
- *T4.3.4* — _If the evidence is silent on the giving direction, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence is not entirely silent: Heb 11:7 (MB-VCG-04) presents Noah as giving rescue to his household, and the crucifixion-irony verses (MB-VCG-02) testify to Jesus's prior giving of rescue to others. However, direct human-to-human giving of physical rescue is peripheral in the corpus, with God as the primary giver. The human-giving direction is present but lightly attested.
- *T4.4.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic being received by a person from another — and if so, how does it operate in that reception?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — Reception of physical rescue from another person is attested in MB-VCG-04: Noah's household receives the deliverance his faith-action provides; they are 'saved' through his obedience. MB-VCG-02's ironic mockers inadvertently testify that others had previously received physical rescue from Jesus (Mar 15:31 'he saved others'). The primary reception dynamic in the corpus, however, runs from God to the human: in MB-VCG-01, the disciples and Peter receive divine rescue in response to their cry — reception is immediate, bodily (rescued from drowning), and follows the faith-cry as its direct consequence. In MB-VCG-03, Heb 5:7 presents the Son as receiving a hearing from the Father — the rescue sought is received as answered prayer, though resolved through surrender rather than physical escape. Reception, where it appears, operates as the terminus of the faith-cry arc.
- *T4.4.2* — _What inner conditions enable or block reception of this characteristic from another person?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence is most explicit on blocking conditions. Luk 23:39 (MB-VCG-02) presents a criminal who demands physical rescue 'without repentance or faith' — the keywords 'faith absent' and 'unbelief' mark this as blocked reception. The contrast with the penitent criminal implies that a posture of genuine acknowledgement and trust is what enables reception. In MB-VCG-04, Noah's household receives rescue by being within the orbit of the faith-actor; no inner condition of their own is explicitly described — the evidence is silent on whether they themselves exercise faith or simply benefit from Noah's. In MB-VCG-01, Peter and the disciples receive divine rescue through the very act of crying out — the enabling condition is the faith-cry itself, however terrified. MB-VCG-05's Act 27:20 presents total abandonment of hope as the state in which rescue is neither sought nor received; whether the crew later received rescue is outside the verse set. Reception appears enabled by: directed cry to the rescuing presence, presence within the faith-actor's protective action, or genuine (even terrified) petition. Blocked by: demanding without faith, unbelief, or collapse into hopeless resignation without renewed cry.
- *T4.4.3* — _What is the inner-being state of the person who encounters this characteristic from another but does not receive it?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — Luk 23:39 (MB-VCG-02) provides the clearest datum: the criminal who demands rescue from Jesus encounters the rescuing presence but does not receive rescue — his inner state is described as making the demand 'without repentance or faith,' marked by the keywords 'faith absent' and 'unbelief.' The inner state is one of entitled demand without genuine directedness toward the rescuer as such; the person wants the outcome (rescue from crucifixion) but not the relationship or the posture it requires. MB-VCG-05's Act 27:20 presents a different non-reception state: not demand but despair — 'hope of being saved abandoned.' This inner state is not hostile to rescue but has given up expecting it; the persons are not resisting but collapsing. These two cases represent the two non-receptive states in the evidence: active unbelieving demand (Luk 23:39) and passive hopeless resignation (Act 27:20).
- *T4.4.4* — _If the evidence is silent on the receiving direction, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence is not silent on reception: MB-VCG-01 shows disciples and Peter receiving divine rescue, MB-VCG-04 shows Noah's household receiving his faith-driven rescue, and MB-VCG-02 attests prior reception by unnamed others. Reception is present across multiple VCGs. No silence exists on this direction.
- *T4.5.1* — _Does the evidence indicate whether this characteristic operates differently within existing relational bonds versus across relational distan…_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence shows the characteristic operating predominantly within existing relational bonds. MB-VCG-01's storm cries occur within the disciple-Lord relationship; the disciples and Peter appeal to one they already follow. MB-VCG-03's Joh 12:27 and Heb 5:7 depict the Son appealing to the Father — the most intimate possible relational bond. MB-VCG-04's Heb 11:7 operates within the household (Noah saving his family) and within the covenantal relationship of faith and divine warning. Luk 1:71's corporate rescue operates for a covenanted people. MB-VCG-02's crucifixion verse (Luk 23:39) is the sole case where the demand for rescue comes from a relational outsider — a criminal who demands rescue 'without repentance or faith.' The contrast between Luk 23:39 (faith absent, unbelief) and Luk 23:40–43 (the penitent criminal) suggests the characteristic is accessible to those outside existing bonds only where faith or genuine petition is present; Luk 23:39 shows it withheld from demands lacking this posture. Evidence for operation across relational difference is sparse and largely negative.
- *T4.5.2* — _Does this characteristic operate within covenantal contexts only, or does it cross covenantal boundaries?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence suggests the characteristic primarily but not exclusively operates within covenantal contexts. MB-VCG-01 (disciples, Peter) and MB-VCG-03 (Jesus's own petition) are embedded within established covenantal-relational bonds. MB-VCG-04's Heb 11:7 and Luk 1:71 are explicitly covenantal. However, Act 27:20 (MB-VCG-05) takes place aboard a ship with a mixed crew — sailors and prisoners, not a covenanted community — and sōzō is used for the hoped-for physical rescue of all aboard, suggesting the characteristic's temporal-physical dimension can operate outside covenant for non-covenanted persons in mortal danger. Similarly, Mat 8:25's storm threatens the whole boat including Jesus; the rescue extends to all present. MB-VCG-02's crucifixion verses involve mockers and soldiers entirely outside covenant relationship. The evidence does not show the characteristic restricted to covenant insiders in its physical-rescue dimension; its availability to non-covenanted persons in extremis is at least implicit in Act 27:20 and the storm narratives.
- *T4.5.3* — _What does the evidence reveal about the relational scope of this characteristic — who is included and who is not?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence reveals a wide but qualified relational scope. Included in the data are: disciples within the covenant community (MB-VCG-01), Jesus the Son himself (MB-VCG-03), a covenanted household (Heb 11:7, MB-VCG-04), a whole covenant people (Luk 1:71, MB-VCG-04), those in mortal peril regardless of explicit covenant status (Act 27:20, MB-VCG-05 — a mixed ship's crew), and those to whom Jesus had already extended rescue ('he saved others,' MB-VCG-02). Excluded or blocked in the data: the criminal who demands without faith (Luk 23:39, faith absent, unbelief — rescue is not given to him in the verse). The evidence does not show the characteristic explicitly restricted to Israel or to baptised believers; Act 27:20's mixed crew and Mat 8:25's immediate storm rescue suggest a wider availability in mortal-physical contexts. The qualifying factor appears to be the faith-posture or genuine cry rather than ethnic or covenantal membership per se.
- *T4.5.4* — _If the evidence is silent on relational boundaries, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence is not silent; relational boundary data is available, as addressed in T4.5.1–T4.5.3 above. The corpus addresses operation within covenantal bonds (MB-VCG-01, MB-VCG-03, MB-VCG-04), outside those bonds (Act 27:20, Luk 23:39), and the qualifying role of faith-posture. No silence exists on this dimension.
<!-- /EVIDENCE -->

<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T4 silences for Physical rescue from mortal danger -->

**T4 — Relational Interfaces**

- *T4.6.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating in relation to other spiritual beings — angelic or adversarial — and if so, how?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** S — The verse evidence in M38-B contains no material depicting angelic or adversarial spiritual beings as agents, mediators, or opponents of physical rescue. The mockers in MB-VCG-02 are human figures (passersby, priests, soldiers, a criminal); the storm in MB-VCG-01 and MB-VCG-05 is a natural phenomenon; the enemies in Psa 69:18 and Luk 1:71 are human opponents. No adversarial spiritual agency is named as the source of the mortal threat, and no angelic mediation of rescue is depicted in any verse in this sub-group.
- *T4.6.2* — _Is this characteristic a site of adversarial activity — something that can be attacked, distorted, or weaponised by adversarial spiritual po…_
  → **[CHAR-2]** S — The verse evidence in M38-B contains no data depicting adversarial spiritual powers attacking, distorting, or weaponising physical rescue. The threats are natural (storm, crucifixion, illness implied in the sub-group definition) or human (enemies, mockers). No adversarial spiritual agency is presented as opposing or corrupting the faith-cry, the divine rescue, or the human hope for deliverance.
- *T4.6.3* — _Is this characteristic communicated, strengthened, or mediated through angelic ministry in the evidence?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** S — No verse in M38-B depicts angelic beings communicating, strengthening, or mediating physical rescue. The divine rescue in MB-VCG-01 is direct; MB-VCG-03's Heb 5:7 involves no angelic mediator; MB-VCG-04's divine warning to Noah in Heb 11:7 does not specify angelic agency. The evidence is silent on angelic mediation of this characteristic.
- *T4.6.4* — _If the evidence is silent on the spiritual beings interface, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-2]** S — The evidence is silent on the spiritual beings interface across all dimensions — angelic mediation, adversarial attack, and spiritual-being involvement in physical rescue are all absent from the M38-B verse set. This silence is explicit and consistent across all fifteen verses in the sub-group.
<!-- /EVIDENCE -->

<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T5 findings for Physical rescue from mortal danger -->

**T5 — Formative and Developmental Dimension**

- *T5.1.1* — _Does this characteristic produce transformation in the person — and if so, does it change the person's condition, the person's orientation t…_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence shows transformation operating primarily at the level of condition rather than orientation, but with orientation-change present in the higher-register cases. In MB-VCG-01, Mat 14:30 and Mat 8:25 present rescue that changes the person's physical condition — Peter is no longer sinking; the disciples are no longer perishing. The transformation is objective and external: rescued from mortal danger. In MB-VCG-03, however, Joh 12:27 and Heb 5:7 reveal a more complex transformation: Jesus's prayer for rescue from 'this hour' is resolved not by condition-change (he does die) but by orientation-change — the soul troubled by the prospect of suffering moves to embrace its redemptive purpose ('for this purpose I came to this hour'). Heb 5:7's 'heard because of his reverence' implies that the hearing produces not condition-escape but inner resolution through surrender. In MB-VCG-04, Heb 11:7's Noah undergoes no recorded transformation of inner orientation; the transformation is the household's physical survival. The evidence thus shows: condition-change in the panic-cry and household-rescue registers; orientation-change (or orientation-deepening) in the Son's own anguished petition register.
- *T5.1.2* — _Is the transformation produced by this characteristic reversible or irreversible in the verse evidence?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence is mixed and register-dependent. In the physical rescue register (MB-VCG-01), the rescue from drowning is a reversible-class transformation: Peter is rescued from the immediate danger of sinking, but the mortal frailty that made him sinkable remains. The rescue is real but does not permanently alter the person's susceptibility to danger. Act 27:20 (MB-VCG-05) similarly concerns a temporal, physical rescue that, even if it had occurred and hope been maintained, would not permanently secure the persons against future mortal threats. In MB-VCG-04's Heb 11:7, Noah's household is saved from the flood — an irreversible event in that that specific destruction does not recur — but again, the persons remain mortal. The one case with an irreversibility marker is MB-VCG-04's statement that Noah 'became heir of the righteousness that comes by faith' — this inner-being consequence of the faith-rescue arc has a character of enduring deposit rather than mere event. In MB-VCG-03, the Son's volitional surrender (Joh 12:27) and the 'learning obedience' trajectory (Heb 5:7 in context) point toward an irreversible inner transformation — perfection through suffering — that is not undone. The evidence does not present a single answer: physical rescue is event-level and potentially reversible in kind; inner transformation in the surrender/formation registers has irreversible markers.
- *T5.1.3* — _If the evidence is silent on transformation, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence is not silent on transformation; findings are provided in T5.1.1 and T5.1.2 above. Transformation (both condition-change and orientation-change) is attested across MB-VCG-01, MB-VCG-03, and MB-VCG-04. No silence note is required.
- *T5.2.1* — _Does the verse evidence describe a sequence of inner states through which this characteristic moves the person — a before, during, and after…_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — Yes, the evidence describes a clear before-during-after sequence, most explicitly in MB-VCG-01 and MB-VCG-03. The sequence in MB-VCG-01 (Mat 14:30 especially): Before — bold venture (Peter steps onto the water), normal existence disrupted by wind and waves; During — terror and beginning to sink, the faith-cry; After — rescue enacted, and in the broader Matthean account, awe and worship ('truly you are the Son of God,' Mat 14:33). The 'before' is ordinary existence or bold venture; the 'during' is the mortal-threat state including fear and cry; the 'after' is rescue and its aftermath. MB-VCG-03 (Joh 12:27): Before — arrival at the hour of suffering, soul 'troubled'; During — contemplating the cry for rescue, then the volitional surrender ('for this purpose I came'); After — the Father's voice, resolution. MB-VCG-05 (Act 27:20): the sequence is partial — before: voyage and storm building; during: all hope abandoned, inner despair; after: outside the verse, rescue comes. MB-VCG-04 (Heb 11:7): Before — divine warning received; During — reverent faith building the ark; After — household saved, Noah becomes heir of righteousness. The sequence is well-attested.
- *T5.2.2* — _What are those states, and what does the sequence reveal about how this characteristic works?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — Drawing from T5.2.1 findings, the sequence of inner states most fully documented in the corpus is: (1) Ordinary existence or bold venture — the person is engaged with life as normal, or has stepped out in active faith (Mat 14:28–29 context for Peter; Noah's pre-warning state; Jesus's movement toward the hour). (2) Disruption by mortal threat — the threat breaks in (wind and waves, storm, the cross, enemies) and the inner being is seized by fear, terror, trouble, or despair ('soul troubled,' Joh 12:27; 'fear,' Mat 8:25/14:30; 'hope abandoned,' Act 27:20). (3) The crisis-point inner act — either the faith-cry ('Lord, save me,' Mat 14:30; 'Save us,' Mat 8:25; 'save me from this hour,' Joh 12:27 contemplated; 'anguished cries,' Heb 5:7) or, in the negative case, the collapse into hopeless resignation (Act 27:20) or faithless demand (Luk 23:39). (4) Resolution — rescue (Mat 14:30 aftermath; Noah's household; Luk 1:71), or volitional surrender with inner resolution (Joh 12:27 'for this purpose I came'), or continued despair without resolution yet (Act 27:20). What the sequence reveals: the characteristic works by bringing the endangered inner being to the crisis-point where the only available resource is the faith-cry, and the cry is the decisive inner act that either releases rescue or, in the higher register, transforms the inner being's relationship to the suffering itself.
- *T5.2.3* — _If the evidence is silent on sequence, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence is not silent on sequence; the before-during-after sequence is well-attested in MB-VCG-01 and MB-VCG-03, with partial data in MB-VCG-04 and MB-VCG-05. Findings are provided in T5.2.1 and T5.2.2. No silence note is required.
- *T5.3.1* — _What mechanism does this characteristic use to produce change in the person — discipline, encounter, gradual formation, sudden transformatio…_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence identifies two distinct mechanisms operating in different registers. First, sudden encounter: in MB-VCG-01, the mechanism is immediate — the crisis arrives suddenly (wind, waves, sinking), the cry is uttered in a single moment, and the rescue is enacted in direct response. The change (from perishing to rescued) is sudden and event-driven. This is the dominant mechanism in the panic-cry register. Second, gradual formation through obedient faith-action: in MB-VCG-04's Heb 11:7, the mechanism is extended over time — Noah receives warning, and then builds the ark over a prolonged period, with reverent faith as the sustained inner orientation producing eventual deliverance. The change is accumulative rather than instantaneous. A third mechanism is present in MB-VCG-03: anguished petition resolved through volitional surrender — not sudden rescue but deepening of inner orientation through the act of bringing anguish before the Father and finding it heard and resolved through embrace of purpose. This is an interior transformation-through-encounter mechanism distinct from both sudden rescue and gradual formation.
- *T5.3.2* — _Does the evidence distinguish between mechanisms in different contexts?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — Yes, the evidence clearly distinguishes three mechanisms operating in different contexts (see T5.3.1): (1) Sudden-encounter rescue in the panic-cry context (MB-VCG-01: Mat 8:25, Mat 14:30): the crisis arrives without warning, the cry is instantaneous, and rescue is immediate. The mechanism is event-rupture → cry → divine act. No gradual formation is in view; the change is punctiliar. (2) Gradual-formation through sustained obedient faith in the household-rescue context (MB-VCG-04: Heb 11:7): the mechanism is divine warning → reverent reception → extended embodied obedience → rescue as culmination. The time-scale is long; the inner-being formation is cumulative. (3) Anguished-petition-and-surrender in the Son's own crisis context (MB-VCG-03: Joh 12:27, Heb 5:7): the mechanism is mortal-threat awareness → contemplation of rescue-cry → volitional reorientation → heard petition resolved through embrace of purpose. This is neither purely sudden nor purely gradual; it is a deliberate interior act of surrender in the face of known suffering. MB-VCG-05 (Act 27:20) presents a fourth degenerate case: no mechanism of change is enacted; despair without cry, and hope's extinction is the state recorded.
- *T5.3.3* — _If the evidence is silent on mechanism, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence is not silent on mechanism; three distinct mechanisms are identified in T5.3.1 and T5.3.2 above (sudden-encounter rescue, gradual-formation through obedient faith, and anguished-petition-and-surrender). No silence note is required.
- *T5.4.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating in relation to suffering or affliction — as a response to it, a product of it, or…_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — Suffering and mortal affliction is the constitutive context of this entire characteristic — it is the defining precondition, not an occasional feature. Every VCG operates within a context of acute suffering or danger: MB-VCG-01 (drowning threat), MB-VCG-02 (crucifixion), MB-VCG-03 (the 'hour' of suffering and death, 'anguished cries'), MB-VCG-04 (destruction threat, enemies), MB-VCG-05 (shipwreck despair). The characteristic is a response to suffering: the faith-cry is generated by the experience of mortal threat; the divine rescue is directed at the afflicted person. In MB-VCG-03, suffering is not merely context but is itself the occasion for the deepest expression of the characteristic: Heb 5:7's 'anguished cries' and 'reverence' describe the Son's inner-being engagement with imminent death, and Joh 12:27's 'soul troubled' presents suffering as the condition that produces the most profound movement of petition and surrender. Suffering is thus simultaneously context, occasion, and revealer of the faith-cry's depth.
- *T5.4.2* — _Does suffering deepen, test, reveal, or produce this characteristic in the person?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence shows suffering functioning in multiple roles relative to this characteristic: Revealing: Mat 14:30's panic and cry reveals the depth (and the limit) of Peter's faith — he steps out boldly but sinks when the wind is felt; the suffering-context of the waves reveals what was previously untested. Testing: the storm in Mat 8:25 tests the disciples' faith to its limit — they resort to the cry, which is itself a test-outcome revealing that even terror-driven faith-cry suffices. Producing (deepening): Heb 5:7 presents the Son's anguished cries as the condition through which his reverence and obedience were expressed and through which (in the Heb 5:8–9 context at the boundary of the verse set) perfection was produced. Suffering does not merely elicit the cry; it is the instrument through which the inner-being quality is forged. In Heb 11:7's Noah, the threat of destruction (revealed suffering) produces the sustained faith-action that constitutes the characteristic's deepest expression in that register. Act 27:20 presents a negative case: suffering at sufficient extremity produces hope's extinction rather than deepening of the faith-cry — suggesting that suffering can either deepen or overwhelm, depending on whether the inner cry is sustained.
- *T5.4.3* — _If the evidence is silent on the relationship to suffering, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence is not silent on suffering; mortal affliction is the constitutive context of the entire characteristic, and the relationship to suffering is addressed in T5.4.1 and T5.4.2 above. No silence note is required.
- *T5.5.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic participating in the longer arc of character formation and sanctification — shaping the per…_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence for formation over time is present but limited to specific registers. MB-VCG-04's Heb 11:7 most clearly depicts formation: Noah's 'reverent faith' that builds over time and produces both household rescue and his own becoming 'heir of the righteousness that comes by faith.' The obedient faith-action is simultaneously the vehicle of rescue and the instrument of Noah's own formation toward righteousness. In MB-VCG-03, Heb 5:7 is part of a larger Hebrews argument (5:7–9) that the Son 'learned obedience through what he suffered' — the anguished petition and its heard-but-not-escape resolution is presented by the author of Hebrews as formation-through-suffering, producing perfection and becoming 'the source of eternal salvation.' However, the M38-B verse set contains only Heb 5:7, not 5:8–9; the formation arc is implied at the boundary of the verse evidence. The dominant register in this sub-group (panic-cry, crucifixion irony) does not yield formation data — it is too event-focused and moment-specific. Formation is present in the corpus but peripheral.
- *T5.5.2* — _What does the evidence reveal about the role of this characteristic in that longer arc?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence reveals that this characteristic participates in the formation arc primarily in two ways, both attested sparingly in the corpus. First, as the occasion of righteousness-imputation through faith: Heb 11:7 explicitly states that Noah's faith-driven rescue action resulted in his becoming 'heir of the righteousness that comes by faith' — the rescue-faith is simultaneously the ground of his formation as a righteous person. The characteristic is not merely event-rescue but a faith-act that deposits righteousness. Second, as the context for learning obedience through suffering: Heb 5:7 is embedded in a passage (5:7–9) where the Son's anguished petition and heard-but-surrender-resolved crisis is the context for 'learning obedience through what he suffered' — the crisis-petition sequence is intrinsic to the formation arc, not incidental to it. In the panic-cry register (MB-VCG-01), the evidence does not present the rescue as formative over time; it is event-rescue that may occasion subsequent worship and deepened trust (Mat 14:33 context) but the verse evidence within M38-B does not extend to this. The formation role of this characteristic is present but concentrated in the Hebrews evidence (Heb 5:7, 11:7).
- *T5.5.3* — _If the evidence is silent on formation and sanctification, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence is not entirely silent, but formation data is sparse and concentrated in the Hebrews evidence (Heb 5:7, Heb 11:7). The dominant registers of this sub-group (panic-cry, crucifixion-irony) yield little or no formation data. The honest summary: formation is present but peripheral in this corpus; the sub-group's primary phenomenological focus is event-rescue rather than character formation. See T5.5.1 and T5.5.2 for the available evidence.
- *T5.6.1* — _Does the verse evidence point this characteristic toward an eschatological fullness — a future state toward which its present operation is o…_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence is largely non-eschatological by the sub-group's own definition (boundary resolution: M38-B is held distinct from M38-A by the absence of eschatological content). However, two partial indicators are present. Luk 1:71 (MB-VCG-04) places physical rescue within the Benedictus, which has an implicit eschatological horizon — the rescue from enemies is part of the promised Abrahamic and Davidic fulfilment that moves toward a coming redemption. This is eschatological framing even if the immediate referent is political deliverance. Heb 11:7 (MB-VCG-04) situates Noah within the Hebrews 11 faith-hall, which moves toward the eschatological city and resurrection (Heb 11:13–16, 39–40) — Noah's rescue becomes a type within a trajectory pointing toward fullness not yet received. These are horizon-indicators rather than explicit eschatological content within the verses themselves. The characteristic in its panic-cry and crucifixion registers (MB-VCG-01, MB-VCG-02, MB-VCG-05) shows no eschatological orientation.
- *T5.6.2* — _What does the present experience of this characteristic anticipate about its future fullness?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence for eschatological anticipation is limited by the sub-group's definitional boundary (physical rescue distinct from M38-A's eschatological content), but two indicators are present. Heb 11:7 situates Noah's rescue within the Hebrews 11 faith-narrative, which explicitly moves toward a future the patriarchs 'did not receive but saw from a distance' (Heb 11:13, 39–40, outside the verse set but the immediate literary context). Noah's physical rescue from the flood functions in Hebrews as a type of a greater deliverance not yet fully realised. The present experience of mortal rescue anticipates a future in which the rescued are brought fully into the inheritance promised — the 'city that has foundations' (Heb 11:10). Luk 1:71's rescue from enemies within the Benedictus anticipates a Messianic fulfilment that extends beyond mere political deliverance. In the panic-cry register, no eschatological anticipation is present in the verse evidence itself — the rescued Peter and disciples are rescued for continued temporal existence, not explicitly oriented toward eschatological fullness. The eschatological anticipation is present in the Hebrews/Lukan registers only.
- *T5.6.3* — _If the evidence is silent on eschatological trajectory, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence is not entirely silent, but eschatological content is deliberately bounded out of this sub-group by the BOUNDARY resolution (M38-B distinct from M38-A by absence of eschatological content). What eschatological horizon exists is present only at the literary edges of the Hebrews and Lukan evidence (Heb 11:7 within the faith-hall narrative; Luk 1:71 within the Benedictus). The panic-cry, crucifixion-irony, and shipwreck-despair registers are genuinely silent on eschatological trajectory. The partial eschatological indicators are noted in T5.6.1 and T5.6.2; their peripherality reflects the sub-group's intentional definitional boundary.
- *T5.7.2* — _Does the evidence indicate generational consequence — a deposit carried forward beyond the individual?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — Yes, MB-VCG-04 provides the clearest evidence of generational consequence. Heb 11:7 explicitly records that Noah's faith-driven rescue produced household deliverance — his rescue extends to those entrusted to his care, carrying the consequence of his faith beyond his own person to the next generation. The ark saves his family; the consequence of his faith-act is corporate and generational, not confined to the individual. Luk 1:71 (MB-VCG-04) announces rescue 'from our enemies' for a whole people — the soteria is corporate and carries forward through the covenant community, not limited to a single individual's experience. These two verses are the strongest evidence of generational deposit in the corpus. In the panic-cry register (MB-VCG-01) and the Son's anguished-petition register (MB-VCG-03), the evidence is individual rather than generational within the verse set itself. The generational/corporate consequence is present in the corpus but concentrated in MB-VCG-04.
<!-- /EVIDENCE -->

<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T1 findings for Physical rescue from mortal danger -->

**T1 — Definition**

- *T1.1.1* — _What is this characteristic called in the programme — and what does the name itself signal about its essential nature?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The characteristic is named "Physical rescue from mortal danger" (M38-B). The name signals several things about essential nature: first, it is defined by the object of the rescue act — not spiritual salvation, not eschatological deliverance, but physical, embodied, mortal-danger rescue; second, the word "mortal" foregrounds that the danger is death-threatening, not merely uncomfortable, establishing extremity as the operative register; third, the name is event-oriented rather than disposition-oriented, signalling that the characteristic is anchored in an acute situational crisis rather than a sustained dispositional state. The name does not foreground the inner-being dimension (terror, faith-cry, despair) but rather the external referent of the salvation act. This is significant: the inner-being content (fear, panic, urgent cry) is the actual phenomenological data, but the name classifies by what is being rescued from rather than by what the inner being experiences. This signals that the programme treats the characteristic as defined by its context and object, with the inner-being states as its expressions rather than its essence.
- *T1.1.2* — _What do the primary Hebrew and Greek terms reveal at the definitional level — before deeper lexical analysis begins?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The primary Greek term is G4982 sōzō (Mat 8:25; Mat 14:30; Mat 27:40; Mat 27:49; Mar 15:30; Mar 15:31; Luk 23:35; Luk 23:37; Luk 23:39; Joh 12:27; Act 27:20; Heb 5:7) and G4991 soteria (Luk 1:71; Heb 11:7). The Hebrew term is H6299 padah (Psa 69:18). At the definitional level before deeper analysis: sōzō carries at its root the sense of making safe, preserving from destruction, bringing through to safety — it is a transitive rescue verb whose object is a person in danger of perishing. The word operates on a spectrum from physical to spiritual, but in all its instances it implies that the person/thing being saved would otherwise be lost. Soteria is the noun form — salvation, deliverance, safety as a state achieved. Padah (H6299) means to ransom, to redeem by paying a price or by acting to release — it carries the sense of rescue through decisive intervention, often in contexts of redemption from slavery or enemy. The common definitional thread is: a being in genuine danger of destruction, and an external agent who intervenes to bring that being to safety. The terms are all transitive — rescue always has a subject (rescuer), object (endangered one), and a situation of threat. They define the characteristic from the outside (what is happening to the person) before revealing anything about the inner-being state.
- *T1.1.3* — _Does the name carry directional, relational, or constitutional implications that orient the enquiry from the outset?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The name "Physical rescue from mortal danger" carries all three types of implication. Directional: the name implies a movement — rescue is from somewhere (mortal danger) to somewhere else (safety) — establishing the characteristic as inherently directional and dynamic. The enquiry is thereby oriented toward the trajectory of the endangered inner being rather than toward a stable state. Relational: "rescue" requires a rescuer and a rescued — the name implicitly posits a two-party relational structure (endangered creature + rescuing agent) without which "rescue" is not a coherent concept. The enquiry is thereby oriented toward the relationship between the endangered inner being and the divine rescuer rather than toward the inner being in isolation. Constitutional: "physical" specifies the constitutional level — this is not spiritual or eschatological rescue but rescue of the embodied, mortal person, situating the characteristic at the intersection of inner being and physical vulnerability. The enquiry is thereby oriented toward the somatic-affective interface: how mortal bodily danger registers in and through the inner being. All three orientations are present from the outset in the name itself.
- *T1.2.1* — _What kind of inner-being phenomenon does this characteristic appear to be — an act, a disposition, a condition, a quality, or something else…_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The characteristic appears to be a dynamic inner-being condition — not a stable disposition but a transient state activated by acute threat. The evidence across MB-VCG-01 through MB-VCG-05 consistently presents the inner being in movement: terror → desperate faith-cry → rescue (Mat 8:25; Mat 14:30); anguish → petition → surrender (Joh 12:27; Heb 5:7); reverent faith → sustained obedient action → household deliverance (Heb 11:7); hope → hope's extinction → despair (Act 27:20). The characteristic is not a quality one possesses continuously but a condition the inner being enters under specific circumstances of mortal threat. It has the structure of a crisis-state: provoked by external danger, involving affective, volitional, and relational faculties simultaneously, and resolving (or failing to resolve) through either rescue received or hope lost. The crucifixion mockery passages (MB-VCG-02) are the anomaly: the inner-being phenomenon there is primarily ironic observation rather than personal crisis-state, which is why those verses sit somewhat uncomfortably in the sub-group's phenomenological core.
- *T1.2.2* — _Is this characteristic simple in structure or does it appear to have constituent elements at first encounter?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The characteristic is evidently composite in structure at first encounter. The sub-group definition itself names multiple elements: terror, panic, urgent faith-cry, and despair-then-relief. These are not identical phenomena but distinct inner-being movements that together constitute the characteristic. The verse evidence confirms this complexity: Mat 14:30 (MB-VCG-01) shows the movement from bold venture → panic → faith-cry → rescue in rapid succession — at least four distinct moments. Heb 5:7 (MB-VCG-03) shows anguish + reverent petition + being heard — three distinct elements. Heb 11:7 (MB-VCG-04) shows warning received + reverent faith + sustained obedient action + deliverance — four elements across an extended time. The crucifixion mockery passages (MB-VCG-02) add a further element not present elsewhere: the ironic observation of rescue withheld by choice. Act 27:20 (MB-VCG-05) introduces the element of hope's extinction. The characteristic is therefore structurally composite: it has an affective element (fear, terror, anguish, despair), a volitional element (cry, petition, surrender, or hopeless closure), a relational element (direction toward rescuer or absence thereof), and a resolution element (rescue received, rescue withheld, or hope lost). These elements co-occur in varying configurations across the sub-group.
- *T1.2.3* — _What is the current best working description of this characteristic — encapsulating its constitutional location, the faculties it primarily …_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — Working description: Physical rescue from mortal danger is a composite, crisis-activated inner-being condition located at the intersection of affective life and volitional agency, triggered by acute or sustained mortal physical threat, in which the person's ordinary inner equilibrium is overwhelmed by terror, anguish, or despair, and the will is pressed to its sole available movement: a desperate, directional cry or reverent petition toward the divine rescuer. The characteristic primarily engages the affective faculty (fear, terror, anguish, despair), the volitional faculty (the act of crying out, petitioning, surrendering, or in failure-cases, closing off in hopelessness), and the relational faculty (the inner being's orientation toward God as the only available source of rescue). Its impact on the person when received in faith is relief from terror, deepened relational dependence on the rescuer, and progressive formation as a faith-heir; when blocked or absent, its impact is despair, faithless demand, or despairing resignation. The characteristic is not a stable disposition but a dynamic state that has temporal structure — it is entered, navigated, and resolved (or failed) — and its resolution either deepens faith-identity or deepens despair.
- *T1.3.1* — _What is the structural opposite of this characteristic — the inner-being reality that stands against or excludes it?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The structural opposite of this characteristic, as evidenced by the corpus, is the inner-being state of self-sufficiency under threat — the confidence that no cry toward an external rescuer is needed or possible. This takes two distinct forms in the evidence. First, faithless demand: Luk 23:39 (MB-VCG-02) presents the criminal demanding rescue without petition, faith, or surrender — treating the would-be rescuer as an instrument rather than approaching him in dependent relation. Second, extinct hope: Act 27:20 (MB-VCG-05) presents the state in which even the cry is given up — not defiant self-sufficiency but a collapse into despair that no longer reaches outward. Both modes exclude the characteristic because the faith-cry is absent: the inner being has either never formed the relational dependency (faithless demand) or has abandoned it (hope's extinction). The structural opposite is therefore: inner closure to the possibility of received rescue, whether through presumptuous demand or through despairing resignation.
- *T1.3.2* — _What does this characteristic explicitly exclude or resist?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The characteristic explicitly excludes or resists three inner-being states evidenced in the corpus: (1) Faithless demand (Luk 23:39, MB-VCG-02): the criminal's demand for rescue "without repentance or faith" resists the characteristic's constitutive element of relational petition — demanding rescue is not the same as crying for it in faith. (2) Despairing closure (Act 27:20, MB-VCG-05): when all hope of rescue is abandoned, the inner movement toward the rescuer ceases — the characteristic requires at minimum the possibility of a cry; once hope is extinct, the characteristic's operation is suspended. (3) Self-sufficient mockery (MB-VCG-02 generally): the mockers at the crucifixion who deploy sōzō as taunt implicitly resist the characteristic by treating rescue as something to be demanded or scoffed at rather than received through dependent petition. The characteristic also implicitly resists self-rescue: in MB-VCG-02 and MB-VCG-03, the very possibility of self-rescue is present but chosen against — the characteristic requires that the rescue come from outside, not from the self. Self-sufficiency under mortal threat is thus structurally excluded.
- *T1.3.3* — _What is this characteristic not — where does it end and something else begin?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The characteristic is not: (1) Eschatological salvation (M38-A): per the researcher's boundary resolution, verses in which sōzō refers to final spiritual deliverance from sin and judgment are held in a distinct sub-group; M38-B ends where eternal destiny begins. The fear-faith dynamic in this sub-group is time-bound, body-oriented, and does not engage eschatological content. (2) Penitential faith: the characteristic does not involve repentance of sin as a constitutive element — the disciples' storm-cry and Peter's sinking are not acts of confession; even Heb 5:7 does not present the Son's prayer as penitential. Where penitential faith appears (the contrasting criminal of Luk 23:40–43, not in this corpus), it is adjacent but distinct. (3) Confident assurance: the characteristic involves crisis-state, not the settled trust of one who knows deliverance is secured — the faith-cry is the opposite of triumphant confidence; it is faith exercised at its most fragile and desperate. (4) The characteristic ends where the crisis is resolved: once rescue is received and the mortal threat is removed, the characteristic's crisis-state dissolves — what may follow (deepened faith-identity, gratitude, witness) belongs to other characteristics. The characteristic is bounded by the onset and resolution of acute mortal threat.
- *T1.4.1* — _In what distinct ways does this characteristic operate within the inner person?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The verse evidence reveals at least four distinct modes of operation: (1) Panic-cry mode (Mat 8:25; Mat 14:30, MB-VCG-01; Psa 69:18, MB-VCG-01): the inner being is gripped by acute terror and the characteristic operates as an involuntary eruption of desperate petition — the cry is the sole act of the creaturely will in the moment of overwhelm. (2) Anguished-surrender mode (Joh 12:27; Heb 5:7, MB-VCG-03): the characteristic operates through layered inner movement — genuine creaturely distress is held alongside deep reverence and volitional surrender, producing a petition that is both desperate and willingly subordinated to redemptive purpose. (3) Sustained-obedience mode (Heb 11:7, MB-VCG-04): the characteristic operates not through crisis-cry but through reverent faith expressed in prolonged, embodied action over time, producing deliverance for those under one's care rather than for oneself alone. (4) Corporate-anticipatory mode (Luk 1:71, MB-VCG-04): the characteristic operates at the level of a people's hope and expectation of rescue from collective mortal threat, engaging the inner being at the level of solidarity and anticipatory relief rather than personal panic.
- *T1.4.2* — _Does this characteristic operate differently depending on context, direction, or constitutional level?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — Yes, the evidence shows variation across context, direction, and constitutional level. By context: in acute storm-crisis (Mat 8:25; Mat 14:30, MB-VCG-01), the characteristic operates as involuntary eruption — the crisis context collapses deliberation and the cry is immediate; in the extended flood context (Heb 11:7, MB-VCG-04), it operates as sustained obedient response to a prophesied warning, with deliberation and action over time. By direction: in Mat 14:30 and Heb 5:7 (MB-VCG-01, MB-VCG-03), the direction is individual-to-God; in Luk 1:71 and Heb 11:7 (MB-VCG-04), the direction includes a corporate or household dimension — rescue is received for and through others, not only for the self. By constitutional level: in the crucifixion passages (MB-VCG-02) and Joh 12:27/Heb 5:7 (MB-VCG-03), the one who could exercise physical self-rescue chooses not to — the characteristic operates at the level of the will's disposition toward sacrifice rather than at the level of terror-response. In MB-VCG-03, the characteristic operates in the Son whose constitutional level is uniquely layered (creaturely distress + divine mission), producing a mode of operation not available to ordinary human experience.
- *T1.4.3* — _Does this characteristic have a communicative or speech-based mode of operation — and if so, how does it function?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — Yes, the characteristic has a prominently communicative-speech mode of operation. In Mat 8:25 (MB-VCG-01), the disciples cry out with speech: "Save us, Lord; we are perishing" — the characteristic operates as voiced petition, a speech act directed at the rescuer. In Mat 14:30 (MB-VCG-01), Peter "cried out" (ekraxen) — a loud vocal act. In Psa 69:18 (MB-VCG-01), the psalmist speaks directly to God: "Draw near to me, redeem me." In Heb 5:7 (MB-VCG-03), the Son's prayer is described with "loud cries and tears" (krauge kai dakrua) — vocal anguish is the medium. Joh 12:27 (MB-VCG-03) presents spoken deliberation: "What shall I say? 'Father, save me from this hour'?" — the speech mode is there even in its considered, deliberative form. The speech mode functions as the externalisation of the inner-being state: terror or anguish that cannot be resolved internally is directed outward in voice toward the rescuer. The cry is not merely expressive (emoting) but petitionary (seeking response): it is a speech act that expects an answer. This communicative mode is the characteristic's primary mode in its crisis-register; in Heb 11:7 and Luk 1:71 (MB-VCG-04), the mode shifts to action and communal declaration rather than individual voiced cry.
- *T1.5.1* — _What is the first or most immediate inner-being response to receiving or encountering this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — Where rescue is received, the most immediate inner-being response evidenced is relief that displaces fear — the resolution of acute terror. In Mat 14:30–31 (MB-VCG-01), the immediate context implies that Peter's cry is followed by Jesus extending his hand and lifting him — the resolution of sinking and panic into security. In Heb 5:7 (MB-VCG-03), the Father "hears" the petition — the immediate inner response to being heard is the registration that the cry has reached its object. In Act 27:20 (MB-VCG-05), the inverse is evidenced: when no rescue appears available, the immediate inner response is the abandonment of hope — despair is the immediate response to the apparent absence of this characteristic. The immediate response thus varies depending on whether rescue is received or withheld: reception produces relief-from-terror; apparent absence produces despair. The evidence does not present prolonged or mediated immediate responses — the inner-being reaction to rescue is depicted as instantaneous reorientation from fear to safety.
- *T1.5.2* — _Is the immediate response consistent across the verse evidence, or does it vary?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The immediate response varies significantly across the verse evidence. In MB-VCG-01 (Mat 8:25; Mat 14:30; Psa 69:18), the immediate response to mortal threat is the urgent cry — consistent in its panic-driven, directional character. In MB-VCG-02 (Mat 27:40; 27:49; Mar 15:30; 15:31; Luk 23:35; 23:37; 23:39), the "immediate response" is not a cry from the endangered but rather mockery or demand from observers — the phenomenological register is entirely different; no personal terror-cry is present. In MB-VCG-03 (Joh 12:27; Heb 5:7), the immediate response is anguished petition combined with reverent surrender — more layered and deliberate than the panic-cry. In MB-VCG-04 (Luk 1:71; Heb 11:7), the immediate response is not an acute cry but either corporate anticipatory relief (Luk 1:71) or reverent, obedient action (Heb 11:7). In MB-VCG-05 (Act 27:20), the immediate response is the extinction of hope — no cry, only despair. The response is therefore not consistent: it ranges from panic-cry to anguished petition to obedient action to hopeless silence, depending on VCG context.
- *T1.5.3* — _Where the verse evidence is silent on immediate response, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The verse evidence is silent on immediate inner-being response in the following instances: Mat 27:40; Mat 27:49; Mar 15:30; Mar 15:31; Luk 23:35; Luk 23:37 (MB-VCG-02) — these verses record the words and postures of mockers and observers; they do not record the immediate inner-being response of Jesus to the taunts, nor do they describe any inner movement of the mockers themselves upon their own speech. The immediate inner state of Jesus in response to the mockery is not the subject of these verses (that is addressed separately in Joh 12:27 and Heb 5:7 in MB-VCG-03). Luk 1:71 (MB-VCG-04) — the Benedictus is a declarative statement about what God has done or will do; it does not record the immediate inner response of any individual who encounters the rescue. The evidence for immediate response is therefore concentrated in MB-VCG-01 (terror → cry), MB-VCG-03 (anguish → petition), and MB-VCG-05 (threat → hopeless despair); MB-VCG-02 and most of MB-VCG-04 are silent on immediate personal inner response.
- *T1.6.1* — _What does this characteristic produce in the inner being over time?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The sustained effect is less uniformly evidenced than the immediate response, but several trajectories are visible. In Heb 11:7 (MB-VCG-04), sustained reception of (or faith-toward) physical deliverance produces in Noah the condition of being "an heir of righteousness by faith" — the characteristic, sustained over the long arc of obedient construction and eventual rescue, produces an established dispositional identity as a faith-heir. In Heb 5:7 (MB-VCG-03), the Son's heard petition contributes to being "perfected through suffering" — the sustained effect of anguished prayer answered is completion or maturation of obedience. In Psa 69:18 (MB-VCG-01), the sustained plea for divine nearness and ransom operates within a psalmic pattern in which repeated experience of divine rescue deepens the psalmist's reliance on divine intervention. The crucifixion mockery passages (MB-VCG-02) are silent on sustained inner effect. Act 27:20 (MB-VCG-05) shows that sustained hopelessness produces only deeper despair, not reorientation. The dominant sustained effect where rescue is received appears to be deepened faith-identity and volitional orientation toward the rescuing God.
- *T1.6.2* — _What states, qualities, capacities, or orientations does sustained exposure to or possession of this characteristic establish?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence permits identification of the following sustained states and orientations: (1) Faith-heir identity (Heb 11:7, MB-VCG-04): sustained obedient response to divinely-warned mortal threat establishes the person as an "heir of righteousness by faith" — a dispositional-identity state that transcends the original crisis. (2) Perfected/completed obedience (Heb 5:7 context, MB-VCG-03): the Son's sustained anguished petition and its resolution contribute to being "made perfect" — suggesting that sustained experience of this characteristic develops completeness of obedience. (3) Deepened relational dependence: across MB-VCG-01, the pattern of crisis-cry and response to it (Psa 69:18 within the psalmic tradition) establishes a habitual orientation of turning to God under threat — the sustained capacity for dependent petition. (4) Conversely, sustained despair (Act 27:20, MB-VCG-05) establishes a state of resigned hopelessness — a closed inner orientation that no longer reaches toward rescue. The evidence suggests that sustained exposure establishes orientations in the direction the original response was set: faith-oriented response deepens faith-identity; despair-closure deepens despair.
- *T1.6.3* — _Does the sustained effect differ from the immediate response — and if so, how?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — Yes, the sustained effect differs from the immediate response in kind and register. The immediate response is crisis-acute: terror, panic-cry, anguished petition, or hopeless despair — all are time-bound states of extremis. The sustained effect, where evidenced, moves into dispositional and identity territory that the immediate response does not. In Heb 11:7 (MB-VCG-04), the immediate response to divine warning is reverent fear and the beginning of ark construction; the sustained effect across the duration of the construction and deliverance is the establishment of Noah as "heir of righteousness by faith" — a dispositional-identity state entirely distinct from the original crisis-fear. In Heb 5:7 (MB-VCG-03), the immediate response is anguished, tearful prayer; the sustained effect (within the Heb 5 context) is being "perfected" through the suffering — a completion of obedience that is structurally different from the distress that initiated the prayer. In MB-VCG-01, the evidence for sustained effect is implicit: the disciples who cry in the storm and are rescued are the same disciples who will be formed by repeated encounter with Jesus's rescuing power, but the text does not elaborate the sustained effect within these specific verses. Act 27:20 (MB-VCG-05) shows that the sustained effect of abandoned hope is simply deeper despair — there is no positive trajectory from hopeless resignation.
- *T1.7.1* — _What inner conditions or orientations enable genuine reception of this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The verse evidence identifies several enabling inner conditions: (1) Recognition of genuine helplessness: in Mat 8:25 and Mat 14:30 (MB-VCG-01), the cry arises precisely when self-rescue is impossible — the inner being must first abandon self-sufficiency before the cry becomes genuine. (2) Relational orientation toward the rescuer: Peter's cry is "Lord, save me" — it is addressed, directional, personal; the cry is made possible by a prior relational framework in which Jesus is recognised as one who can and will respond. (3) Reverent faith: Heb 11:7 (MB-VCG-04) names "reverence" (eulabētheis) as the inner orientation that enables Noah's sustained obedient action — the characteristic is received through faith that respects and responds to divine warning. (4) Willing surrender of self-determined rescue: Joh 12:27 and Heb 5:7 (MB-VCG-03) show that the anguished petition is most fully received when the inner being yields its will to the Father's purpose — reception is not merely asking but releasing. These conditions are absent in Luk 23:39 (faithless demand) and Act 27:20 (despairing closure), which mark the boundaries of non-reception.
- *T1.7.2* — _What inner conditions block, distort, or prevent reception?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — Several blocking conditions are evidenced: (1) Absent faith / faithless demand (Luk 23:39, MB-VCG-02): the criminal who demands rescue "without repentance or faith" demonstrates that treating the rescuer as an instrument rather than as a sovereign person to whom petition is owed blocks genuine reception — the demand-posture forecloses the relational space in which rescue is received. (2) Despairing closure (Act 27:20, MB-VCG-05): when hope is abandoned entirely, the inner movement toward the rescuer ceases — despair functions as a blocking condition insofar as it extinguishes the very capacity to cry out. (3) Presumption/mockery (MB-VCG-02 generally): the mockers who taunt Jesus to save himself are not receiving the characteristic but inverting it — mockery distorts by treating rescue as a demonstration of power to be performed on demand rather than a gift to be received. (4) Self-referential orientation: the crucifixion mockery passages implicitly show that an inner orientation focused on spectacle or self-interest (the mockers' scoffing, the criminal's self-serving demand) prevents reception by keeping the inner being turned toward itself rather than toward the rescuer.
- *T1.7.3* — _What is the inner-being state of the person who encounters this characteristic but does not receive it?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The verse evidence provides two distinct profiles of non-reception: (1) Faithless demand — Luk 23:39 (MB-VCG-02): the criminal who encounters Jesus and demands rescue without repentance or faith exhibits an inner state of entitlement and self-focus. The demand "save yourself and us" treats rescue as an owed service rather than a gift of grace. The inner-being state is one of unbelief and self-referential urgency — the person is in the right situation (adjacent to the rescuer in mortal danger) but the inner orientation is inverted. (2) Despairing closure — Act 27:20 (MB-VCG-05): those aboard the ship who abandon all hope of rescue exhibit an inner state of resigned hopelessness — not actively resistant but passively closed. The inner being has ceased to reach toward rescue because it has concluded that no rescue is possible. This is non-reception through collapse rather than through refusal. In both cases, the person fails to receive the characteristic because the inner being is not positioned in dependent, relational faith-petition toward the rescuer: either it is turned defiantly toward its own demands (Luk 23:39) or it has turned inward in collapsed resignation (Act 27:20). The two modes are affectively opposite — demanding vs. despairing — but structurally identical in their closure to received rescue.
- *T1.8.1* — _What is the primary inner-being dimension of this characteristic from the programme's dimension vocabulary?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The primary inner-being dimension of this characteristic is the affective-volitional dimension: it is constitutively an experience of fear, terror, or despair (affective) that drives an act of will (the cry, petition, or surrender). The two components are inseparable in the evidence: the cry of Mat 14:30 is simultaneously an emotional registration of panic and a volitional act of faith-petition; the anguish of Heb 5:7 is simultaneously emotional distress and disciplined reverent prayer. The affective state (fear, terror, distress) is the occasion and impetus; the volitional movement (crying out, petitioning, surrendering) is the act. Neither alone constitutes the characteristic — the characteristic is the dynamic interaction between overwhelming affective state and the will's movement toward the rescuer despite and through that state. Secondary dimensions include the relational dimension (the cry is directed toward a person) and the somatic dimension (the body is physically imperilled), but these are conditions and contexts rather than the primary inner-being locus.
- *T1.8.2* — _What evidence from the verse evidence supports this classification?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The evidence supporting the affective-volitional dimension classification (established at T1.1.8) is as follows: Affective evidence: Mat 8:25 records the disciples' terror-laden cry "we are perishing" — acute affective distress is the precondition of the cry; Mat 14:30 records that Peter "saw the wind" and "was afraid" (edeilasen, the fear is explicitly named) before crying out — fear is the affective register that activates the volitional cry; Joh 12:27 has Jesus say "my soul is troubled" (tetataktai hē psychē mou) — the affective disturbance is named as the inner-being precondition of the petition "save me from this hour"; Heb 5:7 describes "loud cries and tears" (krauge kai dakrua) — affective intensity is the phenomenological marker of the prayer. Volitional evidence: in all MB-VCG-01 and MB-VCG-03 verses, the cry is an act of will — not passive collapse but active petition directed at a person; in Heb 11:7 (MB-VCG-04), the volitional element is even more prominent — Noah "moved with reverent fear" (eulabētheis) and "constructed an ark" — will expressed through sustained obedient action. The affective-volitional dual structure is thus consistently evidenced; the evidence strongly supports this as the primary dimension classification.
- *T1.8.3* — _Does this characteristic carry secondary dimensions — and if so, what are they, and do they compete with the primary classification?_
  → **[CHAR-2]** E — The primary classification is affective-volitional (established at T1.1.8 and supported at T1.2.8). Secondary dimensions present in the verse evidence are: (1) Relational dimension: the characteristic is constitutively relational — the cry is always directed toward a person (God, the Father, the Lord Jesus); the rescue requires a responding agent; even the despair of Act 27:20 (MB-VCG-05) is the relational dimension inverted (no rescuer anticipated). This secondary relational dimension is present in virtually every verse. (2) Somatic-constitutional dimension: the characteristic is triggered by physical mortal threat — the body is imperilled — and this somatic context is not incidental but constitutive; without the body's actual danger, the characteristic does not arise in this form. Heb 5:7 ("in the days of his flesh") explicitly marks the somatic dimension. (3) Volitional-obedience dimension: in Heb 11:7 and Heb 5:7 (MB-VCG-03, MB-VCG-04), the characteristic operates through disciplined obedience and surrender — this is more volitional than affective, and these verses could conceivably be classified primarily under volitional dimensions. These secondary dimensions do not compete with the primary affective-volitional classification in most verses, but in Heb 11:7 the volitional-obedience dimension is arguably as prominent as the affective dimension, creating mild internal tension in the classification. The relational dimension is pervasive but best understood as the structural form through which the affective-volitional content is expressed, not a competing primary dimension.
<!-- /EVIDENCE -->


**Key verses for Physical rescue from mortal danger:**

**Mat 14:30** (G4982 sōzō) [M38-B-VCG-01]
> Mat 14:30 But when he saw the wind , he was afraid , and beginning to sink he cried out, “ Lord , save me .”
_Meaning of the term here:_ Peter, sinking in wind and waves, cries 'Lord, save me' — physical rescue from drowning, with the inner being moving from bold venture to panic and desperate faith-cry in an instant.

**Mar 15:31** (G4982 sōzō) [M38-B-VCG-02]
> Mar 15:31 So also the chief priests with the scribes mocked him to one another , saying , “He saved others ; he cannot save himself .
_Meaning of the term here:_ The priests' mockery — 'he saved others; he cannot save himself' — ironically testifies to Jesus's prior saving acts while denying his power; sōzō oscillates between physical rescue and the deeper saving mission he will not abandon.

**Heb 5:7** (G4982 sōzō) [M38-B-VCG-03]
> Heb 5:7 In the days of his flesh , Jesus offered up prayers and supplications , with loud cries and tears , to him who was able to save him from death , and he was heard because of his reverence .
_Meaning of the term here:_ Jesus prayed with anguished cries to be saved from death and was heard; the inner being of the Son himself engages in desperate petition — rescue sought through reverence and heard by the Father.

**Heb 11:7** (G4991 soteria) [M38-B-VCG-04]
> Heb 11:7 By faith Noah , being warned by God concerning events as yet unseen , in reverent fear constructed an ark for the saving of his household . By this he condemned the world and became an heir of the righteousness that comes by faith .
_Meaning of the term here:_ Noah's faith-driven construction of the ark saved his household from physical destruction; the inner being's reverent faith produces embodied action that delivers those entrusted to him.

**Act 27:20** (G4982 sōzō) [M38-B-VCG-05]
> Act 27:20 When neither sun nor stars appeared for many days , and no small tempest lay on us, all hope of our being saved was at last abandoned .
_Meaning of the term here:_ In the context of a life-threatening storm, all hope of physical rescue was abandoned by those aboard — this is sōzō in its physical/temporal rescue sense, with the inner state being one of despair and lost hope.


### 5.3 Healing wholeness through faith exercised

     SOURCE: all evidence for this characteristic on this lens is below
