<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T4 findings for Healing wholeness through faith exercised -->

**T4 — Relational Interfaces**

- *T4.1.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating from God toward the human person — and if so, how?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence strongly supports God-to-human operation, mediated through Jesus as divine agent. In Mat 9:22 and Mar 5:34, Jesus speaks healing into existence ("your faith has made you well"), functioning as the active divine source of the sōzō event. In Mar 10:52 and Luk 18:42, Jesus restores sight to Bartimaeus and the blind man, again naming faith as the instrument but himself as the giver. Luk 8:50 is particularly explicit: Jesus commands "only believe, and she will be made well," positioning himself as the one who will accomplish the rescue from death — the human's faith opens the door, but the saving act flows from Jesus as divine agent. In Jam 5:15, the prayer of faith heals, but the Lord is implicitly the healer who raises up the sick person. In 1Pe 3:21, salvation comes through resurrection (grounded in Christ's), not human effort — God through Christ is the source. The pattern across M38-C-VCG-01, VCG-02, and VCG-03 is consistent: God in Christ is the originating agent of wholeness, and the human's faith is the receptor-channel through which divine action flows.
- *T4.1.2* — _What does the evidence reveal about the basis on which God extends this characteristic — is it conditional, unconditional, covenantal, or re…_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence reveals a primarily responsive basis: God in Christ responds to faith as the presented condition, but the response is neither mechanical nor merely transactional. In Mat 9:22, Jesus "turned" and saw the woman — the response is personal and relational before the pronouncement. In Mar 5:34, "your faith has made you well" — faith is named as the operative factor, making the extension conditional upon faith exercised. Similarly Mar 10:52 and Luk 18:42 both credit faith as the factor Jesus acknowledges. Yet in Luk 8:50 (VCG-02), Jesus does not merely respond to already-present faith but commands it: "only believe" — suggesting that God's extension of healing wholeness can precede and summon faith rather than simply reward it, introducing a covenantal-initiative dimension. Jam 5:15 adds a corporate dimension: the prayer of faith as condition for healing and forgiveness. The evidence does not support unconditional extension of healing wholeness; faith is consistently named as the operative inner condition. The basis is best characterised as responsively covenantal — God in Christ responds to faith, but also calls forth the faith to which he will respond.
- *T4.1.3* — _What does God's extension of this characteristic reveal about his disposition toward the human person?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence reveals a disposition of active willingness toward the whole person, not merely the body. In Mar 5:34, Jesus does not merely cure but sends the woman away "in peace" (eis eirēnēn) — the inner-being dimension is deliberately addressed. In Luk 8:48, the same peace-grant appears, confirming that Jesus's disposition extends to the inner state, not only the physical condition. In Luk 17:19, the Samaritan leper's faith-wholeness is distinguished from the other nine's physical cure by Jesus's explicit word — God's extension through Christ is directed toward the whole person whose inner being (faith, gratitude, return) is engaged. Bartimaeus's immediate following of Jesus (Mar 10:52) is noted by the narrative as a response to total restoration, suggesting that Jesus's healing intends discipleship-relation, not merely physical relief. Jam 5:15 confirms that God's extension encompasses forgiveness of sin alongside bodily healing, showing divine disposition toward comprehensive restoration. The cumulative picture across all three VCGs is a disposition of generous, holistic benevolence toward the whole human person — body, conscience, and relational standing before God.
- *T4.1.4* — _If the evidence is silent on God-to-human operation, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence is not silent on God-to-human operation; it is extensively evidenced across all three VCGs as established in obs_id=324, 325, and 326 above. No silence notation is required.
- *T4.2.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating in the human person's movement toward God — in seeking, supplication, worship, or…_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence shows clear human-toward-God movement across multiple registers. In Mat 9:21 (VCG-01), the woman's interior conviction — "if I touch his garment I shall be made well" — is a movement of faith directed toward Jesus as divine agent; her press through the crowd is a bodily enactment of this God-ward seeking. In Mar 5:28, the same interior conviction operates as a pre-verbal act of trust directed toward Jesus. In Mar 5:23 (VCG-02), Jairus falls at Jesus's feet with a desperate plea for his dying daughter — a posture of supplication. In Luk 8:50, the command "only believe" implies that faith is the instrument of human movement toward God in the crisis moment. In Luk 17:19, the Samaritan leper's return and prostration before Jesus is explicitly a movement of grateful worship, and Jesus distinguishes it as the act of faith that saves. In Jam 5:15 (VCG-03), intercessory prayer is the form of human movement toward God, with the prayer of faith as the vehicle through which healing and forgiveness flow. In 1Pe 3:21, baptism functions as "an appeal to God for a good conscience" — a formal covenantal movement of the inner person toward God. Human-to-God movement is thus evidenced in modes of seeking, supplication, interior conviction, intercessory prayer, and covenantal appeal.
- *T4.2.2* — _What does the evidence reveal about the inner posture required for this movement?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence reveals faith as the primary and consistently named inner posture. In Mat 9:22, Mar 5:34, Mar 10:52, Luk 8:48, Luk 17:19, and Luk 18:42, Jesus's formula "your faith has made you well" names faith as the operative inner posture without which the saving event would not occur. The character of that faith is further specified by the individual accounts: the woman of Mat 9:21 and Mar 5:28 exhibits quiet interior conviction, formed before any word is spoken; Bartimaeus (Mar 10:52, Luk 18:42) exhibits persistent, publicly expressed, socially costly faith — he cries out despite rebuke. Jairus (Mar 5:23) exhibits urgent, desperate supplication. The Samaritan leper (Luk 17:19) adds gratitude and return-worship as components of the faith-posture that distinguishes wholeness from mere cure. In Luk 8:50, "only believe" positions faith as the counter-posture to fear — the required inner orientation is trust deliberately held against dread. Jam 5:15 names the "prayer of faith" — implying confident, believing intercession rather than anxious petition. Across the evidence, the required inner posture is faith characterised by conviction, persistence, and willingness to direct the whole self toward Jesus as the source of wholeness.
- *T4.2.3* — _What does the human-to-God direction of this characteristic reveal about the person's relationship with God?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The human-to-God direction reveals that the persons involved treat Jesus as directly accessible, personally responsive, and wholly sufficient for their need — a relational posture that implies recognition of divine authority and benevolence. The woman of Mat 9:21 acts without being told to; her inner faith-speech demonstrates a relationship structured by confident expectation of Jesus's healing power. Bartimaeus's persistence despite social rebuke (Mar 10:52) reveals a relationship in which the person's confidence in Jesus exceeds social pressure and shame. The Samaritan leper's return (Luk 17:19) reveals that the healed person understands the relationship as one requiring acknowledgment and gratitude — not merely transactional but covenantal in its response. Jairus's prostrated supplication (Mar 5:23) reveals a relationship in which the person casts all other resources aside and presents themselves in utter dependence. In 1Pe 3:21, the inner appeal to God for a good conscience reveals a relationship in which the person's most intimate inner state — conscience — is held before God as the site of salvation's work. The consistent picture across M38-C is that the human-to-God movement in this characteristic expresses a relationship of transparent need, personal trust, and recognition of divine sufficiency.
- *T4.2.4* — _If the evidence is silent on human-to-God operation, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence is not silent on human-to-God operation; it is thoroughly evidenced across all three VCGs as established in obs_id=328, 329, and 330 above. No silence notation is required.
- *T4.3.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic being extended by one person toward another — and if so, how does it operate in that giving…_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence shows person-to-person extension most directly in Jam 5:15 (VCG-03): the prayer of faith exercised by elders or community members on behalf of the sick person is the mechanism by which healing and forgiveness come. Here the giving is intercessory — one person's faith-act carries saving effect for another who may not be able to exercise faith independently. In Mar 5:23 (VCG-02), Jairus's desperate faith-supplication is exercised on behalf of his daughter — a parent's faith operating as a giving act toward another's need. The broader synoptic narratives (VCG-01) are primarily individual (each person exercises their own faith), but even there the characteristic exhibits a giving dimension in that Jesus extends the confirming word — "your faith has made you well" — which is a gift of recognition and completion that seals the healing. The evidence does not show laypeople routinely giving this characteristic to one another in the synoptic material; the person-to-person giving is concentrated in intercessory prayer (Jam 5:15) and parental advocacy (Mar 5:23).
- *T4.3.2* — _What inner conditions or orientations in the giver enable genuine extension of this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — In Jam 5:15, the enabling inner condition is named explicitly: the "prayer of faith" — the giver must be operating in believing prayer, not merely ritualistic petition. The implication is that the intercessor must themselves be in right standing (Jam 5:16 context, though not in the M38-C corpus, confirms this pattern). In Mar 5:23, Jairus's inner orientation is urgency combined with earnest faith — he has exhausted other options and presents himself fully before Jesus. The enabling inner condition is total reliance: no divided trust, no alternative recourse. In both cases, the giver's inner posture mirrors the individual faith-posture evidenced in VCG-01: conviction of Jesus's sufficiency, willingness to press the need before him without holding back. The evidence does not describe cynical, detached, or rote extension of this characteristic producing genuine results; the inner condition of the giver appears to be integral to the effectiveness of the giving.
- *T4.3.4* — _If the evidence is silent on the giving direction, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence is not fully silent: Jam 5:15 and Mar 5:23 provide evidence for the giving direction as noted in obs_id=332 and 333. However, the evidence is partial and concentrated in intercessory and parental contexts rather than generalised person-to-person giving.
- *T4.4.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic being received by a person from another — and if so, how does it operate in that reception?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence shows reception primarily as Jesus-to-individual, but within the M38-C corpus there is evidence of person-mediated reception in Jam 5:15: the sick person receives healing and forgiveness through the prayer of faith offered by others, not solely through their own individual faith. In the synoptic narratives, the reception is direct and immediate — the moment Jesus pronounces "your faith has made you well," the sōzō is received. Mar 5:34 describes the woman being told to "go in peace" — the reception is sealed by Jesus's word. Mar 10:52 notes that Bartimaeus received his sight "immediately" — the reception is instantaneous upon Jesus's confirming word. In Mar 5:23 (VCG-02), the daughter is the recipient of a healing sought by her father — she receives without herself exercising faith in the narrative as described. Reception of this characteristic, when mediated through another's faith (Jam 5:15, Mar 5:23), operates as an overflow of the other's trust toward the recipient's need.
- *T4.4.2* — _What inner conditions enable or block reception of this characteristic from another person?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — Where the recipient is themselves the faith-exerciser (Mat 9:21-22, Mar 5:28, 34, Mar 10:52, Luk 8:48, Luk 17:19, Luk 18:42), the enabling condition is the interior conviction and active trust already evidenced in T4.2.2 above. In Luk 8:50 (VCG-02), fear is explicitly identified as the blocking condition: Jesus says "do not fear, only believe" — fear and faith are set as mutually exclusive inner postures, with fear blocking and faith enabling reception. In the case of Jairus's daughter (Mar 5:23, Luk 8:50), the recipient is passive (dying or dead), which suggests that when the recipient cannot exercise faith personally, another's faith can open the door to reception. The evidence does not describe an active inner blocking posture in M38-C beyond the fear identified in Luk 8:50.
- *T4.4.4* — _If the evidence is silent on the receiving direction, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence is not silent on reception; it is well-evidenced across VCG-01 and VCG-02 and partially in VCG-03 as noted in obs_id=336 and 337. No silence notation is required.
- *T4.5.1* — _Does the evidence indicate whether this characteristic operates differently within existing relational bonds versus across relational distan…_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence includes at least one explicit example of this characteristic operating across covenantal and ethnic distance. In Luk 17:19, the returning leper is a Samaritan — outside the Jewish covenant — and Jesus singles him out as the one whose faith brought the full sōzō, not merely physical cure. The nine (presumably Jewish) lepers received the physical cure but did not receive the explicit sōzō-with-faith-ascription. This suggests the characteristic operates regardless of, and perhaps conspicuously across, relational and covenantal distance. Within existing bonds: Jairus (Mar 5:23) is a synagogue ruler, within the covenant community, and his faith operates effectively. The woman of Mat 9:21-22 and Mar 5:28, 34 is within the covenant community. Bartimaeus (Mar 10:52, Luk 18:42) is a Jewish beggar, also within the community. The evidence does not show the characteristic operating less effectively across relational difference; if anything, the Samaritan leper example (Luk 17:19) suggests a heightened commendation of faith exercised from outside the expected relational bond.
- *T4.5.2* — _Does this characteristic operate within covenantal contexts only, or does it cross covenantal boundaries?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The Luk 17:19 evidence is definitive: the Samaritan leper is outside the Jewish covenant and receives the fullest expression of this characteristic — Jesus explicitly attributes sōzō to his faith. This establishes that the characteristic crosses covenantal boundaries. The woman with the haemorrhage (Mar 5:28-34) and Bartimaeus (Mar 10:52) are within the covenant, confirming that the characteristic also operates within covenantal contexts. Jam 5:15 is addressed to Christian communities (a new covenant context), confirming covenantal operation. 1Pe 3:21 is explicitly baptismal — a covenantal entry act. The evidence thus shows this characteristic operating both within and across covenantal boundaries, with faith as the operative factor rather than covenantal standing.
- *T4.5.3* — _What does the evidence reveal about the relational scope of this characteristic — who is included and who is not?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence suggests inclusion is determined by faith exercised rather than by identity, status, or relational proximity. The included in the M38-C corpus are: a socially marginalised woman (haemorrhage, ritual uncleanness — Mat 9:21-22, Mar 5:28-34), a synagogue ruler (Jairus — Mar 5:23), a blind roadside beggar (Bartimaeus — Mar 10:52, Luk 18:42), a Samaritan leper (Luk 17:19), a dying child (Luk 8:50 by extension), sick members of a faith community (Jam 5:15), and those undergoing baptism (1Pe 3:21). The range is deliberately diverse: gender, class, ethnicity, health status, and social standing vary across the corpus. No exclusion is stated or implied in this characteristic's evidence on grounds of identity. The operative factor for inclusion is consistently faith as the inner-being activating condition.
- *T4.5.4* — _If the evidence is silent on relational boundaries, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence is not silent on relational boundaries; it addresses them through the Samaritan leper example and the diversity of recipients as noted in obs_id=340, 341, and 342. No silence notation is required.

**T4 — Relational Interfaces**

- *T4.4.3* — _What is the inner-being state of the person who encounters this characteristic from another but does not receive it?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** S — The M38-C evidence block does not present a case where a person encounters this characteristic and explicitly fails to receive it. The ten lepers in Luk 17:11-19 receive physical healing, but only the Samaritan returns — the nine do not encounter the characteristic and refuse it, they simply do not return for the deeper sōzō (Luk 17:19). This is suggestive but does not provide a direct description of the inner-being state of the non-receiver. No verse in VCG-01, VCG-02, or VCG-03 explicitly describes the inner state of non-reception within this characteristic's corpus.
- *T4.6.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating in relation to other spiritual beings — angelic or adversarial — and if so, how?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** S — No verse in M38-C-VCG-01, VCG-02, or VCG-03 references angelic beings or adversarial spiritual powers in connection with the operation of this characteristic. The synoptic healing narratives in this sub-group are not exorcism accounts and do not identify demonic opposition. Jam 5:15 and 1Pe 3:21 likewise make no reference to spiritual beings other than God/Christ. The spiritual beings interface is not evidenced in this characteristic's corpus.
- *T4.6.2* — _Is this characteristic a site of adversarial activity — something that can be attacked, distorted, or weaponised by adversarial spiritual po…_
  → **[CHAR-3]** S — No evidence in M38-C addresses adversarial spiritual activity directed against healing-wholeness-through-faith-exercised. No verse in VCG-01, VCG-02, or VCG-03 mentions satanic or demonic opposition to the faith that activates sōzō in this characteristic. This is a genuine silence in the M38-C evidence block.
- *T4.6.3* — _Is this characteristic communicated, strengthened, or mediated through angelic ministry in the evidence?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** S — No verse in M38-C references angelic mediation in the operation of healing-wholeness-through-faith-exercised. The mediation in all cases is direct — Jesus to the individual, or intercessory prayer (Jam 5:15) as a human-divine channel. Angelic ministry is entirely absent from this characteristic's evidence block.
- *T4.6.4* — _If the evidence is silent on the spiritual beings interface, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-3]** S — The evidence is silent on the spiritual beings interface across all three dimensions (angelic ministry, adversarial attack, and spiritual mediation). This silence is explicit and confirmed by review of all 12 IB verses in M38-C. The characteristic as evidenced operates exclusively in the divine-human and human-human dimensions without reference to spiritual beings.

<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T5 findings for Healing wholeness through faith exercised -->

**T5 — Formative and Developmental Dimension**

- *T5.1.1* — _Does this characteristic produce transformation in the person — and if so, does it change the person's condition, the person's orientation t…_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence clearly shows transformation of both condition and orientation. Regarding condition: in Mat 9:22, Mar 5:34, and Luk 8:48, the woman's physical condition is changed — her haemorrhage ceases. In Mar 10:52 and Luk 18:42, Bartimaeus's sight is restored. In Luk 17:19, the Samaritan leper's skin is healed. In Luk 8:50, a dead girl lives. In Jam 5:15, the sick are raised up and sins forgiven — both physical and conscience conditions are transformed. Regarding orientation: the peace granted in Mar 5:34 and Luk 8:48 ("go in peace") names an inner-being orientation transformation — the woman is no longer in the posture of desperate seeking but of settled wholeness. Bartimaeus's immediate following of Jesus (Mar 10:52) names an orientation transformation — from roadside beggar to disciple. The Samaritan leper's return and prostration (Luk 17:19) names a transformation from recipient to worshipper. In 1Pe 3:21, the appeal for a good conscience through baptism transforms the person's inner standing before God — orientation is fundamentally reset. The evidence supports transformation of both condition and orientation as characteristic outcomes.
- *T5.1.2* — _Is the transformation produced by this characteristic reversible or irreversible in the verse evidence?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence presents the transformation as irreversible in its basic register. The synoptic healing formulas ("your faith has made you well," "go in peace") use aorist or perfect constructions that describe completed states; no narrative in M38-C describes a healed person relapsing or the sōzō-event being undone. The peace granted (Mar 5:34, Luk 8:48) and the following of Jesus (Mar 10:52) present ongoing transformed orientations. In Jam 5:15, the forgiveness of sins (if there are any) is presented as a settled result of the prayer of faith, not a provisional one. In 1Pe 3:21, the appeal for a good conscience through resurrection is grounded in the permanence of Christ's resurrection — the basis itself is irreversible. The evidence does not address whether the physical healing could in principle be undone; it addresses the sōzō-event as a whole, which includes spiritual transformation, and presents that as a complete and non-provisional gift. Irreversibility is the functional posture of the evidence, though the M38-C corpus does not explicitly argue this point.
- *T5.1.3* — _If the evidence is silent on transformation, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence is not silent on transformation; it is central to the characteristic's operation as established in obs_id=348 and 349 above. No silence notation is required.
- *T5.2.1* — _Does the verse evidence describe a sequence of inner states through which this characteristic moves the person — a before, during, and after…_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence provides a discernible sequence, most fully articulated in the VCG-01 narratives. Before: Mat 9:21 and Mar 5:28 describe the woman's pre-touch interior conviction — she is in a state of suffering, social exclusion, and exhausted resources, yet forming active inner faith-speech ("if I touch his garment I shall be made well"). This is the before-state: need + active interior faith forming. During: the act of pressing through the crowd and touching, or crying out (Bartimaeus, Mar 10:52, Luk 18:42), represents the moment of faith externalised — the inner conviction becomes enacted. In Mar 5:33 (adjacent context), the woman's fear and trembling as she falls before Jesus captures a during-state of exposed vulnerability. After: Mar 5:34 and Luk 8:48 name the after-state explicitly — peace (eirēnē) and wholeness (sōzō complete). Bartimaeus moves from blindness to sight to following (Mar 10:52) — a three-stage after sequence. In Luk 17:19, the Samaritan moves from healed leper to returning worshipper to recipient of full sōzō. Jam 5:15 (VCG-03) implies a sequence: sick and possibly sinful → prayed over in faith → healed and forgiven. 1Pe 3:21: guilty conscience → appeal through baptism → good conscience before God.
- *T5.2.2* — _What are those states, and what does the sequence reveal about how this characteristic works?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — Drawing on the full VCG evidence, the sequence is: (1) Acute need registered in the inner being — physical suffering, mortal threat, or burdened conscience; (2) Faith formed as interior conviction directed toward Jesus as the source of wholeness — this is the characteristic's distinctive inner-being event, occurring before any outward confirmation; (3) Faith externalised in action — pressing through crowds, crying out, falling at Jesus's feet, intercessory prayer, baptismal appeal; (4) Divine response — Jesus acts, speaks, or the prayer of faith achieves its result; (5) Reception and transformation — body healed, conscience cleared, peace received, wholeness given; (6) Reorientation — peace, following of Jesus, return in worship, or ongoing appeal grounded in resurrection. The sequence reveals that this characteristic works through the inner being as the generative site: faith does not merely accompany an external event but precedes and activates it. The transformation is not imposed from outside onto a passive recipient but emerges through the person's own interior movement of trust. The sequence also reveals that wholeness (sōzō) is the terminus of the sequence, not merely health — indicating that the characteristic aims at a state of integrated being rather than symptom relief.
- *T5.2.3* — _If the evidence is silent on sequence, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence is not silent on sequence; it is substantially evidenced across VCG-01, VCG-02, and VCG-03 as established in obs_id=351 and 352 above. No silence notation is required.
- *T5.3.1* — _What mechanism does this characteristic use to produce change in the person — discipline, encounter, gradual formation, sudden transformatio…_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The dominant mechanism in the M38-C evidence is encounter-mediated sudden transformation. In Mat 9:22 and Mar 5:34, the healing is immediate upon Jesus's word; in Mar 10:52, Bartimaeus receives sight "immediately" (euthys); in Luk 8:48 and Luk 17:19, the transformations are reported as completed events rather than processes. Luk 8:50 situates the mechanism as crisis-faith encounter — the command "only believe" activates the transformation in the context of a death situation. There is no evidence in M38-C of gradual formation or disciplinary process as the operative mechanism for the sōzō event itself. However, the aftermath of Bartimaeus's healing (Mar 10:52: "followed him on the way") suggests that the sudden encounter opens into an ongoing formation trajectory, though this is not the mechanism of the initial transformation. In Jam 5:15 (VCG-03), the mechanism is intercessory prayer — a deliberate communal faith-act — which also appears to produce the result without a described gradual process. In 1Pe 3:21, the mechanism is the baptismal appeal, a ritual act of conscience-direction toward God grounded in Christ's resurrection, which is a specific covenantal encounter rather than gradual formation.
- *T5.3.2* — _Does the evidence distinguish between mechanisms in different contexts?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence does distinguish, though subtly. In VCG-01, the mechanism is personal faith-encounter with Jesus, expressed in interior conviction and physical approach — individual and direct. In VCG-02, the mechanism shifts to faith held against fear in a crisis — the inner posture required is active displacement of dread by trust, which is a different psychological mechanism from the quiet interior conviction of VCG-01. In VCG-03, two distinct mechanisms appear: (a) in Jam 5:15, communal intercessory faith-prayer — mediated through others on behalf of the sick person, implicating a relational-ecclesial mechanism; (b) in 1Pe 3:21, the baptismal appeal — a formal covenantal act of conscience-directed petition, invoking resurrection as the ground of change. The mechanisms thus vary by context: individual quiet conviction (VCG-01), crisis faith-counter-to-fear (VCG-02), communal intercession (Jam 5:15), and covenantal conscience-appeal (1Pe 3:21). The common element across all is faith as the activating inner faculty, but the form and context of its exercise differ.
- *T5.3.3* — _If the evidence is silent on mechanism, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence is not silent on mechanism; it is evidenced with contextual differentiation across all three VCGs as established in obs_id=354 and 355 above. No silence notation is required.
- *T5.4.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating in relation to suffering or affliction — as a response to it, a product of it, or…_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — Suffering and affliction are the consistent presenting context across the entire M38-C corpus. In Mat 9:21 and Mar 5:28, the woman with the haemorrhage has suffered for twelve years (Mar 5:25-26, contextual); her suffering is the context within which faith forms and is exercised. In Mar 5:23 (VCG-02), Jairus's daughter is at the point of death — mortal affliction is the precise context of the faith-exercise. In Luk 8:50, fear accompanies news of the daughter's death — suffering and grief are the setting for the command to believe. In Mar 10:52 and Luk 18:42, blindness is the long-term affliction within which Bartimaeus exercises faith. In Luk 17:19, leprosy — with all its social and physical suffering — is the affliction context. In Jam 5:15, sickness (asthenōn) is the presenting condition addressed by the prayer of faith. In 1Pe 3:21, the guilty conscience is the affliction — an inner suffering — that the appeal in baptism addresses. This characteristic is not operative in the absence of need; it is precisely the condition of suffering or affliction that activates the faith-exercise and makes the sōzō event possible. Suffering is the context and the occasion; faith is the response; wholeness is the result.
- *T5.4.2* — _Does suffering deepen, test, reveal, or produce this characteristic in the person?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence suggests that suffering reveals and occasions this characteristic rather than explicitly deepening or producing it. In Mat 9:21 and Mar 5:28, the woman's twelve years of suffering have not produced faith by a described formation process; rather, the account presents her faith as already formed and operative when she encounters Jesus — the suffering is the occasion, not the pedagogy. In Mar 5:23, Jairus's desperate faith emerges at the extreme of affliction (daughter dying), suggesting that the acuteness of need functions to strip away intermediate resources and direct the person wholly toward Jesus — suffering as the clearing agent that reveals faith's necessity. Bartimaeus (Mar 10:52) has lived with blindness long enough that his cry is urgent and socially unashamed — his chronic affliction may have formed in him the persistence that characterises his faith. In Luk 8:50, the arrival of death-news sharpens the moment of faith-test acutely. The evidence does not articulate a theological reflection on suffering as sanctifying for this characteristic (as Romans 5 or James 1 might); it presents suffering as the revelatory context in which faith is exercised and recognised.
- *T5.4.3* — _If the evidence is silent on the relationship to suffering, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence is not silent; suffering and affliction are present in every verse-account in M38-C as established in obs_id=357 and 358. No silence notation is required.
- *T5.5.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic participating in the longer arc of character formation and sanctification — shaping the per…_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence provides partial indication of a longer formation arc, most clearly in Mar 10:52 where Bartimaeus "followed him on the way" immediately after receiving sight — the healing-wholeness event opens into an ongoing discipleship trajectory. This is the strongest indication in M38-C that the characteristic's transformation does not end at the moment of sōzō but initiates a continuing formation in following Jesus. In Luk 17:19, the Samaritan's return and worship suggests that the full sōzō (as distinguished from the nine's physical cure) inaugurates a posture of grateful worshipful relationship with God — an ongoing orientation rather than a one-time event. In 1Pe 3:21, the appeal for a good conscience is grounded in resurrection — a present state that sustains ongoing relationship with God, not merely a moment of entry. Jam 5:15 includes forgiveness of sins as a concurrent dimension of the healing-sōzō event, suggesting that the whole-person transformation touches the conscience in ways that bear on ongoing moral and spiritual formation. However, the M38-C corpus does not develop an extended theology of formation through this characteristic; the indications are present but not elaborated.
- *T5.5.2* — _What does the evidence reveal about the role of this characteristic in that longer arc?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence reveals that this characteristic functions as a threshold event in the formation arc rather than as an extended developmental process. The sōzō event in the synoptic accounts is a decisive moment that reorients the whole person: Bartimaeus's following (Mar 10:52), the Samaritan's return (Luk 17:19), and the peace-receipt (Mar 5:34, Luk 8:48) all name new orientations that imply ongoing trajectories. The characteristic's role in the longer arc is thus as an initiating rupture — a point at which the inner being is wholly reconstituted through faith and wholeness received — from which formation continues. The faith exercised at the threshold also models the posture that will characterise the ongoing formation journey: persistent, whole-person trust in Jesus as the source of sōzō. Jam 5:15 and 1Pe 3:21 suggest that the conscience-cleansing dimension of this characteristic is specifically important for sanctification — the person's inner standing before God is restored, providing the foundation from which holy living proceeds. The evidence presents the characteristic as formation-enabling but does not itself describe the subsequent formation process.
- *T5.5.3* — _If the evidence is silent on formation and sanctification, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence is not entirely silent but is only partially evidenced, as noted in obs_id=360 and 361 above. The formation arc is implied by post-healing discipleship (Mar 10:52) and conscience-cleansing (Jam 5:15, 1Pe 3:21) but is not the primary focus of M38-C's verse evidence.
- *T5.6.1* — _Does the verse evidence point this characteristic toward an eschatological fullness — a future state toward which its present operation is o…_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence provides one clear eschatological grounding: 1Pe 3:21 explicitly connects the present sōzō-appeal (baptism as conscience-appeal to God) with Christ's resurrection, which in Petrine theology is the first-fruits of the general resurrection and eschatological renewal. The appeal for a good conscience "through the resurrection of Jesus Christ" situates the present inner-being transformation within an eschatological frame — the resurrection that grounds present salvation is also the guarantee of future fullness. The synoptic accounts (VCG-01, VCG-02) do not explicitly articulate an eschatological trajectory for the healing events they describe; the healings are presented as in-breaking of the Kingdom of God (consistent with their broader Synoptic context) but this is not explicitly stated within the M38-C verses themselves. Jam 5:15's sōzō (healing and forgiveness) similarly does not explicitly project an eschatological horizon, though forgiveness of sins has eschatological consequences in the wider James context. The eschatological trajectory in M38-C is partially evidenced in 1Pe 3:21 and implied by the Kingdom context of the synoptic healings, but is not the primary register of the characteristic's verse evidence.
- *T5.6.2* — _What does the present experience of this characteristic anticipate about its future fullness?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — From 1Pe 3:21, the present experience of a good conscience before God through the resurrection-grounded baptismal appeal anticipates a final standing before God in which conscience is fully vindicated — the eschatological judgment rendered favourably because the appeal has already been made and received. The synoptic healings, read within their Kingdom-of-God context (though not explicitly in the M38-C verses), anticipate a final wholeness in which no sickness, blindness, or social exclusion remains — the healings are proleptic enactments of eschatological sōzō. Bartimaeus's following on "the way" (Mar 10:52) in Markan theology carries eschatological overtone — the way of Jesus leads to the cross and resurrection, into which the disciple is drawn. The present experience of faith-activated wholeness thus anticipates an eschatological state in which the physico-spiritual integration achieved through these encounters is fully and permanently realised, with faith giving way to sight (cf. the restoration of Bartimaeus's sight as a figure of this) and peace fully received. These eschatological implications are inferred from the evidence's register rather than explicitly stated within the M38-C verse corpus.
- *T5.6.3* — _If the evidence is silent on eschatological trajectory, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The evidence is not entirely silent: 1Pe 3:21 provides explicit eschatological grounding as noted above, and the synoptic healing accounts carry implicit Kingdom-of-God eschatological resonance. The trajectory is partially evidenced but not the dominant register of M38-C's corpus. No full silence notation is required, but the evidence is acknowledged as partial.
<!-- /EVIDENCE -->

<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T5 silences for Healing wholeness through faith exercised -->

**T5 — Formative and Developmental Dimension**

- *T5.7.2* — _Does the evidence indicate generational consequence — a deposit carried forward beyond the individual?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** S — No verse in M38-C-VCG-01, VCG-02, or VCG-03 addresses generational transmission of healing-wholeness-through-faith-exercised. The characteristic as evidenced is individual and immediate (synoptic accounts) or communal but not generational (Jam 5:15, 1Pe 3:21). No generational consequence is evidenced in the M38-C corpus.
<!-- /EVIDENCE -->

<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T1 findings for Healing wholeness through faith exercised -->

**T1 — Definition**

- *T1.1.1* — _What is this characteristic called in the programme — and what does the name itself signal about its essential nature?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Healing wholeness through faith exercised." The name signals several structural commitments simultaneously. "Healing" locates the primary presenting register: the inner being is in a condition requiring restoration, not merely improvement. "Wholeness" signals that the outcome is integrated and total — not partial cure but a convergence of physical and spiritual restoration, which is the sōzō semantic field's distinctive contribution. "Through" indicates instrumentality and directional movement: the characteristic is not a state received passively but a result achieved by means of something. "Faith exercised" is the instrument named — not faith as disposition only, but faith in active, operative mode; the participle "exercised" marks intentional, directional inner-being action. The name as a whole therefore signals: this is an active inner-being phenomenon (not passive), its activity is faith directed toward an object (God/Christ), and its goal is the bridging of physical and spiritual need in a single integrated rescue event. The word "exercised" is the name's most decisive signal — it distinguishes this characteristic from faith as settled doctrinal assent and positions it as a dynamic, moment-bound inner act that produces ontological change.
- *T1.1.2* — _What do the primary Hebrew and Greek terms reveal at the definitional level — before deeper lexical analysis begins?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The characteristic's primary Greek term is sōzō (G4982), with the inner-faculty term being pistis/pisteuō (faith/believe) as the instrument named by Jesus in the healing declarations. At the definitional level, sōzō carries a semantic range that refuses separation of physical and spiritual rescue — it can denote rescue from physical danger, healing of bodily disease, and soteriological salvation, with these senses intersecting rather than being mutually exclusive. The programme's researcher direction explicitly notes a "sōzō sense-split" — this sub-group is held at the intersection where healing and saving are inseparable, which is sōzō's own semantic structure. Pistis/pisteuō at the definitional level denotes trust, reliance, and commitment directed toward an object — it is inherently relational and directional, presupposing both a truster and a trusted. The combination of sōzō and pistis in the standard Synoptic formula ("your faith has sōzō'd you") therefore produces at the definitional level: trust-directed-toward-God/Christ as the inner act that releases integrated rescue (physical-plus-spiritual). The Hebrew background of shālōm (peace, wholeness, completeness) — evoked by Jesus's "go in peace" in Mar 5:34 and Luk 8:48 — adds the dimension that the result of sōzō-through-pistis is wholeness as restored-right-order, not merely absence of disease.
- *T1.1.3* — _Does the name carry directional, relational, or constitutional implications that orient the enquiry from the outset?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The name "Healing wholeness through faith exercised" carries all three implications noted in the prompt. (1) Directional: "through" establishes a vector — the characteristic moves from a starting condition (brokenness, disease, fear, troubled conscience) through faith as the mechanism toward wholeness. The name is not static but describes a trajectory with an originating point, a mediating act, and a destination. (2) Relational: "faith exercised" is inherently relational — faith has an object; it is directed toward someone. Though the name does not name the object (Christ), the verse evidence makes clear that this faith is always exercised toward Jesus as the source of sōzō. The name's faith-exercised language presupposes a truster-trusted relationship. (3) Constitutional: "wholeness" — not "cure" or "recovery" — signals that the result affects the person's constitution, not merely their somatic condition. Wholeness is an integrated ontological state. "Healing" paired with "wholeness" prevents reduction to merely spiritual salvation (the characteristic does include physical cure) while "wholeness" prevents reduction to merely physical cure. The name therefore orients the enquiry from the outset toward a trajectory that is directional (movement through faith), relational (faith toward an object-person), and constitutional (the result is integrated personhood), all three dimensions being necessary for a correct understanding of what the characteristic is and does.
- *T1.2.1* — _What kind of inner-being phenomenon does this characteristic appear to be — an act, a disposition, a condition, a quality, or something else…_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The verse evidence positions this characteristic primarily as an act — a specific, directional inner-being movement — rather than a stable disposition or quality. The woman's pre-touch interior monologue in Mat 9:21 and Mar 5:28 ("if I touch his garment I will be made well") is a dateable inner event with a before and after, not a background disposition. Bartimaeus's persistent cry in Mar 10:52, the father's plea in Mar 5:23, and the Samaritan's return in Luk 17:19 all exhibit discrete, directional acts. However, the characteristic is not pure momentary act either — the woman presses through a crowd, suggesting sustained inner-being commitment over time. Jam 5:15 extends this to communal intercessory practice, which implies a more sustained mode. The most accurate classification is: faith exercised is an act with dispositional depth — a moment-bound inner-being movement that is possible because of an underlying trust-orientation, but which is itself distinct from that underlying orientation. It is the actualization of faith in a specific movement toward God for specific wholeness. In programme vocabulary, it is best described as an inner-being act arising from and expressing a deeper dispositional trust.
- *T1.2.2* — _Is this characteristic simple in structure or does it appear to have constituent elements at first encounter?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The characteristic is composite at first encounter, not simple. Three constituent elements are immediately visible in the verse evidence. (1) An inner conviction or trust-forming act: the woman's interior monologue (Mat 9:21; Mar 5:28) shows that faith must be formed as a specific directed conviction before it becomes operative — this is an epistemic-volitional element. (2) An outward expression or movement: touching the garment, crying out persistently (Bartimaeus, Mar 10:52), returning to give thanks (Luk 17:19), praying for the sick (Jam 5:15) — the inner conviction must pass through some expression or directional act; faith exercised is not purely interior but moves outward. (3) A reception and confirmation event: Jesus's naming of faith as the cause ("your faith has made you well") and the bestowing of peace or sight or cleansed conscience — the characteristic is only complete when the inner act has reached its object and been received as sōzō. These three elements — formation, expression, reception — are distinguishable but not separable in the evidence. A fourth element is present in M38-C-VCG-03: intercessory extension, where faith operates not only for the self but on behalf of another (Jam 5:15). The characteristic is therefore structurally composite with at least three to four constituent elements.
- *T1.2.3* — _What is the current best working description of this characteristic — encapsulating its constitutional location, the faculties it primarily …_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — Best working description, drawn from the cumulative verse evidence: This characteristic is a composite inner-being act — constitutionally located at the intersection of will, trust, and conscience — by which a person in a condition of physical or spiritual fragmentation forms an explicit, directed interior conviction of reliance upon Christ for integrated wholeness, and through that inner act receives a sōzō event that bridges bodily healing and spiritual restoration. The faculties primarily engaged are: (a) the volitional faculty (the act of believing/trusting is an act of the will directed toward Christ); (b) the cognitive-formative faculty (an interior conviction is formed, sometimes as explicit inner speech as in Mat 9:21); (c) the conscience (as the organ of moral-spiritual standing before God, engaged particularly in Jam 5:15 and 1Pe 3:21). The impact on the person operates at three levels: (a) immediate — peace, sight, conscience-cleared, or gratitude-return; (b) sustained — reoriented discipleship, conscience-integrity, capacity for intercessory faith; (c) constitutional — the person is repositioned from fragmented self-sufficiency or fear toward integrated wholeness-in-relation-to-Christ. The characteristic is not a passive condition but an active, crisis-precipitated or Spirit-prompted act that, when complete, leaves the person constitutionally altered.
- *T1.3.1* — _What is the structural opposite of this characteristic — the inner-being reality that stands against or excludes it?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The corpus directly names the structural opposite: fear. In Luk 8:50, Jesus sets "do not fear" against "only believe" in an explicit antithetical parallelism — the inner being must displace fear in order to exercise faith toward wholeness. Fear is therefore not merely an accompanying affect but the structural opposite that this characteristic must overcome or displace. The father's situation in Mar 5:23 (urgent, death-facing plea) also operates in the fear-faith opposition: the urgency of his plea names the mortal dread that faith-exercised moves against. A secondary structural opposite is evidenced in the nine lepers of Luk 17:17-19: their failure to return suggests that self-sufficient continuation — taking healing without returning in faith — is also an opposing inner posture, though this is less explicitly marked than fear. The primary structural opposite is therefore fear (phobos) as the inner-being reality of self-protective withdrawal in the face of mortal threat, which directly excludes the outward movement of trust toward God that this characteristic requires.
- *T1.3.2* — _What does this characteristic explicitly exclude or resist?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The characteristic explicitly excludes or resists the following, drawn from direct verse evidence. (1) Fear as governing inner state: Luk 8:50 sets "do not fear" as the direct prerequisite for "only believe" — faith exercised resists fear's governance of the inner being. (2) External-only or ritual-only cleansing: 1Pe 3:21 explicitly states that sōzō through baptism is not the removal of dirt from the body but an appeal of conscience — the characteristic resists reduction to external rite; the inner faculty is the real locus. (3) Gratitude-deferred or ungrateful reception: the nine lepers in Luk 17:17-19 (implied contrast with v.19) suggest that receiving physical cure without the returning faith-exercise of acknowledgment is excluded from full sōzō — the characteristic resists being dissolved into bare physical benefit without relational response. (4) Self-sufficiency as alternative to faith: the act of forming interior conviction directed toward Christ as the source of wholeness excludes self-reliance as the operative inner posture — one cannot simultaneously rely on oneself and exercise faith toward Christ for the same wholeness. These exclusions collectively define the boundary: the characteristic ends where fear, external-only ritual, ungrateful passivity, or self-sufficiency begin.
- *T1.3.3* — _What is this characteristic not — where does it end and something else begin?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — Boundary definition from the verse evidence. (1) This characteristic is not soteriological salvation as standalone M38-A or M38-B event: the programme's researcher direction (sōzō sense-split) explicitly holds this sub-group distinct from general salvation (M38-A) and physical-only rescue (M38-B). Where sōzō denotes spiritual salvation without explicit physical healing context, or physical rescue without faith-as-named-activator, it belongs to a different sub-group. (2) It is not faith as general disposition or background virtue: this characteristic is faith exercised — activated, operative, directional — not faith as settled doctrinal orientation or character trait. Where faith names a person's general orientation to God without the specific movement toward wholeness, it is a different phenomenon. (3) It is not medical healing or natural recovery: the characteristic requires sōzō (not iaomai or therapeuō alone) with faith explicitly named as activating cause; healing that occurs without this faith-attribution does not belong to this characteristic. (4) It is not ritual as external act: 1Pe 3:21 explicitly marks the boundary — baptismal water as external physical cleansing does not belong to this characteristic; it is the interior conscience-appeal that constitutes the sōzō-event. (5) It is not fear-motivated superstition: the woman's touch is not magical thinking but formed interior conviction directed toward a specific person (Jesus), which is the structural difference between faith-exercised and mere ritual contact.
- *T1.4.1* — _In what distinct ways does this characteristic operate within the inner person?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The verse evidence reveals at least three distinct modes of operation. (1) Interior conviction mode (M38-C-VCG-01): the characteristic operates as pre-verbal, pre-contact inner monologue or formed conviction — the woman in Mat 9:21 and Mar 5:28 forms the conviction inwardly before touching, and this interior act is itself causally efficacious; Jesus confirms it was operative ("your faith has made you well"). (2) Crisis-displacement mode (M38-C-VCG-02): the characteristic operates as active counter-movement against mortal fear — in Luk 8:50 and Mar 5:23, faith is not quiet conviction but urgent, desperate exercise that displaces fear as the governing inner state; the faith-act is precipitated by extreme threat and operates through intensity rather than composure. (3) Intercessory/conscience mode (M38-C-VCG-03): the characteristic operates through the conscience as primary organ (1Pe 3:21 — "appeal to God for a good conscience") and through intercessory faith on behalf of another (Jam 5:15 — "the prayer of faith will save the sick"). In this mode, the characteristic's operation is not self-directed but other-directed, and the restoration is explicitly conscience-level rather than somatic only. These three modes are genuinely distinct in their presenting dynamics, though all three share the structural feature of faith as the inner-being activating movement toward God.
- *T1.4.2* — _Does this characteristic operate differently depending on context, direction, or constitutional level?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — Yes, the evidence supports contextual and directional variation in operation, as distinct from the three structural modes identified in T1.4.1. (1) Self-directed vs. other-directed: In M38-C-VCG-01 and VCG-02, faith is exercised by the healing-seeker for their own wholeness or by a parent for their child (Mar 5:23). In M38-C-VCG-03 (Jam 5:15), faith operates intercessorily — the elders' prayer of faith operates on behalf of a sick person who may themselves be passive. The inner-being engagement differs: self-directed faith involves the person's own volitional-trust act; intercessory faith involves the inner-being of a third party acting as conduit. (2) Crisis-intensity vs. composure-level: VCG-02's crisis-displacement mode (Luk 8:50; Mar 5:23) operates at higher intensity than VCG-01's quiet interior conviction mode (Mat 9:21; Mar 5:28). The constitutional level at which faith engages the inner being appears to shift with urgency — both are genuine faith-exercises but with different phenomenological registers. (3) Bodily-healing register vs. conscience-register: The Synoptic healing narratives operate primarily in the somatic register that carries spiritual overtone, while 1Pe 3:21 operates primarily in the conscience register with the somatic (baptism) as outward correlate. The characteristic therefore differentiates in operation across these contextual-constitutional dimensions while retaining the same structural core.
- *T1.4.3* — _Does this characteristic have a communicative or speech-based mode of operation — and if so, how does it function?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — Yes, the verse evidence documents a speech-based mode, though it operates at two distinct levels. (1) Inner speech mode: Mat 9:21 and Mar 5:28 show the woman's faith operating as interior monologue — "if I touch his garment I will be made well" — which is a verbally-formed interior conviction, not merely affective hope. The inner speech structures the faith-act, giving it propositional content and directional commitment. This inner speech is causally prior to the outward act and is what Jesus names ("your faith has made you well") — the faith was operative as formed inner speech before any touch. (2) Outer cry mode: Bartimaeus in Mar 10:52 cries out verbally — "Jesus, Son of David, have mercy on me" (v.47-48, preceding v.52) — as the faith-speech that occasions the healing. The outer cry is the external expression of inner faith, and Jesus responds to it as the indicator of faith. The communicative function in both modes is the same: speech (inner or outer) articulates the conviction, identifies the object of trust, and thereby enacts the faith-exercise. Speech is not decorative but constitutive of this mode — the forming of the words (inner or outer) is the formation of the faith-act. Jam 5:15 adds a third speech mode: the communal prayer of faith spoken by elders over the sick, where spoken intercession is the operative vehicle of healing-through-faith.
- *T1.5.1* — _What is the first or most immediate inner-being response to receiving or encountering this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The corpus evidences two distinct immediate responses depending on the mode of encounter. (1) Where faith has been exercised and sōzō received, the immediate inner response is peace: Mar 5:34 and Luk 8:48 both close with Jesus granting the woman "peace" (eirēnē) — this is the direct inner-being marker of what the person first experiences upon receiving healing-wholeness through faith. Peace here functions not as a general well-being but as the specific inner-being state that attends the settling of the faith-crisis: the striving of faith has reached its object, and the inner being rests in that reception. (2) In the blindness-healing cases (Mar 10:52; Luk 18:42), the immediate response is discipleship-orientation — "followed him on the way" — suggesting that in the sight-restoration mode, the first inner response is not peace-as-rest but forward-movement toward Christ. (3) In the conscience-appeal mode (1Pe 3:21), the immediate inner response is not named explicitly but the structure implies conscience-cleared: the appeal to God is answered by resurrection, and the good conscience is the immediate inner result. Across all three modes, the immediate response involves an inner-being shift from the state that preceded the faith-exercise (fear, darkness, troubled conscience) to a new integrated state (peace, sight/following, clear conscience).
- *T1.5.2* — _Is the immediate response consistent across the verse evidence, or does it vary?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The immediate response varies across the verse evidence, and the variation is structurally meaningful rather than arbitrary. (1) Peace (eirēnē): Mar 5:34 and Luk 8:48 both close with Jesus pronouncing peace — in the haemorrhage narrative, peace is the named immediate inner-being state following reception of healing-through-faith. (2) Sight and discipleship-movement: Mar 10:52 and Luk 18:42 close with physical restoration (sight received immediately) and then forward movement (Bartimaeus "followed him on the way"). The immediate response here is not peace-as-rest but sight-enabled following — a directional movement rather than a settled state. (3) Conscience-cleared: 1Pe 3:21 implies that the immediate result of the conscience-appeal is a good conscience established before God — the conscience's inner register shifts from troubled to clear. (4) Gratitude-return: Luk 17:19 positions the Samaritan's return as the faith-act that precedes the declaration of wholeness, so the immediate response is the completion of the faith-act in grateful recognition. The responses are consistent in one respect — each names an inner-being shift from a prior fragmented or distressed state to an integrated one — but the specific form of the shift varies with the mode of operation and the dimension primarily engaged (somatic-spiritual, volitional-directional, or conscience-level).
- *T1.5.3* — _Where the verse evidence is silent on immediate response, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — Silences in the immediate response evidence are as follows. (1) Mar 5:23 (VCG-02): Jairus's plea for his dying daughter documents the faith-exercise (urgent plea) but the healing of the daughter (Luk 8:50 parallel) is narrated in the following pericope; the immediate inner response of Jairus himself upon receiving healing for his daughter is not explicitly named in either verse of the M38-C corpus (Mar 5:23; Luk 8:50). The corpus captures the faith-exercise moment and the command to believe but not the inner-being state of Jairus post-healing. (2) Jam 5:15 (VCG-03): The verse describes the prayer of faith and its result (the sick will be saved; sins forgiven) but does not name the immediate inner-being response of the healed person. The intercessory mode of this verse leaves the recipient's inner response unnarrated. (3) 1Pe 3:21 (VCG-03): The conscience-appeal through baptism is described structurally but the immediate inner experience of the person who makes this appeal and receives the good conscience is not phenomenologically narrated in the verse; the sustained state (good conscience) is named but the moment of reception is not described.
- *T1.6.1* — _What does this characteristic produce in the inner being over time?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The verse evidence for sustained effect is present but sparse, concentrated in two instances. (1) Mar 10:52 — Bartimaeus's sustained effect is discipleship: "he followed him on the way." The "on the way" language in Mark implies not a momentary following but ongoing relational orientation toward Jesus on the road to Jerusalem and beyond. This suggests that the sustained inner-being effect of receiving wholeness through faith is the establishment of a new directional orientation — the person is now a follower, moving with Christ. (2) 1Pe 3:21 — the sustained effect of baptism-as-conscience-appeal is a good conscience maintained before God "through the resurrection of Jesus Christ." The conscience is not merely cleansed once but sustained in right standing by virtue of the resurrection ground on which the appeal is made. The sustained effect is therefore ongoing conscience-integrity. (3) Luk 17:19 implies that grateful return to the giver of wholeness is the sustained posture, though this is an inference from the single act. Taken together, the sustained effects evidenced are: re-oriented discipleship (following Christ), conscience-integrity maintained before God, and gratitude as an ongoing orientation. These are inner-being changes at the level of constitution, not merely momentary affective states.
- *T1.6.2* — _What states, qualities, capacities, or orientations does sustained exposure to or possession of this characteristic establish?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The verse evidence for sustained states is limited but specific. (1) Reoriented discipleship: Mar 10:52 establishes that the sustained state following faith-exercised-wholeness is a following-orientation toward Christ — "on the way" in Markan theology implies ongoing journeying with Jesus toward Jerusalem and beyond; the capacity established is continued relational-volitional alignment with Christ. (2) Conscience-integrity: 1Pe 3:21's "good conscience" maintained "through the resurrection of Jesus Christ" implies that the sustained quality is conscience-integrity — an ongoing inner-being state of right standing before God, not merely momentary clearance. This is a quality of the inner person's relationship to God that becomes their new constitution. (3) Gratitude as dispositional orientation: Luk 17:19 suggests that the wholeness received through faith generates a sustained grateful orientation toward the giver — the Samaritan's return is both an immediate act and an indicator of a dispositional posture. (4) Intercessory capacity: Jam 5:15 within a community-practice frame suggests that sustained faith-exercise develops intercessory capacity — the characteristic, when practiced in the VCG-03 mode, establishes the person/community as ongoing sites of faith-activated rescue for others. These sustained effects establish the person at the level of inner constitution rather than episodic experience.
- *T1.6.3* — _Does the sustained effect differ from the immediate response — and if so, how?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — Yes, the sustained effect differs from the immediate response in both register and duration. (1) In the haemorrhage narrative, the immediate response is peace (Mar 5:34; Luk 8:48) — this names the inner-being state at the moment of reception. The sustained effect evidenced from the Bartimaeus parallel is discipleship-orientation (Mar 10:52 — "followed him on the way") — a long-term relational-directional constitution that goes beyond the immediate peace-experience. The immediate response is a state (peace); the sustained effect is an orientation (following). These are different in kind: one is receptive and settling, the other is active and directional. (2) In the conscience-appeal mode (1Pe 3:21), the good conscience appears to be both immediate result and sustained state — it is both what is received and what is maintained through the resurrection ground. Here the distinction between immediate and sustained is less sharp. (3) For intercessory faith (Jam 5:15), no immediate inner response is named for the healed person (see T1.5.3 silences), but the sustained effect of community-practiced intercessory faith would be the establishment of the community as a site of ongoing integrated rescue — a capacity-building sustained effect that exceeds the individual healing event. The general pattern is: immediate response = reception of specific inner-being shift (peace, sight, clear conscience); sustained effect = constitutional reorientation (discipleship, conscience-integrity, intercessory capacity) that endures beyond the precipitating event.
- *T1.7.1* — _What inner conditions or orientations enable genuine reception of this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The verse evidence names several enabling inner conditions explicitly. (1) Interior conviction formed and held: Mat 9:21 and Mar 5:28 show the woman forming an explicit interior conviction ("if I touch his garment I will be made well") before any outward completion — the faith must be formed inwardly first; diffuse or unformed desire is not evidenced as sufficient. (2) Persistent directional intent under pressure: the woman presses through a crowd; Bartimaeus calls repeatedly despite rebuke (Mar 10:48) — the inner condition is not passive waiting but active, sustained directional movement toward Jesus despite obstacles. (3) Fear displaced rather than indulged: Luk 8:50 positions faith as the active inner replacement for fear — the enabling condition is the willingness to counter fear with belief. (4) Gratitude and relational recognition: Luk 17:19 suggests that complete reception involves returning to acknowledge the source — the Samaritan's return is marked as the faith-act that distinguishes whole-making sōzō from mere physical cure. (5) Conscience-openness to appeal: 1Pe 3:21 requires that the inner being be in a posture of appeal to God rather than self-sufficiency — the conscience must acknowledge need and turn toward God for right standing.
- *T1.7.2* — _What inner conditions block, distort, or prevent reception?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The verse evidence names or implies several blocking conditions. (1) Fear as governing state: Luk 8:50's structure — "do not fear; only believe" — positions fear as the primary blocking condition. When fear governs the inner being, faith-exercise cannot proceed because fear is the opposite directional movement (inward self-protection rather than outward trust-extension toward God). (2) Failure to bring faith to completion in grateful recognition: Luk 17:17-19 implies that the nine lepers who received physical cure but did not return failed to receive full sōzō-wholeness. The blocking condition here is the inner posture of taking benefit without relational acknowledgment — a form of inner-being self-sufficiency that receives the physical without opening to the spiritual register. (3) Reliance on external rite as substitute for inner appeal: 1Pe 3:21 explicitly blocks the distortion of treating baptism as physical cleansing rather than conscience-appeal — the blocking/distorting condition is ritualism that does not engage the conscience's inner faculty. (4) Crowd-pressure or social discouragement: Bartimaeus is rebuked and told to be silent (Mar 10:48) — while he persists, the implicit blocking condition is social pressure to suppress the faith-cry; those who capitulate to such pressure would not exercise the faith that activates sōzō. These blocking conditions identify the inner-being vulnerabilities that prevent reception.
- *T1.7.3* — _What is the inner-being state of the person who encounters this characteristic but does not receive it?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The corpus provides partial evidence for this through contrast cases. (1) The nine lepers (Luk 17:17-19): those who receive physical cure but do not return with faith-in-gratitude encounter the possibility of sōzō-wholeness but do not receive it in its full register. Their inner-being state is one of benefit-reception without relational acknowledgment — they remain in a posture of self-continuation after healing rather than return-and-recognition. The verse does not name their inner state as fear or hostility, but as ungrateful self-directedness. (2) The crowd that rebukes Bartimaeus (Mar 10:48): those who tell him to be silent are in a state of social conformity that suppresses faith-cry — they encounter the possibility of faith-exercised but remain in a normalised expectation that healing is not available or appropriate. Their inner state is one of resigned normalcy rather than faith-urgency. (3) Luk 8:50 implies that those who remain in fear rather than believing encounter the saving-healing possibility but cannot receive it — their inner state is fear-governed. (4) 1Pe 3:21 implies that those who treat baptism as external rite rather than conscience-appeal encounter the form of the characteristic without its inner reality — their state is formalistic externalism, with conscience unengaged. The common thread is: the person who does not receive the characteristic remains in a state of inner fragmentation — fear-governed, self-directed, socially conformist, or externally-focused — without the inner-being movement toward God that reception requires.
- *T1.8.1* — _What is the primary inner-being dimension of this characteristic from the programme's dimension vocabulary?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The primary inner-being dimension of this characteristic is volitional-faith: the characteristic operates as a specific act of the will directed by trust toward God. The evidence for this classification is concentrated in M38-C-VCG-01 and VCG-02: the woman's interior monologue (Mat 9:21; Mar 5:28), Jesus's repeated formula "your faith has made you well" (Mat 9:22; Mar 5:34; Luk 8:48; Luk 17:19; Luk 18:42; Mar 10:52), and the command "only believe" against fear (Luk 8:50) all locate the primary faculty in an act of the will shaped by trust. Faith as exercised here is not primarily cognitive (doctrinal assent), not primarily affective (emotional response), but volitional-trust: a directed inner act of reliance. Secondary dimensions are present — conscience (1Pe 3:21), relational-hope (Mat 9:21's expectation of wholeness), and somatic-spiritual bridging (Jam 5:15) — but these function as correlates or outcomes of the volitional-faith act rather than the primary dimension. The characteristic's classification is therefore volitional-faith as primary, with conscience and relational-hope as secondary dimensions.
- *T1.8.2* — _What evidence from the verse evidence supports this classification?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — The classification of this characteristic's primary dimension as volitional-faith (established in T1.8.1) is supported by the following specific evidence. (1) The standard Synoptic formula "your faith has made you well" (Mat 9:22; Mar 5:34; Luk 8:48; Luk 17:19; Luk 18:42; Mar 10:52) directly attributes the operative cause to an inner-being faculty — faith — that is consistently located in the person exercising it. The formula names faith as the instrument, not emotion, not knowledge, not bodily action alone. (2) The interior monologue in Mat 9:21 and Mar 5:28 shows faith operating as a formed volitional act — "I will be made well" — which is a directional willing, not merely feeling or thinking. (3) The command "only believe" in Luk 8:50 positions believing as a volitional act that can be commanded — emotions cannot be commanded directly; will can. This supports volitional primacy. (4) 1Pe 3:21's "appeal to God for a good conscience" identifies the operative inner faculty as conscience-mediated volitional petition — the word "appeal" (eperōtēma) carries the sense of a formal directed request, which is an act of the will. (5) Jam 5:15's "prayer of faith" similarly identifies the operative mode as volitional-directed-trust expressed through prayer. All evidence converges on volitional-faith as the governing dimension.
- *T1.8.3* — _Does this characteristic carry secondary dimensions — and if so, what are they, and do they compete with the primary classification?_
  → **[CHAR-3]** E — Secondary dimensions are evidenced and do not compete with the primary volitional-faith classification but supplement it. (1) Conscience dimension: 1Pe 3:21 and Jam 5:15 both engage the conscience as a primary inner-being organ — "appeal to God for a good conscience" (1Pe 3:21); sins forgiven alongside healing (Jam 5:15). The conscience dimension is real and irreducible in M38-C-VCG-03, but it appears as the inner-being locus that faith-exercised restores rather than as the operating faculty of faith itself. Conscience is the dimension addressed; volitional-faith is the dimension that addresses it. (2) Relational-hope dimension: the woman's interior conviction (Mat 9:21; Mar 5:28) contains an anticipatory hope — "I will be made well" — which is a hope-oriented projection of the inner being toward future reception. This is a secondary relational-hope dimension active in the faith-formation stage. (3) Somatic-spiritual bridging dimension: the characteristic consistently bridges physical and spiritual registers, which means that the somatic dimension of the inner person (or its analogue — the person's embodied condition) is engaged alongside the volitional-spiritual. This is not a competing classification but a distinctive feature that marks this characteristic as embodied-faith, not disembodied mysticism. None of these secondary dimensions displace volitional-faith as primary; each names an aspect of what faith-exercised engages or produces rather than an alternative primary faculty.
<!-- /EVIDENCE -->


**Key verses for Healing wholeness through faith exercised:**

**Mat 9:22** (G4982 sōzō) [M38-C-VCG-01]
> Mat 9:22 Jesus turned , and seeing her he said , “Take heart , daughter ; your faith has made you well .” And instantly the woman was made well .
_Meaning of the term here:_ Jesus confirms healing through faith — 'your faith has made you well' — the sōzō is healing with spiritual resonance, with faith as the inner-being faculty that activates the saving act.

**Luk 8:50** (G4982 sōzō) [M38-C-VCG-02]
> Luk 8:50 But Jesus on hearing this answered him , “Do not fear ; only believe , and she will be well .”
_Meaning of the term here:_ Jesus counters fear with the command to believe so that the girl will be made well — sōzō names physical rescue from death, with faith explicitly set against fear as the inner faculty that opens the door to saving action.

**Jam 5:15** (G4982 sōzō) [M38-C-VCG-03]
> Jam 5:15 And the prayer of faith will save the one who is sick , and the Lord will raise him up . And if he has committed sins , he will be forgiven .
_Meaning of the term here:_ The prayer of faith heals the sick and secures forgiveness if there is sin; healing and spiritual salvation converge — the inner being is restored in body and conscience through interceding faith.
