<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T4 findings for Conscience cleansed through atonement received -->

**T4 — Relational Interfaces**

- *T4.1.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating from God toward the human person — and if so, how?_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence shows God-to-human operation as the dominant structural axis of this characteristic. VCG-04 is the concentrated locus: Psa 65:3 states that when iniquities "overwhelm" the psalmist, God himself atones for the transgressions — the act is entirely from God's side. Psa 78:38 shows God compassionately atoning for Israel's iniquity and restraining his own wrath "repeatedly rather than destroying them." Psa 79:9 frames God's atoning action as tied to his name and his saving power. Isa 6:7 presents a direct divine-to-human atoning touch: the burning coal cleanses Isaiah's lips and the angelic messenger declares "your guilt is removed, your sin is atoned." Heb 8:12 records God's New Covenant promise to "be merciful (hileōs) toward iniquities and remember sins no more" — pure divine-to-human gift. 1Jo 4:10 (VCG-05 anchor) makes the structure explicit: "not that we loved God but that he loved us and sent his Son as the hilasmos" — divine prior love is the initiating movement. VCG-02 adds Lev 17:11, where God himself provides the blood-mechanism: "I have given it for you on the altar to make atonement for your souls." Even the OT priestly ritual system is framed as God-given provision. The characteristic operates from God to human as its primary directional axis throughout the corpus.
- *T4.1.2* — _What does the evidence reveal about the basis on which God extends this characteristic — is it conditional, unconditional, covenantal, or re…_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence reveals a complex basis that is simultaneously covenantal, compassion-driven, and responsive to human posture, but never unconditional in the sense of requiring nothing. Several distinct bases appear: (1) Covenantal-systematic: VCG-01 (Lev 4–6) presents atonement as God's given system within the covenant — the mechanism is divinely established and guaranteed when properly enacted. God provides the means (Lev 17:11: "I have given it for you"). (2) Compassion-responsive: Psa 78:38 explicitly states God's compassion as the basis — "being compassionate, he atoned for their iniquity and did not destroy them" — suggesting a responsive divine disposition toward the persistently sinful. (3) Name/glory-driven: Psa 79:9 appeals to God's atoning action "for the glory of your name," indicating divine reputation as a motivation. (4) Love-originated: 1Jo 4:10 makes the basis divine prior love — the initiative comes before human seeking. (5) Faith-conditioned reception: Rom 3:25 specifies that the hilastērios is "received through faith" — God sets forth the provision but it is accessed through faith exercised. (6) Withholdable: VCG-06 shows God explicitly refusing atonement when sin has become persistent and defiant (1Sa 3:14, Isa 22:14). The overall basis is covenantal-compassionate with faith as the reception condition, neither purely unconditional nor purely merit-based.
- *T4.1.3* — _What does God's extension of this characteristic reveal about his disposition toward the human person?_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence reveals a consistent divine disposition of compassionate, initiative-taking mercy toward guilty persons. Psa 78:38 (VCG-04) names God's disposition explicitly: "being compassionate, he atoned for iniquity and did not destroy them" — compassion is the named disposition that produces atoning action rather than deserved destruction. 1Jo 4:10 (VCG-04 anchor) names the disposition as prior love: God acts before human initiative, his love being the source of the sent hilasmos. Lev 17:11 (VCG-02) shows God giving the blood-mechanism himself — "I have given it to you on the altar" — indicating that the provision of the means of atonement is itself an expression of divine benevolence toward the otherwise-uncovered soul. Heb 8:12 uses hileōs — "I will be merciful" — as God's self-describing disposition toward iniquity in the New Covenant. Psa 65:3 reveals the disposition as responsive to human overload: "when iniquities overwhelm us, you atone for our transgressions" — God's covering action is responsive to the human person's inability to bear the weight. The corpus also reveals God's disposition as serious about sin: VCG-06 shows that the same God who compassionately atones can permanently withhold atonement when persistently defied. God's disposition is consistently mercy-toward-the-contrite combined with justice-toward-the-defiant.
- *T4.1.4* — _If the evidence is silent on God-to-human operation, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence is not silent on God-to-human operation; it is extensively documented. See T4.1.1 and T4.1.2 above. VCG-04 and the NT propitiation cluster (VCG-05) provide the most direct evidence. This prompt requires no silence notation.
- *T4.2.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating in the human person's movement toward God — in seeking, supplication, worship, or…_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence shows a secondary but significant human-toward-God movement, primarily expressed as seeking, supplication, and sacrificial approach. VCG-01 (Lev 4–6 cluster) presents the offerer who brings a specified animal and the priest who mediates — the sinner's act of bringing the offering is a movement toward God through the prescribed ritual channel (e.g., Lev 4:20, 4:26, 5:6). Lev 1:4 (VCG-02) shows the worshipper actively laying hands on the sacrifice — a physical movement of identification and self-presentation before God. VCG-03 includes Exo 30:15–16 (ransom payment) and Num 31:50 (warriors voluntarily bringing gold as atonement) — both are human initiatory acts of approach. 2Sa 21:3 shows David seeking to know what atoning act is required — active inquiry in movement toward resolution before God. 2Ch 30:18 presents Hezekiah in intercessory prayer on behalf of others. Luk 18:13 (VCG-05) is the purest human-toward-God posture: the tax collector standing at distance, beating his breast, crying "God, be merciful (hilaskomai) to me, a sinner" — wholly dependent, approaching only through appeal to divine mercy. Human movement toward God in this characteristic is always posited on acknowledged need and inability to self-cover.
- *T4.2.2* — _What does the evidence reveal about the inner posture required for this movement?_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence reveals several inner-posture markers that characterize genuine human movement toward God in atonement-seeking. (1) Acknowledged sin-awareness: VCG-01 consistently presents the offerer as one who "has sinned" (Lev 4:22, 5:6) — the approach requires acknowledging real guilt. Lev 5:6 names the inner movement as guilt surfacing before the act of bringing the offering. (2) Contrition and self-abasement: Luk 18:13 (VCG-05) presents the archetypal posture — standing at distance, eyes downcast, beating the breast, confessing unworthiness. This is the most explicitly described inner-posture text. (3) Dependence on divine provision: The entire VCG-02 substitution cluster (Lev 1:4, Lev 17:11) implies the offerer's recognition that their own life cannot cover their own guilt — another life must substitute. (4) Sincerity over ritual precision: 2Ch 30:18 (VCG-03) shows God accepting those who "set their heart to seek God" even when ritually irregular — sincerity of orientation is required. (5) Faith exercised: Rom 3:25 identifies faith as the specific inner posture through which Christ as hilastērios is received. (6) Corporate solidarity: Deu 21:8 (VCG-07) and Psa 79:9 show communal approach as valid — individual and corporate postures both appear. Defiance and presumption are the negative contrasts, marking the postures that close the pathway (VCG-06).
- *T4.2.3* — _What does the human-to-God direction of this characteristic reveal about the person's relationship with God?_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The human-toward-God movement in atonement-seeking reveals a relationship characterized by acknowledged distance, moral asymmetry, and dependent appeal. The tax collector's posture in Luk 18:13 (VCG-05) is the clearest revelation: "standing far off," not lifting eyes, confessing sinner-status — the relationship is experienced as one of radical disqualification before a holy God, making the movement toward God entirely dependent on divine mercy rather than human merit. Lev 1:4 (VCG-02) reveals that human approach requires the substitution of another life — the person's own life is insufficient; the relationship is one in which the human cannot cover their own guilt before God. 2Sa 21:3 (VCG-03) shows David's inquiry about what atoning act is required — revealing a relationship in which the person recognizes accountability before God and seeks to fulfill the relational obligation. 2Ch 30:18 (VCG-03) reveals that the relationship can accommodate sincere but imperfect approach — Hezekiah's prayer assumes God's willingness to honor genuine heart-orientation even when ritual requirements are unmet. Pro 16:6 (VCG-04) adds that the fear of God is both the relational disposition that accompanies atonement and the moral orientation that flows from it. The human-to-God relationship in this characteristic is consistently one of moral need, dependent approach, and hope in divine mercy.
- *T4.2.4* — _If the evidence is silent on human-to-God operation, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence is not silent on human-to-God operation; it is well-documented. See T4.2.1 and T4.2.2 above. VCG-01, VCG-02, VCG-03, and Luk 18:13 (VCG-05) all provide human-toward-God movement evidence. This prompt requires no silence notation.
- *T4.3.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic being extended by one person toward another — and if so, how does it operate in that giving…_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence shows human-to-human extension operating primarily through priestly mediation and intercession, rather than direct peer-to-peer giving. The priest as mediator is the dominant figure: throughout VCG-01, the priest "makes atonement" for another person — he presents the offering, performs the ritual, and the outcome of forgiveness accrues to the offerer (e.g., Lev 4:20, 4:26, 5:10, 6:7). This is a form of giving: the priest extends atoning coverage to another through his office. Deu 21:8 (VCG-07) shows community leaders and priests together performing an intercession that covers corporate blood guilt. 2Ch 30:18 (VCG-03) shows Hezekiah's royal prayer as extended covering for ritually unprepared worshippers — one person's intercession seeking atoning coverage for others. The evidence does not show one ordinary person simply granting atonement to another; the giving direction is structurally mediated — through priestly office or intercessory prayer — and always requires divine ratification. Human giving of this characteristic is instrumental rather than originating.
- *T4.3.2* — _What inner conditions or orientations in the giver enable genuine extension of this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence addresses this primarily through the priestly and intercessory giving registers. The conditions that appear in the text are: (1) Proper office and consecration: Throughout VCG-01, effective priestly mediation requires that the priest himself be in atoned and consecrated standing (Exo 29:33, VCG-02 — priestly ordination atonement). A priest whose own sin is unpardoned cannot validly mediate (cf. the contrast with 1Sa 3:14, VCG-06, where persistent priestly sin nullifies the atonement pathway). (2) Compassion and pastoral intent: Heb 2:17 (VCG-05) describes Christ as a "merciful high priest" — mercy and solidarity with the recipient's condition are named as the inner orientation enabling effective propitiation. The priest must be "sympathetic" to make effective atonement. (3) Intercessory love: 2Ch 30:18 (VCG-03) shows Hezekiah's appeal grounded in genuine pastoral concern for the people. (4) Absence of punitive intent: The inverse in Jer 18:23 (VCG-06) shows a giver deliberately withholding atoning intercession — the contrast highlights that genuine giving requires benevolent intent toward the recipient. The evidence does not describe ordinary peer-to-peer giving of atonement, so the conditions named are primarily those of the mediator-role.
- *T4.3.3* — _What does the evidence reveal about what the person must have received or become before they can genuinely give this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence addresses this primarily through the priestly-mediator frame. For the priest as giver of atoning mediation: Exo 29:33 (VCG-02) shows that the priest must himself be in atoned and consecrated standing before exercising atoning ministry — the ordination atonement is a prerequisite for priestly function. The priest eats the ordination meal as the seal of their own covered status. This establishes a receive-before-give principle: effective atoning mediation requires that the mediator has themselves been brought into covered, consecrated standing. Heb 2:17 (VCG-05) extends this: Christ as the ultimate high priest had to become "fully human" — acquiring solidarity with those he mediates for — before he could make effective propitiation. The inner-being condition of having experienced what it is to be under sin's weight is implied as necessary for the sympathetic effective mediator. VCG-06's negative case (1Sa 3:14) confirms: a priest whose own sin has closed the atonement pathway cannot give what they have been denied. The principle across the evidence is that genuine giving of atoning mediation requires first being in a state of covered, consecrated standing before God — the giver must be an atoned mediator, not an unresolved sinner.
- *T4.3.4* — _If the evidence is silent on the giving direction, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence is not entirely silent on the giving direction, though human-to-human giving is structurally mediated rather than direct. See T4.3.1 and T4.3.2 above. Priestly mediation throughout VCG-01, intercessory prayer in VCG-03 and VCG-07, and Heb 2:17's merciful-high-priest frame all address the giving direction. This prompt requires no silence notation.
- *T4.4.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic being received by a person from another — and if so, how does it operate in that reception?_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — Reception is extensively documented, though the receiving person always receives from the priest-mediator who acts on God's behalf. VCG-01 repeatedly presents the individual sinner as recipient: the person brings the offering, the priest atones, and "he shall be forgiven" (Lev 4:20, 4:26, 4:31, 4:35, 5:10, 5:13, 5:16, 5:18, 6:7, 19:22). The inner-being movement in reception is consistently named: from "sin-laden" or "guilt-burdened" to "forgiven" and "conscience cleansed." Lev 1:4 (VCG-02) shows the worshipper receiving the "accepted" status through the priest's mediation on their behalf. In Isa 6:7, Isaiah receives atoning cleansing directly — "your guilt is removed, your sin covered." In the NT, Luk 18:13 (VCG-05) shows the tax collector receiving mercy (the text reports he "went home justified"). Rom 3:25 frames propitiation as received "by faith" — the inner posture of receiving is faith exercised toward the blood of Christ. Heb 8:12 presents the New Covenant recipient as one whose iniquities are "remembered no more." Reception in this characteristic produces an objective change in standing — guilt objectively removed — and a corresponding inner-being shift from condemnation to peace.
- *T4.4.2* — _What inner conditions enable or block reception of this characteristic from another person?_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence identifies both enabling and blocking conditions for reception. Enabling conditions: (1) Acknowledged guilt and contrition: VCG-01 consistently shows the recipient approaching "because they have sinned" — the sin must be named and owned. Eze 16:63 (VCG-07) shows the atoned person as one who is silenced in shame — receptivity is marked by inability to self-justify. (2) Faith exercised: Rom 3:25 explicitly names faith as the inner condition for receiving propitiation through Christ's blood. Luk 18:13 models faith-as-desperation: the tax collector's cry is the receiving posture. (3) Sincere seeking: 2Ch 30:18 (VCG-03) shows that sincerity of heart-orientation ("set their heart to seek God") enables reception even when ritual conditions are imperfect. Blocking conditions: (1) Defiance and persistent unrepentance: VCG-06 (1Sa 3:14, Isa 22:14) shows that persistent, unrepentant sin can permanently close the reception pathway — the inner state of continued defiance blocks atonement. (2) Pride and self-sufficiency: Isa 47:11 (VCG-06) presents the "proud city" as one to whom atonement is unavailable — the inner-being posture of self-reliance is contrasted with the contrite posture of the tax collector. (3) Blood-guilt without restitution: Num 35:33 (VCG-06) shows murder-guilt as a category where standard atonement-reception is structurally blocked.
- *T4.4.3* — _What is the inner-being state of the person who encounters this characteristic from another but does not receive it?_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — VCG-06 provides the primary evidence for non-reception. The inner-being state is consistently characterized as guilt unresolved and wrath unrelieved: (1) Permanent guilt-weight: 1Sa 3:14 ("the iniquity of Eli's house shall not be atoned for by sacrifice or offering ever") describes a state where the atoning mechanism is permanently foreclosed — the inner being bears guilt that has no pathway to resolution. (2) Judgment exposure: Isa 22:14 shows guilt declared unresolvable in the person's lifetime — the inner person faces judgment unrelieved. Isa 47:11 presents the proud city as "helpless before judgment" when atonement is unavailable. (3) Moral pollution and communal contamination: Num 35:33 (VCG-06) describes the land itself as polluted by blood-guilt that cannot be covered by standard atonement — the non-reception extends beyond the individual to infect the community. (4) Conscience remaining condemned: The structural logic of the entire corpus implies that the mirror-image of conscience-cleansed is conscience-condemned — the person who does not receive atonement remains in the state the atonement was designed to resolve. Eze 16:63 (VCG-07), in its positive form (shame after atonement received), implies by contrast that prior to atonement the inner state would have been guilt-laden without the relief that follows covering. Non-reception leaves the person in an unrelieved, condemned, and exposed inner state.
- *T4.4.4* — _If the evidence is silent on the receiving direction, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence is not silent on the receiving direction; it is the most thoroughly documented dimension in the corpus. VCG-01 (11 verses), VCG-04 (Psa 65:3, Isa 6:7, Heb 8:12), and VCG-05 (Rom 3:25, Heb 2:17, 1Jo 2:2) all document reception extensively. This prompt requires no silence notation.
- *T4.5.1* — _Does the evidence indicate whether this characteristic operates differently within existing relational bonds versus across relational distan…_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence reveals meaningful boundary distinctions. Within the covenant community, atonement operates through the prescribed priestly system with guaranteed outcomes when properly enacted (VCG-01, Lev 4–6). Exo 30:15–16 (VCG-03) establishes that within Israel every soul — rich and poor equally — receives the same atonement coverage through the ransom payment, indicating that internal status differences do not differentiate access. However, certain relational and moral boundaries limit operation: Exo 29:33 (VCG-02) notes that "outsiders" who eat the ordination meal face exclusion — the atoned status belongs to those consecrated, not those outside the covenant. VCG-06 shows cases where atonement is withheld even within the covenant — when persistent priestly sin has closed the pathway (1Sa 3:14) or when judicial pronouncement is irreversible (Isa 22:14). 2Ch 30:18 (VCG-03) shows atonement extended even to ritually unprepared Israelites who sought sincerely, suggesting that sincere heart-orientation can override ritual boundary within the covenant. In the NT, 1Jo 2:2 explicitly crosses all prior covenantal boundaries: Christ's propitiation extends to "the whole world," not a limited community — representing the characteristic's maximum boundary expansion.
- *T4.5.2* — _Does this characteristic operate within covenantal contexts only, or does it cross covenantal boundaries?_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence shows a clear trajectory from covenantal-bounded toward boundary-crossing operation. In the OT corpus, atonement operates primarily within Israel's covenant framework: Lev 4–6 (VCG-01), Exo 30:15–16 (VCG-03), and the Day of Atonement structures address Israelites specifically. Exo 29:33 (VCG-02) explicitly marks outsiders as excluded from the atoned-and-consecrated status. Even within Israel, Num 35:33 (VCG-06) marks categories of persons for whom atonement is structurally unavailable. The prophetic texts (Isa 6:7, VCG-04; Dan 9:24, VCG-07) begin to expand the horizon: Isaiah's individual cleansing appears independent of standard ritual structure, and Daniel's seventy-weeks atonement has cosmic scope. The NT propitiation cluster (VCG-05) decisively crosses covenantal boundaries: 1Jo 2:2 explicitly states Christ's hilasmos is "not for our sins only but also for the whole world," removing the ethnic-covenantal restriction. Heb 8:12 (VCG-04) presents the New Covenant promise as directed to "the house of Israel and the house of Judah" but in Christ's application extends to all. Rom 3:25 frames propitiation as available to "all who believe," which contextually (Rom 3:22–29) includes both Jew and Gentile. The evidence thus shows progressive boundary-crossing, reaching universality in the NT.
- *T4.5.3* — _What does the evidence reveal about the relational scope of this characteristic — who is included and who is not?_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence reveals a complex scope with gradations. Included in the OT framework: all within Israel who bring the prescribed offering with sincerity — rich and poor equally (Exo 30:15, VCG-03). Lev 5:13 (VCG-01) shows that even economically vulnerable persons who can only afford grain offerings are included — accessibility is explicitly stated. Those ritually unprepared but sincerely seeking are included by divine mercy (2Ch 30:18, VCG-03). Excluded in the OT framework: (1) Outsiders not consecrated (Exo 29:33, VCG-02). (2) Those with unresolved murder-guilt beyond standard atonement (Num 35:33, VCG-06). (3) Those under God's judicial refusal due to persistent, defiant sin (1Sa 3:14, Isa 22:14, VCG-06). (4) The proud and self-reliant (Isa 47:11, VCG-06). Expanded scope in NT: 1Jo 2:2 explicitly states "the whole world" — the covenantal ethnic boundaries are removed. Rom 3:25 makes access dependent on faith rather than ethnic identity. Heb 2:17 frames Christ's solidarity with "the people" in terms that extend beyond Israel. The trajectory is from bounded-covenantal toward universal-by-faith, with the only ongoing exclusion being persistent unbelief or defiance rather than ethnic or ritual status.
- *T4.5.4* — _If the evidence is silent on relational boundaries, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence is not silent on relational boundaries; it addresses them in multiple registers. See T4.5.1, T4.5.2, and T4.5.3 above. The OT priestly system, the equal-ransom passages (VCG-03), VCG-06's exclusion cases, and 1Jo 2:2's universal scope all address the boundary question. This prompt requires no silence notation.
- *T4.6.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating in relation to other spiritual beings — angelic or adversarial — and if so, how?_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence shows one clear instance of angelic mediation in the atoning act. Isa 6:7 (VCG-04) presents a seraph (burning angelic being) who takes a coal from the altar and touches Isaiah's lips, pronouncing "your guilt is removed, your sin is atoned for" — the angelic being is the physical agent of the divine atoning touch, acting on God's behalf as a mediary. This is the only verse in the corpus that directly involves a named spiritual being in the enactment of atonement. The evidence does not show adversarial spiritual activity in relation to this characteristic. No verse presents an adversarial power attacking, distorting, or weaponising the atoning function. VCG-06 (atonement withheld) presents God's own judicial closure of the atonement pathway, not adversarial interference. The angelic interface appears to be purely instrumental and positive — angelic beings as agents of the divine atoning action rather than independent participants.
- *T4.6.3* — _Is this characteristic communicated, strengthened, or mediated through angelic ministry in the evidence?_
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence contains one direct instance of angelic mediation of atonement. Isa 6:7 (VCG-04) presents a seraph who takes a live coal from the altar, flies to Isaiah, touches his lips, and announces "your guilt is removed and your sin atoned for." The angelic being is the physical mediating agent through whom the divine atoning touch reaches Isaiah — without the seraph's action, the atoning effect is not delivered. This is the sole instance in the corpus. The evidence does not show angelic beings communicating or strengthening the inner-being experience of atonement in any other passage. The broader corpus shows atonement mediated through priests (VCG-01, VCG-02), through the blood mechanism (VCG-02), through priestly intercession (VCG-03), and through Christ (VCG-05) — angelic mediation appears only in this one prophetic vision context. The Isa 6:7 case may be understood as a unique theophanic-visionary context rather than a normative pattern of angelic atoning ministry.
- *T4.6.4* — _If the evidence is silent on the spiritual beings interface, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-4]** E — The evidence is not entirely silent: Isa 6:7 (VCG-04) provides a direct instance of angelic mediation of atonement. However, apart from this single verse, the corpus is largely silent on spiritual beings involvement. There is no adversarial spiritual dimension addressed anywhere in the evidence block. The spiritual beings interface is minimally represented relative to the corpus's overall scope.
<!-- /EVIDENCE -->

<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T4 silences for Conscience cleansed through atonement received -->

**T4 — Relational Interfaces**

- *T4.6.2* — _Is this characteristic a site of adversarial activity — something that can be attacked, distorted, or weaponised by adversarial spiritual po…_
  → **[CHAR-4]** S — The verse corpus for Characteristic 4 does not contain evidence of adversarial spiritual activity directed against the atoning mechanism or the conscience-cleansed state. VCG-06 presents cases of atonement being withheld or unavailable, but these are attributed to God's judicial action and human persistent sin, not to adversarial spiritual interference. No verse in the evidence block depicts Satan, demonic powers, or other adversarial beings attacking, distorting, blocking, or weaponising the atonement process or the cleared conscience. Isa 6:7 involves a seraph acting positively on God's behalf. The corpus is silent on adversarial spiritual engagement with this characteristic.
