<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T4 findings for Salvation anticipated and hope sustained -->

**T4 — Relational Interfaces**

- *T4.1.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating from God toward the human person — and if so, how?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence strongly affirms God-to-human operation as the foundational structure of salvation-as-hope. Across all VCGs, salvation is categorically a divine gift moving toward the human person, not a human achievement. In M38-F-VCG-01, Gen 49:18 names the LORD as the soul's only source of deliverance; 1Pe 1:5 states believers are "guarded by God's power through faith" toward a yet-to-be-revealed salvation — the guarding initiative is entirely divine. In M38-F-VCG-02, Isa 51:6 declares God's salvation "everlasting," contrasting it with a cosmos that perishes — God is the one who sustains what time cannot erode. In M38-F-VCG-04, God is figured as divine warrior riding a chariot of salvation (Hab 3:8), arming himself with a helmet of salvation (Isa 59:17), and declaring his will not to rest until Jerusalem's salvation blazes forth (Isa 62:1) — the initiative is God's urgent, militant movement toward the human. In M38-F-VCG-06, Tit 3:4 names the appearance of God's "goodness and loving kindness" as Savior, and Tit 3:6 notes the Spirit "poured out richly" through the Savior — both movements are divine extension toward the human person. The characteristic is constitutively God-originating and God-sustained; human hope is the inner-being response to divine initiative, not its cause.
- *T4.1.2* — _What does the evidence reveal about the basis on which God extends this characteristic — is it conditional, unconditional, covenantal, or re…_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence reveals a multi-modal basis that is simultaneously covenantal, character-grounded, and responsive, but not primarily conditional on human performance. In M38-F-VCG-04, God's warrior-advance toward salvation (Hab 3:8, Isa 59:17, Isa 62:1) suggests a divine commitment that is self-impelled — "he will not rest until Jerusalem's salvation blazes forth" (Isa 62:1) — indicating an unconditional resolve grounded in God's own character and purpose. In M38-F-VCG-06, Tit 3:4 grounds salvation in "God's goodness and loving kindness," not human merit — the basis is divine disposition. In M38-F-VCG-02, Isa 51:6's eternal salvation is contrasted with perishing creation, suggesting its basis is God's own unchanging nature. Covenantal grounding appears in Luk 1:69 (ancestral promise) and Isa 49:8 (divinely appointed day of favor). The responsive dimension appears in Isa 33:2 (God answers the pleading community) and Isa 49:8 (he "answers and helps" in the appointed moment). The overall pattern: salvation-hope is extended on covenantal-character grounds, not conditioned on human achievement, though responsive to trust and supplication.
- *T4.1.3* — _What does God's extension of this characteristic reveal about his disposition toward the human person?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — God's extension of salvation-hope reveals a disposition of militant, urgent, generous, and personally engaged commitment toward the human person. In M38-F-VCG-04, God's self-arming with a "helmet of salvation" (Isa 59:17) and riding a "chariot of salvation" into cosmic battle (Hab 3:8) discloses a disposition of warrior-love — God mobilizes himself for human rescue. His declaration that he "will not rest" until salvation blazes forth (Isa 62:1) reveals urgency and persistence, not detached sovereignty. In M38-F-VCG-06, Tit 3:4's naming of "goodness and loving kindness" as the face of the Savior's appearing reveals that salvation discloses the warmth of divine character. Isa 51:6's eternal salvation and Isa 51:8's salvation "to all generations" reveal a permanence of divine commitment exceeding any creaturely relationship. Luk 1:47's Mary naming God as "my Savior" — personal and particular — shows the disposition extends to individual persons, not merely to collective humanity. God's disposition toward the person anticipating salvation is one of covenantal loyalty, personal interest, cosmic engagement, and self-giving generosity.
- *T4.1.4* — _If the evidence is silent on God-to-human operation, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence is not silent on God-to-human operation; it is extensively attested. See T4.1.1 (obs_id=324) and T4.1.2 (obs_id=325) and T4.1.3 (obs_id=326) for full evidence. The God-to-human direction is the foundational and dominant axis of this characteristic across all seven VCGs.
- *T4.2.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating in the human person's movement toward God — in seeking, supplication, worship, or…_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence shows a robust human-to-God directional movement, primarily in the forms of supplication, waiting, and worship. In M38-F-VCG-01, Isa 33:2 pictures the community pleading for salvation in distress, placing their "waiting trust entirely in God" as their only deliverer — this is supplication oriented Godward as the sole arm of rescue. Gen 49:18 is an explicit declaration of waiting directed toward the LORD. In M38-F-VCG-07, Isa 26:18's recognition of human failure at deliverance ("labored but produced nothing") represents the soul's forced acknowledgment of incapacity and implicit turn Godward. In M38-F-VCG-06, Mary's spirit "exults in God as her Savior" (Luk 1:47) — this is worship movement toward God arising from experienced salvation. In M38-F-VCG-03, Heb 9:28 describes believers "eagerly waiting" for the returning Christ — the inner being is actively postured Godward in anticipation. The human-to-God movement in this characteristic takes multiple forms: voiced supplication (Isa 33:2), confessed longing (Gen 49:18, Isa 59:11), worshipful exultation (Luk 1:47), prophetic searching (1Pe 1:10), and sustained eschatological orientation (Phili 3:20, Tit 2:13).
- *T4.2.2* — _What does the evidence reveal about the inner posture required for this movement?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence consistently reveals that the inner posture required for the human-to-God movement is active, forward-leaning expectancy combined with honest acknowledgment of human incapacity. Gen 49:18's waiting is volitional, not passive: the soul consciously names the LORD as its sole deliverer. Isa 33:2's supplication arises from distress without self-sufficiency. Isa 59:11's mourning that salvation is "far off" involves honest recognition of the gap between hope and experience. Job 13:16 (M38-F-VCG-05) reveals that the posture includes integrity — confidence before God grounded not in achievement but in willingness to approach despite danger. 1Pe 1:10's prophets model intense, searching inquiry — not casual expectation but rigorous engagement with the not-yet-received. Heb 9:28's "eagerly waiting" (M38-F-VCG-03) adds the dimension of active desire. Taken together: the required inner posture is volitional expectancy + honest acknowledgment of need + integrity of approach + directedness entirely toward God as the only source.
- *T4.2.3* — _What does the human-to-God direction of this characteristic reveal about the person's relationship with God?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The human-to-God movement of salvation-anticipated reveals a relationship characterized by exclusive dependence, honest vulnerability, and confident access. Gen 49:18's waiting exclusively on the LORD discloses a relationship in which no other source of deliverance is named or sought — God is the single, non-negotiable referent of the soul's hope. Isa 33:2's supplication from distress reveals a relationship in which the community considers themselves legitimately able to bring their crisis to God, expecting response. Isa 59:11's mourning before God about salvation's absence discloses a relationship honest enough to voice unfulfilled longing without abandoning the relationship. Job 13:16's willingness to "approach God despite danger" reveals a relationship bold enough to sustain directedness toward God even under existential threat. Luk 1:47's exultation reveals a relationship in which received salvation produces worship — God is not merely utility but the proper object of the whole person's joy. The evidence thus shows a relationship of exclusive trust, honest lament, bold approach, and worshipful response — all held together by the sustained orientation of the soul toward God as the only source of salvation.
- *T4.2.4* — _If the evidence is silent on human-to-God operation, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence is not silent on the human-to-God direction; it is substantially attested. See T4.2.1 (obs_id=328) through T4.2.3 (obs_id=330) for full evidence. The human-to-God movement — through waiting, supplication, worship, and prophetic searching — is a major structural component of this characteristic's inner-being profile.
- *T4.3.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic being extended by one person toward another — and if so, how does it operate in that giving…_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence shows this characteristic being extended person-to-person in a limited but specific form: the communication of salvation-hope through suffering and apostolic witness. In M38-F-VCG-05, 2Cor 1:6 explicitly states that Paul's afflictions "work for the salvation and comfort of others who endure the same sufferings" — the apostle's patient endurance becomes a salvific gift to others, a giving of solidarity that participates in their salvation. In M38-F-VCG-01, 1Pe 1:10 records that prophets "searched and inquired carefully about this salvation" for the benefit of future generations — a generative, forward-giving of anticipated salvation across time. Phili 1:19 notes that corporate prayer works for Paul's personal deliverance — the community's intercession is a form of giving salvific hope toward another. The giving direction is not absent but is subordinate to the divine-human axis; human-to-human extension operates primarily through suffering-solidarity, intercession, and prophetic witness rather than through direct conferral of salvation itself.
- *T4.3.2* — _What inner conditions or orientations in the giver enable genuine extension of this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence suggests that genuine extension of salvation-hope to others requires prior reception and present endurance. In 2Cor 1:6 (M38-F-VCG-05), Paul's afflictions work salvifically for others precisely because he has experienced the same sufferings and endured them — his capacity to give arises from his own tested hope, not from insulation from suffering. In 1Pe 1:10, the prophets' generative searching extended the gift of anticipated salvation to others because they themselves were seized by the grace they described, "searching and inquiring carefully" without possessing the benefit themselves. The inner conditions appear to be: (1) having received and internalized salvific hope oneself; (2) willingness to suffer without abandoning hope; (3) a posture of other-directedness that transmits what one has received or endured. The evidence does not describe a formal mechanism of giving but rather an organic overflow — hope that has been tested and sustained becomes communicable hope for others.
- *T4.3.3* — _What does the evidence reveal about what the person must have received or become before they can genuinely give this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence suggests two prerequisites for genuine giving of salvation-hope to others: (1) prior reception of the hope itself, and (2) actual experience of testing and endurance. In 1Pe 1:10, the prophets searched with intensity for a salvation they would not themselves receive — yet their searching itself constituted a giving forward. This implies that engagement with and genuine longing for salvation, even without final possession, enables extension to others. In 2Cor 1:6, Paul's afflictions give salvific benefit to others who "endure the same sufferings" — the prerequisite here is having endured, not having been insulated. The evidence does not describe giving from a position of completed, stable arrival; rather, giving occurs from within sustained hope that has been tested. The giver must have become someone whose hope is real enough to have endured its deferrals — a person who has experienced salvation as present-yet-not-fully-seen (Rom 8:24's "saved in hope") rather than as mere aspiration.
- *T4.3.4* — _If the evidence is silent on the giving direction, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence is not fully silent on the giving direction. Human-to-human extension of salvation-hope is evidenced in 2Cor 1:6, Phili 1:19, and 1Pe 1:10. However, this direction is significantly subordinate in the corpus to the divine-human axis; the giving direction is present but not elaborated as a primary concern of the characteristic. See T4.3.1 (obs_id=332) and T4.3.3 (obs_id=334) for what evidence exists.
- *T4.4.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic being received by a person from another — and if so, how does it operate in that reception?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence shows this characteristic received through several distinct channels. In M38-F-VCG-05, 2Cor 1:6 explicitly names those who receive "salvation and comfort" through the apostle's affliction-borne witness — they receive salvific benefit through the solidarity of shared suffering. In Phili 1:19, Paul receives deliverance "through corporate prayer and the Spirit's aid" — communal intercession is the medium through which rescue comes. In M38-F-VCG-01, 1Pe 1:10 implies successive generations received the benefit of prophets' searching — salvation's hope is transmitted and received across generational lines. In M38-F-VCG-06, Heb 1:14 notes believers receive angelic ministry oriented toward their salvation inheritance — reception here is mediated through celestial service. The dominant mode of reception in the evidence is covenantal and communal rather than isolated and individual: the person receives salvation-hope through the community's prayer (Phili 1:19), through testimony of those who suffered (2Cor 1:6), and through inherited prophetic promise (1Pe 1:10).
- *T4.4.4* — _If the evidence is silent on the receiving direction, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence is not fully silent on the receiving direction; human-to-human reception is present in 2Cor 1:6 and Phili 1:19. However, the internal conditions that enable or block person-to-person reception (obs_id=337) are not addressed in the corpus, as noted in T4.4.2. The receiving direction exists in the evidence but is thin; the dominant receiving pattern is human reception from God, not from another person.
- *T4.5.1* — _Does the evidence indicate whether this characteristic operates differently within existing relational bonds versus across relational distan…_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence shows this characteristic explicitly crossing relational and ethnic boundaries while also operating within covenantal bonds. In M38-F-VCG-02, Isa 49:6 extends salvation "to all nations to the ends of the earth" through the servant — the scope explicitly breaks through national particularity. Isa 52:10 describes God's saving arm "visible to all nations." Luk 3:6 declares "all flesh shall see God's salvation" — the boundary-crossing character is intrinsic to the eschatological form of this salvation. Yet the evidence also shows covenant-specific operation: Luk 1:69 grounds salvation in the Davidic house and ancestral promise; Isa 62:1 focuses urgently on Jerusalem's salvation; 2Pe 3:15 addresses a specific community of believers for whom the Lord's patience is salvation. The evidence thus describes a characteristic that originates in covenantal particularity (Davidic promise, Israel's waiting) and moves outward toward universal scope — operating differently in each register but not excluding either.
- *T4.5.2* — _Does this characteristic operate within covenantal contexts only, or does it cross covenantal boundaries?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence clearly shows this characteristic crossing covenantal boundaries. M38-F-VCG-02 is the strongest cluster of evidence: Isa 49:6 explicitly names the servant's mission to extend God's salvation "to all nations to the ends of the earth," and Isa 52:10 declares God's saving arm revealed "to all nations." Luk 3:6's prophetic citation that "all flesh shall see God's salvation" removes any ethnic or covenantal restriction from the eschatological scope. These verses do not merely tolerate boundary-crossing; they define the fullness of salvation as universal disclosure. At the same time, the covenantal matrix remains the originating structure: Luk 1:69 roots salvation in the Davidic covenant, and Isa 49:8 grounds it in covenant mediation. The evidence thus describes a characteristic that is covenantally rooted but eschatologically unbounded — salvation anticipated is anticipated for all, not only for covenant insiders.
- *T4.5.3* — _What does the evidence reveal about the relational scope of this characteristic — who is included and who is not?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence reveals an expansive and ultimately universal relational scope with a covenantal origin. The strongest boundary-crossing evidence is in M38-F-VCG-02: Isa 49:6 explicitly names "all nations to the ends of the earth"; Isa 52:10 addresses "all nations"; Luk 3:6 affirms "all flesh shall see God's salvation." These texts remove any principled exclusion from the ultimate scope. 1Ti 4:10 (M38-F-VCG-03) names God as "Savior of all people, especially believers" — an inclusive scope with a covenantal priority. Heb 1:14 (M38-F-VCG-06) identifies "those who will inherit salvation" as the specific beneficiaries of angelic ministry — suggesting that within the universal scope, a particular community is distinguished by active reception and inheritance. The evidence does not describe anyone categorically excluded from the anticipated scope of salvation, but it does distinguish between those who actively wait in hope (the characteristic's subjects) and those for whom salvation remains an unacknowledged possibility.
- *T4.5.4* — _If the evidence is silent on relational boundaries, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence is not silent on relational boundaries; see T4.5.1 (obs_id=340), T4.5.2 (obs_id=341), and T4.5.3 (obs_id=342) for evidence addressing both covenantal particularity and universal scope. The boundary question is directly engaged in the corpus, particularly in M38-F-VCG-02.
- *T4.6.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating in relation to other spiritual beings — angelic or adversarial — and if so, how?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence shows angelic involvement but is nearly silent on adversarial involvement. In M38-F-VCG-06, Heb 1:14 explicitly states that angels are "sent to serve those who will inherit salvation" — the entire ministry of angelic beings is oriented around those whose inner-being is defined by salvation-as-inheritance. This is the clearest instance of spiritual-beings interface: celestial ministry serves the salvation-hope of human persons, making the angelic role instrumental to the characteristic's operation. Beyond this, the evidence does not describe adversarial attack on salvation-hope specifically, though Isa 59:11's community groaning that salvation is "far off" and Isa 26:18's failed deliverance may imply spiritual opposition broadly — but neither text names adversarial agency explicitly. The spiritual beings interface in this characteristic is primarily angelic-ministering rather than adversarial-contesting.
- *T4.6.3* — _Is this characteristic communicated, strengthened, or mediated through angelic ministry in the evidence?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — Yes. Heb 1:14 (M38-F-VCG-06) is explicit: angels are "sent to serve those who will inherit salvation" — the entire purpose of angelic ministry in this verse is defined by the salvation-inheritance of human persons. The inner-being dignity of those anticipating salvation is so significant that celestial service is permanently oriented around it. The verb "sent" (apostellomenous) implies active, sustained deployment rather than incidental assistance. While no other verse in the corpus describes a specific angelic act that mediates or strengthens salvation-hope in a particular moment, Heb 1:14 establishes as a structural principle that angelic ministry is constitutively linked to the salvation-anticipating community. The characteristic is thus not merely a private inner-being phenomenon but one that commands the attention and service of spiritual beings beyond the human register.
- *T4.6.4* — _If the evidence is silent on the spiritual beings interface, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence is not wholly silent on the spiritual beings interface: Heb 1:14 establishes angelic ministry as constitutively oriented toward the salvation-inheriting community (see T4.6.3, obs_id=346). The adversarial dimension of the spiritual beings interface is, however, absent from the evidence (see T4.6.2, obs_id=345). The spiritual beings interface is partially attested: angelic ministry confirmed, adversarial activity unattested.
<!-- /EVIDENCE -->

<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T4 silences for Salvation anticipated and hope sustained -->

**T4 — Relational Interfaces**

- *T4.6.2* — _Is this characteristic a site of adversarial activity — something that can be attacked, distorted, or weaponised by adversarial spiritual po…_
  → **[CHAR-6]** S — The evidence in M38-F does not name adversarial spiritual powers attacking, distorting, or weaponising salvation-hope. The absence-of-salvation experiences evidenced (Isa 59:11's mourning, Isa 26:18's failed deliverance, M38-F-VCG-07) are attributed to human incapacity and communal sin rather than to explicit adversarial spiritual interference. The corpus does not furnish evidence for adversarial activity against this characteristic. This absence should be noted explicitly: the spiritual beings interface in the evidence is angelic-ministerial (Heb 1:14) rather than adversarial-contesting.
<!-- /EVIDENCE -->

<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T5 findings for Salvation anticipated and hope sustained -->

**T5 — Formative and Developmental Dimension**

- *T5.1.1* — _Does this characteristic produce transformation in the person — and if so, does it change the person's condition, the person's orientation t…_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence shows this characteristic producing primarily a transformation of orientation rather than of present condition, while pointing toward a future transformation of condition. The distinctive inner-being state evidenced is the soul reoriented toward a not-yet-received salvation that reshapes how it inhabits its present circumstances. In Rom 8:24 (M38-F-VCG-05), being "saved in hope" is explicitly named as a present inner-being state that reorients the person toward the unseen — condition is not yet changed but orientation has been fundamentally restructured. In 1Th 5:8 (M38-F-VCG-01), the "helmet of salvation" protects the mind and will, arming the inner being for sobriety and vigilance — the orientation to one's present circumstances is transformed even before the eschatological condition is altered. In 2Pe 3:15 (M38-F-VCG-05), the reinterpretation of the Lord's delay as salvific gift transforms the inner being's reading of its condition without changing the condition itself. The eschatological VCGs (M38-F-VCG-02, VCG-03) point toward a future transformation of condition — Heb 9:28's "final salvation," 2Ti 1:10's "immortality brought to light," 2Pe 1:11's "entrance into the eternal kingdom." The characteristic thus produces present transformation of orientation and anticipates future transformation of condition.
- *T5.1.2* — _Is the transformation produced by this characteristic reversible or irreversible in the verse evidence?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence presents a complex picture with both reversible and irreversible dimensions. The eschatological transformation anticipated is explicitly irreversible: 2Ti 1:10 names the Savior as having "abolished death and brought immortality to light" — a transformation that is by definition permanent and cannot be undone. Isa 51:6 and 51:8's everlasting salvation that outlasts cosmic decay implies irreversibility at the eschatological horizon. However, the present inner-being transformation of orientation is evidenced as requiring active maintenance and susceptible to testing: 1Pe 1:5 notes that believers are guarded "through faith" — implying that the faculty of faith must remain operative and is not automatically permanent. Heb 6:9 (M38-F-VCG-06) expresses pastoral concern for the trajectory of the readers, suggesting that the path toward salvation is not irreversibly secured by present experience alone. The evidence implies: (1) the eschatological salvation itself, when received, is irreversible; (2) the present inner-being posture of waiting-hope is real but requires sustaining; (3) the intermediate period involves genuine tension rather than guaranteed automatic progression.
- *T5.1.3* — _If the evidence is silent on transformation, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence is not silent on transformation; see T5.1.1 (obs_id=348) and T5.1.2 (obs_id=349) for detailed findings. Transformation is evidenced both as present reorientation and as anticipated future change of condition. This prompt is satisfied by existing positive findings.
- *T5.2.1* — _Does the verse evidence describe a sequence of inner states through which this characteristic moves the person — a before, during, and after…_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence implies a sequence of inner states, though no single verse narrates all three stages explicitly. The sequence can be reconstructed from the VCGs taken together. *Before* (the soul in unresolved distress): Isa 59:11 and Isa 26:18 (M38-F-VCG-07) evidence the initial state of groaning, mourning, and failed self-rescue — the soul confronts the absence of salvation and its own incapacity. *During* (active waiting and hope-sustained endurance): Gen 49:18, Isa 33:2, and Heb 9:28 evidence the state of active, volitional waiting — hope oriented toward the unseen (Rom 8:24), inner being armed and sustained (1Th 5:8), affliction endured with salvific purpose (2Cor 1:6). *After* (eschatological arrival and received inheritance): Heb 1:14, 2Pe 1:11, Tit 2:13, and 2Ti 1:10 evidence the state anticipated by the waiting — inheritance received, immortality unveiled, glorious appearing encountered, eternal kingdom entered. The sequence moves from recognized incapacity → sustained expectant waiting → eschatological reception; the characteristic is the inner-being state proper to the middle stage of this sequence.
- *T5.2.2* — _What are those states, and what does the sequence reveal about how this characteristic works?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — Building on T5.2.1 (obs_id=351), the three-state sequence and its implications can be named more precisely. *State 1 — Experienced incapacity and longing*: Isa 26:18 (labor producing nothing), Isa 59:11 (groaning that salvation is far off), Isa 33:2 (distress with no human arm). The inner being is in genuine crisis, not theoretical danger. *State 2 — Sustained expectant hope*: Gen 49:18 (active naming of the LORD), Rom 8:24 (saved in hope, not sight), 1Th 5:8 (armed with eschatological hope), Heb 9:28 (eagerly waiting), Isa 56:1 (conduct aligned with imminent salvation). The inner being is in forward-oriented tension — holding the promise against the not-yet. *State 3 — Anticipated eschatological arrival*: Tit 2:13, 2Pe 1:11, 2Ti 1:10, Isa 51:6, Luk 1:47. What the sequence reveals about how this characteristic works: it does not bypass incapacity but moves through it; it does not collapse hope in the absence of fulfillment; it sustains the inner being precisely in the gap between promise and possession. The characteristic is the faculty that makes State 2 possible — the bridge between recognized need and not-yet-received fullness.
- *T5.2.3* — _If the evidence is silent on sequence, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence is not silent on sequence; see T5.2.1 (obs_id=351) and T5.2.2 (obs_id=352) for the reconstructed three-stage sequence (incapacity → sustained waiting → eschatological arrival) and its interpretive implications. This prompt is satisfied by existing positive findings.
- *T5.3.1* — _What mechanism does this characteristic use to produce change in the person — discipline, encounter, gradual formation, sudden transformatio…_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence reveals a dominant mechanism of sustained reorientation through promise-anchored waiting rather than sudden encounter or formal discipline. In 1Pe 1:5, believers are "guarded through faith" — the mechanism is an ongoing divine preservation that works through the faculty of faith over time, not a single transformative moment. In 1Th 5:8, the "helmet of salvation" as an eschatological hope functions as a protective shaping of the mind — a daily armoring that sustains the inner being across circumstances. In Isa 56:1 (M38-F-VCG-01), the nearness of coming salvation "motivates righteous conduct" — the mechanism is future-oriented hope reshaping present will and behavior gradually. In 2Pe 3:15, the reinterpretation of delay as salvific requires a gradual re-cognition, not a sudden revelation. The mechanism most consistent across the evidence is gradual formation through sustained hope: the inner being is changed by the prolonged discipline of waiting, expecting, and re-reading circumstances in light of promised salvation. The secondary mechanism is encounter with divine revelation — God's self-disclosure in the Savior's appearing (Tit 3:4, 2Ti 1:10) producing immediate recognition and response.
- *T5.3.2* — _Does the evidence distinguish between mechanisms in different contexts?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence does suggest contextual differentiation of mechanism. (1) *In the context of distress and crisis*: the mechanism appears to be the divine assurance of imminence — God's warrior-movement toward rescue (Hab 3:8, Isa 59:17, Isa 62:1, M38-F-VCG-04) provides the stabilizing counter-word to the soul's experience of absence. The mechanism here is divine proclamation breaking into experienced hopelessness. (2) *In the context of ongoing communal life*: the mechanism is gradual formation through hope-shaped conduct — Isa 56:1's righteous conduct motivated by imminent salvation; 1Th 5:8's sobriety and vigilance enabled by the helmet of hope. The mechanism is future-oriented recalibration of present behavior. (3) *In the context of eschatological waiting*: the mechanism is sustained faith-exercise — 1Pe 1:5's guarding "through faith," Heb 9:28's "eagerly waiting" — requiring active orientation rather than passive reception. (4) *In the context of suffering*: the mechanism appears to be reinterpretation — 2Pe 3:15's rereading of delay as salvific; 2Cor 1:6's affliction understood as working toward others' salvation. The mechanisms are distinct and context-sensitive; the evidence does not support a single uniform change-mechanism.
- *T5.3.3* — _If the evidence is silent on mechanism, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence is not silent on mechanism; see T5.3.1 (obs_id=354) and T5.3.2 (obs_id=355) for findings identifying gradual formation through sustained hope and contextually differentiated mechanisms (divine proclamation, hope-shaped conduct, faith-exercise, reinterpretive perception). This prompt is satisfied by existing positive findings.
- *T5.4.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating in relation to suffering or affliction — as a response to it, a product of it, or…_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — Suffering and affliction are deeply interwoven with this characteristic across multiple VCGs. In M38-F-VCG-01, Isa 33:2's supplication arises from acute distress; Isa 59:11's groaning is the inner-being response to experienced suffering of absence. In M38-F-VCG-07, Isa 26:18's failed deliverance names the anguish of disappointed labor — suffering as the context in which the soul recognizes its incapacity. In M38-F-VCG-05, 2Cor 1:6 is the most explicit: Paul's afflictions "work for the salvation and comfort of others" — suffering is not merely the context for salvation-hope but the operative mechanism through which it is communicated to others. Phili 1:19's imprisonment is the circumstantial context in which Paul expects deliverance. The relationship is multi-directional: suffering is both the context that makes salvation-hope urgent (Isa 33:2), the experience that tests and reveals hope (2Cor 1:6), and the occasion through which hope becomes communicable to others. Notably, the evidence does not present suffering as producing hope mechanically; rather, sustained hope through suffering is what is evidenced.
- *T5.4.2* — _Does suffering deepen, test, reveal, or produce this characteristic in the person?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence most clearly supports suffering as the context that tests and reveals this characteristic, with some evidence that it deepens and even produces communicable hope. *Testing*: Isa 59:11 and Isa 26:18 (M38-F-VCG-07) show that the absence of salvation is experienced as genuine anguish — the inner being is tested at the point of deferred hope rather than sheltered from it. Job 13:16 (M38-F-VCG-05) shows that Job's willingness to approach God "despite danger" reveals and tests his integrity-grounded hope. *Deepening*: The prophets' intense search (1Pe 1:10) for a salvation they would not themselves receive suggests that prolonged non-receipt does not diminish but may intensify the longing. *Producing*: 2Cor 1:6 is the strongest evidence for suffering as generative — Paul's afflictions do not merely test his hope but produce salvific benefit in others. *Revelation*: Isa 33:2's crisis reveals the community's utter dependence on God, making explicit what distress might otherwise conceal. The characteristic is not produced by suffering ex nihilo, but suffering strips away alternative sources of hope and reveals/deepens the soul's orientation toward divine salvation as the only remaining anchor.
- *T5.4.3* — _If the evidence is silent on the relationship to suffering, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence is not silent on the relationship to suffering; see T5.4.1 (obs_id=357) and T5.4.2 (obs_id=358) for findings. Suffering and affliction are extensively evidenced as the context, testing ground, and communicative medium of this characteristic. This prompt is satisfied by existing positive findings.
- *T5.5.1* — _Does the verse evidence show this characteristic participating in the longer arc of character formation and sanctification — shaping the per…_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence shows this characteristic contributing to the longer arc of formation, though this is not the primary accent of most verses. The clearest evidence is in Isa 56:1 (M38-F-VCG-01), where the nearness of coming salvation "motivates righteous conduct" — the anticipatory posture shapes present behavior and aligns the will with justice. This is a direct linkage between hope-sustained and ethical formation. In 1Th 5:8, eschatological hope functions as mental armor enabling sobriety and vigilance — these are formation virtues sustained over time. In 2Pe 1:11 (M38-F-VCG-06), the "entrance into the eternal kingdom richly provided" is framed in a context (2Pe 1) that explicitly connects to growth in virtue and knowledge — the eschatological hope functions as the horizon that gives formation its purpose and direction. In 1Pe 1:5 (M38-F-VCG-01), the ongoing guarding "through faith" implies a continued active exercise of trust that deepens over time. The evidence does not present a detailed formation program, but the characteristic's sustained forward-orientation shapes conduct, sobriety, and virtue across the waiting period.
- *T5.5.2* — _What does the evidence reveal about the role of this characteristic in that longer arc?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence reveals that this characteristic functions in the formation arc primarily as an orienting horizon and as a present motivation for conduct. In Isa 56:1 (M38-F-VCG-01), the "nearness of coming salvation" motivates righteous conduct and aligns the will with justice — the eschatological horizon directly shapes ethical formation in the present. In 1Th 5:8, eschatological hope forms the mind toward sobriety and vigilance — virtues that belong to sanctification. In 2Pe 3:15 (M38-F-VCG-05), the reinterpretation of delay as salvific patience requires a formation of perception — the person must become one who can read circumstances theologically rather than merely empirically. In Heb 9:28's "eagerly waiting," the character quality formed is active, desirous expectancy rather than passivity. The role of this characteristic in the formation arc is thus: (1) to provide the eschatological horizon that gives formation its purpose; (2) to motivate present righteous conduct through anticipated completion; (3) to form the mind toward endurance, sobriety, and reinterpretive perception through the practice of hope. The characteristic does not stand apart from formation but is embedded within it as both motivation and content.
- *T5.5.3* — _If the evidence is silent on formation and sanctification, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence is not silent on formation and sanctification; see T5.5.1 (obs_id=360) and T5.5.2 (obs_id=361) for findings. The characteristic's role in motivating righteous conduct, forming the mind toward sobriety, and providing the eschatological horizon for formation is evidenced. This prompt is satisfied by existing positive findings.
- *T5.6.1* — _Does the verse evidence point this characteristic toward an eschatological fullness — a future state toward which its present operation is o…_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — Eschatological trajectory is the constitutive structure of this characteristic; the evidence is pervasive and strong. The entire characteristic is defined by forward-orientation toward not-yet-received salvation. M38-F-VCG-03 is the most concentrated evidence: Phili 3:20 awaits a Savior returning from heaven; Tit 2:13 holds the inner being in posture of expectant waiting for the "glorious appearing of the great God and Savior"; Heb 9:28 explicitly names Christ's second appearing as "bringing final salvation to those eagerly waiting." M38-F-VCG-02 adds cosmic scope: Isa 51:6's salvation outlasts a perishing cosmos; Isa 51:8's salvation endures "to all generations." M38-F-VCG-01's 1Pe 1:5 names a "salvation yet to be revealed" as the telos guarding the present inner being. Rom 8:24's "saved in hope" (M38-F-VCG-05) names the present posture as constitutively forward-looking — "hope that is seen is not hope." The present experience of this characteristic is in every case partial, anticipatory, and held toward a fullness that exceeds present experience; the eschatological horizon is not peripheral but structural to the characteristic itself.
- *T5.6.2* — _What does the present experience of this characteristic anticipate about its future fullness?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The present experience of salvation-anticipated contains within itself the specific content of what is anticipated, making the present state the structural precondition of future fullness. The evidence specifies several dimensions of anticipated fullness. (1) *Personal encounter with the Savior*: Tit 2:13 names "the glorious appearing of the great God and Savior" as the hoped-for object — the present posture of waiting anticipates face-to-face encounter with the person of Christ. (2) *Final abolition of death*: 2Ti 1:10's "immortality brought to light" through the Savior's appearing anticipates a condition in which mortality's grip is permanently dissolved. (3) *Entrance into the eternal kingdom*: 2Pe 1:11 anticipates "richly provided entrance" — the present experience of waiting at the threshold anticipates lavish eschatological welcome. (4) *Universal disclosure*: Luk 3:6 and Isa 52:10 anticipate a salvation visible to "all flesh" and "all nations" — the present experience within a particular community anticipates cosmic universal revelation. (5) *Everlasting security*: Isa 51:6's "everlasting salvation" and Isa 26:1's salvation-as-walls anticipate an encompassing security that replaces all present vulnerabilities. The present experience is partial, interior, and hope-sustained; the anticipated fullness is public, embodied, universal, and permanent.
- *T5.6.3* — _If the evidence is silent on eschatological trajectory, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence is not silent on eschatological trajectory; it is the dominant structural feature of this characteristic. See T5.6.1 (obs_id=363) and T5.6.2 (obs_id=364) for extensive findings. Eschatological trajectory is constitutive of Characteristic 6, not peripheral to it. This prompt is satisfied by existing positive findings.
- *T5.7.2* — _Does the evidence indicate generational consequence — a deposit carried forward beyond the individual?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence does indicate generational consequence, though not in the form of a formal constitutional deposit. Isa 51:8 (M38-F-VCG-02) explicitly states that God's salvation endures "to all generations" — salvation-hope is not exhausted in a single generation but is carried forward as an inheritance. In 1Pe 1:10 (M38-F-VCG-01), the prophets searched for a salvation intended for later generations — their sustained longing and searching produced a forward-reaching deposit of anticipated hope that benefited those who came after them. In Luk 1:69 (M38-F-VCG-06), the raising of "a horn of salvation in David's house" names the fulfillment of promises made to Abraham and David — salvation-hope has been carried across many generations as covenantal memory and anticipation. The generational consequence is the transmission of expectant hope as a community heritage: each generation inherits the longing, the promise, and the prophetic deposit of those who waited before them, and contributes to the chain of hope extended toward those who will follow. The deposit is thus covenantally generational rather than biologically genetic.
<!-- /EVIDENCE -->

<!-- EVIDENCE: Ch5 — T1 findings for Salvation anticipated and hope sustained -->

**T1 — Definition**

- *T1.1.1* — _What is this characteristic called in the programme — and what does the name itself signal about its essential nature?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The characteristic is called "Salvation anticipated and hope sustained." The name has two structural components held in tension. "Salvation anticipated" signals that the object is specific and concrete — not a generic hopefulness but hope directed at a particular divine act (salvation/yeshuah/soteria). The word "anticipated" carries temporal weight: what is anticipated is real and incoming but not yet received; anticipation is oriented toward a future that is already determined. "Hope sustained" signals the inner-being posture required while the anticipation is unfulfilled — the self must actively maintain its orientation against the attrition of delay, suffering, and apparent absence. "Sustained" is significant: it implies duration, effort, and stability under pressure. Together the name signals that this characteristic is not a momentary emotional state but a durable inner posture requiring active maintenance of orientation toward an object (salvation) whose arrival is certain but whose timing creates the necessity for sustained hope. The name also implicitly distinguishes this characteristic from M38-A (salvation already received): the defining feature is precisely the not-yet structure of anticipation rather than the already-received structure of possession.
- *T1.1.2* — _What do the primary Hebrew and Greek terms reveal at the definitional level — before deeper lexical analysis begins?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The primary Hebrew term is **H3444 ye.shu.ah** (salvation, deliverance, rescue — from yasha', to save, deliver, give width/freedom to) and the primary Greek terms are **G4991 soteria** (salvation, deliverance, preservation), **G4990 sōtēr** (Savior, deliverer), and **G4982 sōzō** (to save, rescue, make whole). At the definitional level, before lexical depth, these terms reveal the following:

Ye.shu.ah names a concrete act of rescue — movement from constraint to spaciousness, from peril to safety. It is inherently transitive and relational: someone saves someone from something. The term used as the object of hope (Gen 49:18: "I wait for your ye.shu.ah") reveals that what the soul waits for is not an abstract state but a specific divine act of release.

Soteria in Greek similarly names a concrete rescue or preservation from threat — it carries the connotation of wholeness restored. G4990 sōtēr names the agent of that rescue as a personal title, concentrating the hope in a Person. G4982 sōzō as a verb (Rom 8:24) names the same rescue as a process. Taken together, at the pre-analytical level, these terms reveal that the characteristic is not hope-in-general but hope structured around a specific relational rescue — salvation by a named Savior from a real peril. The inner being is not oriented toward wellness or flourishing abstractly, but toward the act of a Person who rescues.
- *T1.1.3* — _Does the name carry directional, relational, or constitutional implications that orient the enquiry from the outset?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The name "Salvation anticipated and hope sustained" carries implications in all three categories.

**Directional**: "Anticipated" is inherently forward-directed — it orients the enquiry toward a future not yet received. This distinguishes the characteristic from M38-A (salvation received) and signals that the entire analysis must remain attentive to the not-yet structure. The name alerts the enquiry to avoid importing present-possession language into the evidence.

**Relational**: "Salvation" as the object of both anticipation and hope is not an abstract state but a divine act performed by a Savior (sōtēr). The name implies a two-party relationship: the one who waits and the One who will save. This relational implication means the characteristic cannot be analyzed as a purely internal psychological state — it is constitutively oriented toward a personal divine agent. Tit 2:13 (VCG-03) and Phili 3:20 confirm that the object is the Savior as Person, not salvation as abstraction.

**Constitutional**: "Hope sustained" implies something about the person's constitutional capacity — the inner being must have a faculty capable of maintaining orientation under the attrition of delay and suffering. This points the enquiry toward the question of which constitutional element (spirit, soul, mind, will) is the seat of this characteristic. The noun "hope" combined with the verb "sustained" implies both a faculty and an act of maintaining it — constitutional and volitional together.
- *T1.2.1* — _What kind of inner-being phenomenon does this characteristic appear to be — an act, a disposition, a condition, a quality, or something else…_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence supports classifying this characteristic as a compound inner-being phenomenon combining dispositional and conditional elements, with recurring volitional acts as its expression. The dispositional element is primary: across VCGs 01–06, the soul is consistently characterized by a settled orientation toward salvation as its object — this is not a series of punctual decisions but a stable stance of the inner being (see the "helmet" imagery of 1Th 5:8, the "citizenship in heaven" of Phili 3:20, the "guarded through faith" of 1Pe 1:5). The conditional element is also present: the soul exists in a specific condition of not-yet-received salvation, which is not chosen but given by the creature's temporal location between promise and fulfillment (Rom 8:24; Heb 9:28). Volitional acts emerge from this disposition-in-condition: Gen 49:18 ("I wait"), Isa 33:2 (the community pleads), Hab 3:8 (confidence in divine warrior action). The VCG-07 evidence (Isa 26:18) introduces a condition-of-failure state that the characteristic by contrast excludes. The most accurate working classification is: a sustained dispositional orientation (hope-directed-toward-salvation) that exists as a condition of creaturely temporality and expresses itself in recurring volitional acts of waiting, trust, and anticipation.
- *T1.2.2* — _Is this characteristic simple in structure or does it appear to have constituent elements at first encounter?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — At first encounter the characteristic appears structurally compound, with at least three identifiable constituent elements that the name already implies.

**Element 1 — Anticipation**: The inner-being act of directed, forward-oriented expectation. This is cognitive and intentional — the mind and will are directed toward something not yet received. It presupposes knowledge of what is anticipated (salvation, the Savior) and confidence in its coming.

**Element 2 — Hope**: The affective-volitional faculty of sustained trust toward the anticipated object. Hope is more than anticipation — it carries the element of personal trust in the one who has promised. 1Pe 1:5 (VCG-01) names faith as the faculty through which the believer is guarded; 1Ti 4:10 (VCG-03) identifies hope set on the living God as Savior.

**Element 3 — Sustaining/endurance**: The temporal dimension — this characteristic is not a momentary orientation but one that must be maintained under delay, suffering, and apparent absence (Rom 8:24; 2Pe 3:15; 2Cor 1:6). The "sustained" element is not automatic but requires something to maintain it.

These three elements are distinguishable but not separable — they co-constitute the characteristic. At first encounter the structure is not simple.
- *T1.2.3* — _What is the current best working description of this characteristic — encapsulating its constitutional location, the faculties it primarily …_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — Current best working description:

Salvation-anticipated-and-hope-sustained is a **compound inner-being orientation** located primarily in the volitional-affective dimension of the person, in which the will (as active faculty of direction) and the affect (as the felt coloring of that direction) are jointly engaged in maintaining a forward-oriented trust toward the divine act of salvation and the person of the Savior, in the condition of temporal not-yet. The characteristic is constitutively relational — it cannot exist without a personal divine object — and constitutively temporal — it exists precisely in the gap between promise and fulfillment. It engages the mind as the faculty that comprehends the promise (1Pe 1:10; 1Th 5:8), the will as the faculty that actively directs and maintains the orientation (Gen 49:18; Heb 9:28), and the affect as the faculty that registers the weight of waiting (Isa 59:11; Luk 1:47). Its impact on the person is triple: it produces stability against despair (Isa 51:6; 51:8), it motivates moral alignment in the present (Isa 56:1), and it shapes identity as one whose deepest belonging is eschatological rather than circumstantial (Phili 3:20; Heb 1:14). The characteristic is not a momentary state but a durable dispositional posture requiring maintenance and expressing itself in recurring volitional acts.
- *T1.3.1* — _What is the structural opposite of this characteristic — the inner-being reality that stands against or excludes it?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence from M38-F-VCG-07 and the negative cases within other VCGs points to two structural opposites, which may be understood as the two failure modes of this characteristic. The first is self-reliant striving toward self-achieved deliverance: Isa 26:18 (VCG-07) shows the community laboring at deliverance and producing nothing — wind, not salvation. This is hope directed toward the wrong object (human capacity) rather than sustained toward divine salvation. The second is despair-under-deferred-hope: Isa 59:11 (VCG-01) shows the inner being groaning with salvation far off, with no divine anchor to sustain the waiting. This is the collapse of sustained orientation into hopeless mourning. These two failure modes together define the boundary: on one side, the soul that turns from waiting on God toward self-rescue; on the other, the soul that loses orientation entirely and sinks into despair. Salvation-anticipated-and-hope-sustained stands between these — it is the posture that neither grasps at self-rescue nor collapses into despair, but actively maintains orientation toward the God who will accomplish what the creature cannot (Isa 62:1; Hab 3:8).
- *T1.3.2* — _What does this characteristic explicitly exclude or resist?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence supports identifying several things this characteristic explicitly excludes or resists.

**It resists despair**: The sustained dimension of the characteristic is precisely the maintenance of orientation against the pull of despair that arises from deferred hope (Isa 59:11, VCG-01; Isa 26:18, VCG-07). The characteristic is constitutively anti-despair — to sustain hope is to refuse the inner collapse that the absence of received salvation would otherwise produce.

**It resists self-reliance as substitute**: Isa 26:18 (VCG-07) explicitly excludes the posture of self-achieved deliverance — labor that produces wind rather than salvation. The characteristic resists any inner turning away from God toward human capacity as the source of rescue.

**It resists the confusion of salvation-not-yet with salvation-never**: 2Pe 3:15 (VCG-05) — the characteristic reframes delay as mercy, resisting the interpretive move that reads God's patience as absence or failure.

**It resists premature closure**: The characteristic is structurally incompatible with a soul that has declared itself already arrived — the eagerly waiting posture of Heb 9:28 and Phili 3:20 excludes a settled presumption that the eschatological fullness is already possessed.

**It resists fear of death and cosmic dissolution**: 2Ti 1:10 (VCG-03) — the Savior's appearing abolished death and brought immortality to light; the characteristic resists existential terror by orienting the soul toward the One who has already undone mortality's finality.
- *T1.3.3* — _What is this characteristic not — where does it end and something else begin?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — Drawing on the full evidence, this characteristic is not the following:

**It is not salvation already received** (M38-A): The defining boundary on this side is explicit in the characteristic's own definition — this is hope sustained as the inner-being content distinct from salvation-already-received. Where Rom 8:24 (VCG-05) says "saved in hope," the emphasis falls on hope as the mode of present salvation, not on its completed possession.

**It is not generic optimism or positive affect**: The characteristic is specifically directed toward the divine act of salvation and the Savior as its object — it ends where hope becomes undirected positive affect or human-capacity confidence. The direction toward God is constitutive; remove it and what remains is not this characteristic.

**It is not eschatological certainty without present experience**: The characteristic maintains the tension of not-yet; where that tension resolves into simple assurance of already-possessed fullness, the characteristic ends and transitions into something closer to realized eschatology or assurance (which has its own registration in M38-A or M18).

**It is not lament per se**: Isa 59:11 shows that mourning-that-salvation-is-far-off is part of the characteristic's territory, but the characteristic ends where lament loses its object and becomes despair-without-orientation. Lament within hope-sustained is bounded by the characteristic; despair that has lost the divine object marks where the characteristic ends.

**It is not the same as M18 Hope** in its generic eschatological register: the cross-reference in the characteristic definition acknowledges proximity to M18, but M38-F is specifically salvation-focused — it is hope whose content is the specific act of divine rescue, not the broader eschatological horizon.
- *T1.4.1* — _In what distinct ways does this characteristic operate within the inner person?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence supports at least five distinct modes of operation, distributed across the VCGs.

1. **Vocational declaration/naming of God**: Gen 49:18 — "I wait for your salvation, O LORD" — the soul explicitly names God as the object of its hope, constituting the characteristic as an act of speech-identification. The inner being orients itself by naming its object.

2. **Protective arming**: 1Th 5:8 (VCG-01) — the hope of salvation functions as a helmet, protecting the mind and will from the attrition of circumstances. The characteristic operates as cognitive and volitional armor.

3. **Motivational alignment**: Isa 56:1 (VCG-01) — the nearness of coming salvation motivates righteous conduct. The characteristic operates as a moral motivator, aligning the will with justice in anticipation of fulfillment.

4. **Communal pleading**: Isa 33:2 (VCG-01) — the community's corporate prayer of desperate reliance on God as their only arm. The characteristic operates in the communal register as shared petition.

5. **Interpretive reframing**: 2Pe 3:15 (VCG-05) — delay is reinterpreted as divine mercy, not absence. The characteristic operates as a hermeneutic through which the inner being interprets its circumstances in the light of salvific promise rather than surface appearances.
- *T1.4.2* — _Does this characteristic operate differently depending on context, direction, or constitutional level?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — Yes, the evidence supports contextual variation in mode across at least three axes.

**Individual vs. communal**: VCG-01 shows both individual operation (Gen 49:18 — "I wait") and communal operation (Isa 33:2 — the community pleads together; Isa 59:11 — the community mourns). The characteristic operates as a personal inner posture and as a shared communal disposition; the communal form appears to have its own distinct dynamic of corporate solidarity sustaining individual hope.

**Present rescue vs. eschatological completion**: VCG-05 shows the characteristic operating in present-tense mode — Phili 1:19 (expected deliverance from prison through prayer and the Spirit), Job 13:16 (confidence before God as present salvation), 2Pe 3:15 (the present time of delay as salvation-gift). VCG-03 shows it operating in fully eschatological mode — Tit 2:13, Heb 9:28, Phili 3:20. The characteristic has a near-horizon mode (present crisis) and a far-horizon mode (parousia/eternal kingdom).

**Cognitive/volitional vs. affective**: At the mind/will level — 1Th 5:8 (the helmet protecting the mind's sobriety) and Isa 56:1 (conduct motivated). At the affective/deeper level — Luk 1:47 (spirit exulting), Isa 59:11 (soul mourning), Isa 59:17 (divine warrior confidence transferred to the waiting). The characteristic engages different constitutional strata.
- *T1.4.3* — _Does this characteristic have a communicative or speech-based mode of operation — and if so, how does it function?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — Yes, the evidence supports a communicative/speech-based mode of operation, operating in multiple speech-forms.

**Declaration to God (address)**: Gen 49:18 (VCG-01) — "I wait for your salvation, O LORD" — this is direct address to God naming him as the object of waiting and the source of hoped-for rescue. The speech act constitutes and expresses the characteristic simultaneously: to say "I wait for your salvation" is to perform the waiting as a speech act before God.

**Corporate petition**: Isa 33:2 (VCG-01) — the community pleads with God as the only arm. The characteristic operates communally through the speech-act of corporate prayer, where shared petition both expresses and sustains the shared posture of hope.

**Prophetic proclamation**: Isa 62:1 (VCG-04) — God's declaration that he will not rest until salvation blazes forth; Isa 56:1 (VCG-01) — the announcement of near salvation motivating righteous conduct. These show the characteristic sustained in the community through proclamation received by faith — the hearer's inner orientation is activated and maintained by the proclaimed word of salvation.

**Lament-speech**: Isa 59:11 (VCG-01) — the community's expression of mourning that salvation is far off. This is speech-toward-God that keeps the inner orientation in play even in the absence of received rescue — lament as a form of sustained orientation.

The communicative mode is not peripheral but constitutive: this characteristic is sustained in and through speech acts before God and with the community.
- *T1.5.1* — _What is the first or most immediate inner-being response to receiving or encountering this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence points to two distinct immediate responses depending on whether the characteristic is encountered as divine declaration or as creaturely reception. When the soul encounters divine declaration of imminent/coming salvation (Isa 56:1, VCG-01; Isa 62:1, VCG-04; Isa 49:8, VCG-04), the immediate inner response appears to be an alignment of the will — conduct and orientation are recalibrated toward righteousness in light of the announced nearness. When the characteristic is encountered as a grace given to the believer (1Pe 1:5, VCG-01; Tit 2:13, VCG-03; Luk 1:47, VCG-06), the most evidenced immediate response is joy and exultation: Mary's spirit exults in God as Savior (Luk 1:47), and the prophetic-communal utterance of Isa 25:9 (implied by characteristic definition) expresses glad recognition. Within the corpus of VCG-03, the immediate response to grasping the Savior's identity and coming is explicitly anticipatory orientation — the inner being turns toward the horizon of the Savior's appearing (Tit 2:13; Phili 3:20). The most immediate single response across the breadth of evidence is re-orientation of the inner being's forward gaze toward its proper object — salvation and the Savior — displacing whatever prior distraction or despair occupied that faculty.
- *T1.5.2* — _Is the immediate response consistent across the verse evidence, or does it vary?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The immediate response varies significantly across the verse evidence and cannot be reduced to a single consistent response. The variation appears to track with the relationship between the encountering soul and the salvation received or promised.

**Joy/exultation** (Luk 1:47, VCG-06): when salvation is named as personally received, the immediate response is spirit-exultation.

**Moral re-alignment** (Isa 56:1, VCG-01): when salvation's nearness is proclaimed, the immediate inner response is motivational — the will moves toward righteousness.

**Mourning/groaning** (Isa 59:11, VCG-01; Rom 8:24, VCG-05): when salvation is present but unfulfilled, the immediate response is a groaning or sighing that coexists with hope rather than replacing it.

**Sobriety/vigilance** (1Th 5:8, VCG-01): when salvation is understood as coming eschatological protection, the immediate response is the arming of the mind for clear-eyed alertness.

**Intense searching** (1Pe 1:10, VCG-01): when salvation is anticipated but not yet understood, the immediate response is cognitive engagement — prophets searching and inquiring carefully.

The variation is not arbitrary but structured: the immediate response tracks the person's position relative to salvation (whether receiving, anticipating, suffering its absence, or arming for its completion). The evidence does not permit a single unified immediate response.
- *T1.5.3* — _Where the verse evidence is silent on immediate response, note this explicitly._
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The verse evidence is relatively thin on immediate response for several specific instances. The following are noted:

**VCG-02** (Isa 26:1; Isa 49:6; Isa 51:6; Isa 51:8; Isa 52:10; Isa 60:18; Luk 3:6): These verses frame salvation as eschatological security and universal scope. None of the Pass A meanings for this VCG name an immediate inner-being response — they characterize the content of salvation and the settled confidence it produces, but do not evidence the first-moment response of the encountering soul. The immediate response for this VCG is silent in the corpus.

**VCG-04** (Isa 49:8; Isa 59:17; Isa 62:1; Hab 3:8): These verses emphasize God's militant saving movement. The Pass A meanings characterize God's action and the assurance it produces, but the immediate inner response of the human soul encountering these declarations is not evidenced at the immediate-response level — the evidence names the sustained state (confidence, assurance) rather than the first-moment response.

**VCG-07** (Isa 26:18): The immediate response to failed self-rescue is implied as despair/anguish, but the verse evidence does not give explicit inner-being vocabulary for the first-moment response. This is a partial gap.

Where explicit immediate-response evidence is available it has been reported in T1.5.1 and T1.5.2.
- *T1.6.1* — _What does this characteristic produce in the inner being over time?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence across the VCGs supports a cluster of sustained inner-being effects.

**Stability under pressure**: M38-F-VCG-02 (Isa 51:6; 51:8) — the soul anchors eternal hope in salvation that outlasts cosmos and enemies; this produces a settled, unshakeable security over time that external decay cannot erode. Isa 26:1 figures this as the city whose very walls are salvation — comprehensive stability.

**Moral formation**: Isa 56:1 (VCG-01) — imminent salvation motivating righteous conduct; sustained exposure to the horizon of coming salvation consistently aligns the will toward justice and righteousness. 1Th 5:8 — the helmet of hope produces sobriety and vigilance as ongoing states.

**Perseverance in suffering**: 2Cor 1:6 (VCG-05) — the soul endures afflictions patiently, learning that suffering produces salvation in others; 1Pe 1:5 — the inner being is guarded through faith, sustaining perseverance in the face of threat.

**Identity transformation**: Isa 60:18 (VCG-02) — the community's identity is wholly redefined by encompassing salvation; Heb 1:14 (VCG-06) — being an heir of salvation shapes the person's dignity and standing. Over time, the characteristic produces a self whose identity is constituted by what it awaits.

**Interpretive wisdom**: 2Pe 3:15 (VCG-05) — the characteristic over time produces the capacity to interpret delay, suffering, and apparent absence as themselves expressions of divine mercy rather than evidence of divine failure.
- *T1.6.2* — _What states, qualities, capacities, or orientations does sustained exposure to or possession of this characteristic establish?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence supports the following sustained states, qualities, capacities, and orientations produced by this characteristic over time.

**States established**: Settled security (Isa 26:1; Isa 51:6; Isa 51:8, VCG-02 — the soul inhabits a salvation that outlasts cosmos and enemies); Assured identity as heir (Heb 1:14; Heb 6:9, VCG-06 — "things belonging to salvation"); Active sobriety (1Th 5:8, VCG-01).

**Qualities established**: Endurance under affliction (2Cor 1:6, VCG-05); Integrity before God (Job 13:16, VCG-05); Patience interpreting delay as mercy (2Pe 3:15, VCG-05); Righteous conduct (Isa 56:1, VCG-01).

**Capacities established**: Hermeneutic capacity — the ability to read delay, suffering, and absence through a salvation-hope frame (2Pe 3:15); Communal sustaining capacity — the ability to sustain others' salvation through one's own endurance (2Cor 1:6); Prophetic-anticipatory capacity — the trained attention to salvation's coming character (1Pe 1:10, VCG-01).

**Orientations established**: Eschatological citizenship — Phili 3:20 (VCG-03), the inner being's primary orientation is no longer present earthly existence but the returning Savior; Universal-scope concern — the soul's horizon expands to include all peoples in the saving purpose (Isa 49:6; Isa 52:10; Luk 3:6, VCG-02).
- *T1.6.3* — _Does the sustained effect differ from the immediate response — and if so, how?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — Yes, the sustained effects evidenced across the VCGs differ substantially from the immediate responses, and the difference is instructive about the characteristic's developmental arc.

The immediate responses are often acute and affectively prominent: joy/exultation (Luk 1:47), mourning (Isa 59:11), moral re-alignment impulse (Isa 56:1), sobriety-arming (1Th 5:8), intense searching (1Pe 1:10). These are responsive, often emotionally inflected, and tied to the moment of encounter with salvation's announcement or absence.

The sustained effects are more stable, dispositional, and constitutionally deep: settled security against cosmic dissolution (Isa 51:6; 51:8); identity-formation as heir of salvation (Heb 1:14; Heb 6:9); hermeneutic reframing capacity for delay and suffering (2Pe 3:15; 2Cor 1:6); eschatological citizenship as primary identity-frame (Phili 3:20). These are not acute responses but stable inner-being shapes formed by long inhabitation of the characteristic.

The arc is therefore: acute affective-volitional response → deepening stability → identity-transformation. The characteristic does not produce a single sustained response but progressively reshapes the constitutional level of the person from the inside, moving from reactive orientation to settled identity. The immediate and sustained effects share the same directional content (salvation/Savior as object) but differ in depth, stability, and constitutional level.
- *T1.7.1* — _What inner conditions or orientations enable genuine reception of this characteristic?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence supports several enabling inner conditions.

**Acknowledged human incapacity**: Isa 26:18 (VCG-07) is the negative case — the soul that trusts its own labor at deliverance cannot receive hope sustained toward divine salvation; genuine reception requires the inner acknowledgment that self-rescue is impossible. Job 13:16 (VCG-05) presents the positive form: Job's willingness to approach God despite danger, resting on integrity before God rather than on strategy or strength, enables hope.

**Active trust/faith**: 1Pe 1:5 (VCG-01) — believers are "guarded through faith" for a salvation yet to be revealed; faith is the named enabling faculty. The characteristic is received by faith and maintained through it.

**Communal/covenantal identity**: Isa 33:2 (VCG-01) — the community pleads together as those whose only arm is God; the characteristic appears to be received within a relational-covenantal framework, not as solitary self-generated optimism.

**Honest mourning of absence**: Isa 59:11 (VCG-01) — the groaning of unfulfilled hope, while painful, is a form of orientation that keeps the inner being's gaze on the right object. This suggests that honest lament is an enabling condition rather than a disqualifying one.

**Receptive, non-grasping posture**: The eschatological evidence of VCG-03 (Tit 2:13; Phili 3:20; Heb 9:28) shows that reception requires an inner posture of waiting — the soul that grasps or already considers itself fully arrived cannot sustain the posture of anticipation.
- *T1.7.2* — _What inner conditions block, distort, or prevent reception?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence supports identifying several blocking or distorting inner conditions.

**Self-reliant striving** (Isa 26:18, VCG-07): The inner posture of trusting in human effort to achieve rescue blocks the reception of hope-sustained-toward-divine-salvation. The soul that labors at its own deliverance cannot simultaneously wait in expectant trust for God's. This is the primary blocking condition identified in the corpus.

**Despair-collapse under deferred hope** (Isa 59:11, VCG-01): When the inner being loses its orientation and groans without divine anchor, the sustaining element of the characteristic is not operational. This is not identical to lament (which can coexist with hope) but is the point where lament has lost its object and hope has dissolved into mourning-for-nothing.

**Present-tense satiation/presumption**: The structure of VCG-03 (Tit 2:13; Phili 3:20; Heb 9:28) implies that a soul that considers itself to have arrived at eschatological fullness already — not awaiting the Savior's return — cannot possess the characteristic of sustained hope. Premature closure is a distortion of the characteristic.

**Fear of death operating without displacement** (2Ti 1:10, VCG-03): Before the Savior's appearing abolished death, fear of mortality functions as a baseline condition that would resist the characteristic unless displaced by the gospel revelation of immortality brought to light.
- *T1.7.3* — _What is the inner-being state of the person who encounters this characteristic but does not receive it?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The evidence in M38-F-VCG-07 and the negative cases in other VCGs offers a composite picture of the non-receiving soul.

**The self-reliant laborer** (Isa 26:18, VCG-07): The person who encounters the promise of salvation but turns instead to human effort produces "nothing" — the inner state is futility, with the characteristic of hopeful orientation toward God absent and replaced by exhausting, outcome-less striving. The soul is not neutral but actively misdirected.

**The despairing mourner** (Isa 59:11, VCG-01): The person who acknowledges salvation's absence but cannot sustain orientation toward God as deliverer sinks into a state of groaning-without-telos — mourning that has lost its anchor. The inner state is anguished directionlessness, neither hopeful nor simply neutral.

**The person without the Savior-object** (implied by VCG-03 structure): A person who encounters the eschatological declaration of Tit 2:13 or Heb 9:28 but does not direct hope toward the Savior personally remains in the state of unanchored temporal existence — living as if earthly citizenship is ultimate (the counter-position to Phili 3:20's eschatological citizenship).

**The despairing non-receiver** is the convergent state: characterized by either misdirected effort or collapsed mourning, the non-receiving person is not at rest but in a state of failed orientation — the soul's capacity for forward-directed hope is present but aimed at the wrong object or has lost its object altogether.
- *T1.8.1* — _What is the primary inner-being dimension of this characteristic from the programme's dimension vocabulary?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — The primary inner-being dimension of this characteristic is the **volitional-affective** dimension, with the volitional element dominant. The characteristic is fundamentally an act and posture of the will orienting itself toward salvation — "I wait" (Gen 49:18) is volitional; "eagerly waiting" (Heb 9:28) is volitional; the community "pleads" (Isa 33:2) is volitional. The helmet imagery of 1Th 5:8 locates the characteristic as protecting the mind/will — it is the will armored by hope. However, the affective dimension is inseparable: mourning that salvation is far off (Isa 59:11), exulting in God as Savior (Luk 1:47), groaning in hope (Rom 8:24) all indicate that the characteristic is not merely cognitive or purely volitional but carries deep affective coloring. The volitional element is primary because the characteristic is consistently described as something the soul does (waits, pleads, searches, eagerly anticipates) rather than something that simply happens to it. The affective element accompanies and suffuses the volitional act but does not replace it as primary.
- *T1.8.2* — _What evidence from the verse evidence supports this classification?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — Supporting evidence for the primary classification of volitional-affective dimension is distributed across all seven VCGs.

**Volitional evidence**: Gen 49:18 (VCG-01) — "I wait for your salvation" is the soul's named volitional act; Heb 9:28 (VCG-03) — "eagerly waiting" is an active volitional posture; Isa 33:2 (VCG-01) — the community's corporate "waiting trust" expressed in petition; 1Ti 4:10 (VCG-03) — hope "set on" the living God is a volitional directing of the inner being; Isa 56:1 (VCG-01) — coming salvation motivating righteous conduct shows the will engaged.

**Affective evidence**: Luk 1:47 (VCG-06) — spirit exulting in the Savior; Isa 59:11 (VCG-01) — soul mourning salvation's absence; Rom 8:24 (VCG-05) — waiting in hope with groaning; 2Ti 1:10 (VCG-03) — mortality's fear displaced by unveiled life, engaging the affective register of dread and relief.

**Cognitive evidence** (supporting secondary dimension): 1Pe 1:10 (VCG-01) — prophets searching and inquiring carefully, engaging the mind; 1Th 5:8 (VCG-01) — helmet protecting the mind for sobriety. The mind is engaged but appears as the instrument through which the will directs itself, rather than the primary seat of the characteristic.

The volitional-affective classification is supported by the preponderance of active-waiting and feeling-laden vocabulary, with volitional acts of orienting, waiting, and pleading primary.
- *T1.8.3* — _Does this characteristic carry secondary dimensions — and if so, what are they, and do they compete with the primary classification?_
  → **[CHAR-6]** E — Yes, the evidence supports two significant secondary dimensions.

**Secondary dimension 1 — Cognitive/noetic**: The evidence of 1Pe 1:10 (VCG-01 — prophets searching and inquiring carefully), 1Th 5:8 (VCG-01 — helmet protecting the mind for sobriety), and 2Pe 3:15 (VCG-05 — reinterpreting delay as mercy) all show a cognitive engagement at the level of understanding and reasoning that is intrinsic to the characteristic rather than merely incidental. The soul's mind must comprehend the promise, interpret circumstances through the salvation-hope frame, and scrutinize the content of salvation for it to function. This cognitive dimension is real but appears to serve the volitional-affective primary dimension rather than competing with it: the mind that comprehends the promise thereby enables the will to direct itself toward it.

**Secondary dimension 2 — Social/communal**: The evidence of Isa 33:2 (VCG-01 — corporate petition), Isa 59:11 (VCG-01 — communal mourning), 2Cor 1:6 (VCG-05 — one person's affliction working salvation in another), Luk 1:69 (VCG-06 — covenantal community's hope), and Luk 3:6 (VCG-02 — all flesh seeing salvation) show that the characteristic has a constitutively communal dimension. It does not operate only as an individual inner state but is sustained and expressed within covenantal community. This secondary dimension does not compete with the primary volitional-affective classification because it names the social location of the characteristic, not its constitutional seat.

The two secondary dimensions (cognitive, communal) are genuine and should not be collapsed into the primary, but they are not competing — they are enabling and constituting conditions of the primary volitional-affective orientation.
